Browse to `http://localhost:8080`. You’ll see the screen in the following figure—there’s already a job configured called `diamol` that is in the failed state. Click the Log In link at the top right, and log in with username `diamol` and password `diamol`.

##### Running Jenkins in a container—it’s fully configured with a user and CI job already set up.

The Jenkins job failed because it’s configured to fetch code from the Gogs Git server, and there’s no code in there yet. The source code for this book is already a Git repository that you originally cloned from GitHub. You can add your local Gogs container as another Git server for the repository and then push the book’s code to your own infrastructure.

##### Try it now

You can add an extra Git server using `git` `remote` `add` and then push to the remote. This uploads the code from your local machine to the Gogs server, which just happens to be a container on your machine too:
    
    
    git remote add local http:/_._ /localhost:3000/diamol/diamol.git
    git push local 2e
    # Gogs will ask you to login -
    # use the diamol username and password you registered in Gogs

Now you have the source code for the whole book in your local Git server. The Jenkins job is configured to look for changes to the code every minute, and if there are changes, it will trigger the CI pipeline. The first job run failed because the code repository didn’t exist, so Jenkins has put the schedule on hold. You’ll need to manually run the job now to start the schedule working again.

##### Try it now

Browse to `http://localhost:8080/job/diamol`. In the screen shown in the following figure, click Build Now in the left-hand menu to run the job. If you don’t see the Build Now option, make sure you’ve logged in to Jenkins with the `diamol` credentials.

##### The Jenkins job page shows the current status of the job and lets you manually start a build.

After a minute or so, the build will complete successfully, the web page will refresh, and you’ll see the output shown in the following figure.

##### The job page in Jenkins shows the status of the most recent builds and the pipeline steps.

Every part of this pipeline ran using Docker containers, taking advantage of a neat trick: containers running in Docker can connect to the Docker API and start new containers on the same Docker Engine they’re running on. The Jenkins image has the Docker CLI installed, and the configuration in the Compose file sets up Jenkins so when it runs Docker commands, they get sent to the Docker Engine on your machine. It sounds odd, but it’s really just taking advantage of the fact that the Docker CLI calls into the Docker API, so CLIs from different places can connect to the same Docker Engine. Figure 11.3 shows how that works.

##### Figure 11.3 Running containers with a volume to bind the private channel for the Docker API

The Docker CLI connects to the local Docker API by default, using a communication channel that is private to your machine—a socket on Linux or a named pipe on Windows. That communication channel can be used as a bind mount for containers, so when the CLI in the container runs, it’s actually connecting to the socket or named pipe on your machine. That unlocks some useful scenarios where apps inside containers can query Docker to find other containers, or start and stop new containers. There’s also a security concern here because the app in the container has full access to all the Docker features on the host, so you need to use this carefully with Docker images that you trust (you can trust my `diamol` images, of course).

Listing 11.1 shows part of the Docker Compose files you ran to start the infrastructure containers, focusing on the Jenkins specification. You can see the volumes are binding to the Docker socket in the Linux version and the named pipe in the Windows version—this is the address of the Docker API.

##### Listing 11.1 Binding the Docker CLI in Jenkins to the Docker Engine
    
    
    # docker-compose.yml
    services:
      jenkins:
        image: diamol/jenkins:2e
        ports:
          - "8080:8080"
        networks:
          - infrastructure
    
    # docker-compose-linux.yml
    jenkins:
      volumes:
        - type: bind
          source: /var/run/docker.sock
          target: /var/run/docker.sock
    
    # docker-compose-windows.yml
    jenkins:
      volumes:
        - type: npipe
          source: \\.\pipe\docker_engine
          target: \\.\pipe\docker_engine

That’s all the infrastructure you need. Jenkins connects to the Docker Engine to run Docker and Docker Compose commands, and it can connect to the Git server and the Docker registry by DNS because they’re all containers in the same Docker network. The CI process runs a single command to build the application, and all the complexity of the build is captured in Dockerfiles and Docker Compose files.

## 11.3 Capturing build settings with Docker Compose

The job that Jenkins ran has built a new version of the random number application from chapter 8. You’ve seen in chapter 10 how you can break up an application definition across multiple Compose files, and this app uses that approach to capture the details of the build settings. Listing 11.2 is abridged from the base `docker -compose.yml` file in the `ch11/exercises` folder—it contains the web and API service definitions with environment variables in the image name.

##### Listing 11.2 Core Docker Compose file using variables in image tags
    
    
    services:
      numbers-api:
        image: 
    ${REGISTRY:-docker.io}/diamol/ch11-api:2e-${BUILD_NUMBER:-local}
        networks:
          - app-net
    
      numbers-web:
        image: 
    ${REGISTRY:-docker.io}/diamol/ch11-web:2e-${BUILD_NUMBER:-local}
        environment:
          - RngApi__Url=http:/./numbers-api/rng
        networks:
          - app-net

The environment variable syntax here includes a default value set with `:-`, so `${REGISTRY:-docker.io}` tells Compose to replace that token at runtime with the value of the environment variable called `REGISTRY`. If that environment variable doesn’t exist or is empty, it will use the default value `docker.io`, which is the domain for Docker Hub. I use the same approach with the image tag, so if the environment variable `BUILD_NUMBER` is set, that value goes into the tag; otherwise, `local` gets used.

This is a very useful pattern for supporting a CI process and a local developer build using the same set of artifacts. When a developer builds the API image, they won’t have any environment variables set, so the image will be called `docker.io/diamol/ch11-numbers-api:2e-v3-build-local`. But `docker.io` is Docker Hub, which is the default domain, so the image will just be shown as `diamol/ch11-numbers-api:2e-v3 -build-local`. When the same build runs in Jenkins, the variables will be set to use the local Docker registry and the actual build number for the job, which Jenkins sets as an incrementing number, so the image name will be `registry.local:5010/diamol/ch11-numbers-api:2e-v3-build-2`.

Setting a flexible image name is an important part of the CI setup, but the key information is specified in the override file `docker-compose-build.yml`, which tells Compose where to find the Dockerfiles.

##### Try it now

You can build the app locally using the same steps as the CI build pipeline. Start from a terminal session, browse to the directory for the chapter, and build the app with Docker Compose:
    
    
    cd ch11/exercises
    # build both images:
    docker compose `
     -f docker-compose.yml `
     -f docker-compose-build.yml `
     build
    # check the labels for the web image:
    docker image inspect -f '{{.Config.Labels}}' `
     diamol/ch11-numbers-api:2e-v3-build-local

You can see my output in figure 11.4.

##### Figure 11.4 Building images with Docker Compose and checking the image labels 

Building the application through Docker Compose effectively runs a `docker` `image` `build` command for every service that has build settings specified. That could be a dozen images or a single one—even for one image, it’s a good practice to build with Compose because then your Compose file specifies the tag you want when you build the image. A few more things in this build are part of a successful CI pipeline—you can see that in the final `inspect` command that lists the labels for the image.

Docker lets you apply labels to most resources—containers, images, networks, and volumes. They’re simple key/value pairs where you can store additional data about the resource. Labels are very useful on images because they get baked into the image and move with it—when you push or pull the image, the labels go along too. When you build your app with a CI pipeline, it’s important to have an audit trail that lets you track back from the running container to the build job that created it, and image labels help you do that.

Listing 11.3 shows part of the Dockerfile for the random number API (you’ll find the full file in the exercises for this chapter at `numbers/numbers-api/ Dockerfile.v4`). There are two new Dockerfile instructions here: `ARG` and `LABEL`.

##### Listing 11.3 Image labels and build arguments in the Dockerfile
    
    
    # app image
    FROM diamol/dotnet-aspnet:2e
    
    ARG BUILD_NUMBER=0
    ARG BUILD_TAG=local
    
    LABEL app_version="3.0"
    LABEL build_number=${BUILD_NUMBER}
    LABEL build_tag=${BUILD_TAG}
    
    ENTRYPOINT ["dotnet", "Numbers.Api.dll"]

The `LABEL` instruction just applies the key/value pair from the Dockerfile to the image when it gets built. You can see `app_version=3.0` specified in the Dockerfile, and that matches the label output in figure 11.4. The other two `LABEL` instructions use environment variables to set the label value, and those environment variables are provided by the `ARG` instructions.

`ARG` is very similar to the `ENV` instruction, except that it works at build time on the image, rather than at run time in the container. They both set the value of an environment variable, but that setting only exists for the duration of the build for `ARG` instructions, so any containers you run from the image don’t see that variable. It’s a great way to pass data into the build process that isn’t relevant for running containers. I’m using it here to provide values that go into the image labels—in the CI process, these record the number of the build and the full build name. The `ARG` instruction also sets default values, so when you build the image locally without passing any variables, you see `build_number:0` and `build_tag:local` in the image labels.

You can see how the environment settings in the CI pipeline get passed down into the Docker `build` command in the Compose override file. Listing 11.4 shows the contents of the `docker-compose-build.yml` file with all the build settings.

##### Listing 11.4 Docker Compose build settings and reusable arguments
    
    
    x-args: &args
      args:
        BUILD_NUMBER: ${BUILD_NUMBER:-0}
        BUILD_TAG: ${BUILD_TAG:-local}
    
    services:
      numbers-api:
        build:
          context: numbers
          dockerfile: numbers-api/Dockerfile.v4
          <<: *args
    
      numbers-web:
        build:
          context: numbers
          dockerfile: numbers-web/Dockerfile.v4
          <<: *args

This Compose file shouldn’t be too complicated unless you skipped chapter 10, in which case, you should go back and read it. It won’t take you more than a lunchtime. There are three parts to the `build` block in the Compose specification:

  * `context`—The path Docker will use as the working directory for the build. This is usually the current directory, which you pass with a period in the `docker` `image` `build` command, but here, it’s the `numbers` directory. The path is relative to the location of the Compose file. 
  * `dockerfile`—The path to the Dockerfile, relative to the context. 
  * `args`—Any build arguments to pass, which need to match the keys specified as `ARG` instructions in the Dockerfile. Both Dockerfiles for this app use the same `BUILD_NUMBER` and `BUILD_TAG` arguments, so I’m using a Compose extension field to define those values once, and YAML merge to apply it to both services. 



You’ll see default values specified in lots of different places to make sure that support for the CI process doesn’t break other workflows. You should always aim for a single Dockerfile that gets built in the same way however the build is run. Default arguments in the Compose file mean the build succeeds when you run it outside of the CI environment, and defaults in the Dockerfile mean the image builds correctly even if you don’t use Compose.

##### Try it now

You can build the random number API image with the normal `image` `build` command, bypassing the setup in the Compose files. You can call the image whatever you like. The build succeeds and the labels get applied because of the defaults in the Dockerfile:
    
    
    # change to the numbers directory
    # (this is done with the context setting in Compose):
    cd ch11/exercises/numbers
    # build the image, specifying the Dockerfile path and a build argument:
    docker image build -f numbers-api/Dockerfile.v4 `
     --build-arg BUILD_TAG=ch11 -t numbers-api .
    # check the labels:
    docker image inspect -f '{{.Config.Labels}}' numbers-api

My output is shown in figure 11.5—you can see in the labels that the value `build_tag:ch11` was set from my `build` command, but the value `build_number:0` was set from the default for the `ARG` in the Dockerfile.

There are quite a few levels of detail here just to get labels into an image, but it’s an important thing to get right. You should be able to run `docker` `image` `inspect` and find exactly where that image came from, tracking it back to the CI job that produced it, which in turn tracks back to the exact version of code that triggered the build. It’s an audit trail from the running container in any environment back to the source code.

##### Figure 11.5 Including default values for build arguments supports the developer build workflow.

## 11.4 Writing CI jobs with no dependencies except Docker

You’ve been happily building images for the random number app in this chapter using Docker and Docker Compose, without needing any other tools installed on your machine. There are two components to the app, and both are written in .NET, but you don’t need the .NET SDK on your machine to build them. They use the multi-stage Dockerfile approach from chapter 4 to compile and package the app, so Docker and Compose are all you need.

This is a major benefit of containerized CI, and it’s supported by all the managed build services such as Docker Hub, GitHub Actions, and Azure DevOps. It means you no longer need a build server with lots of tools installed or for the tools to be kept up-to-date with all the developers. Your build scripts also become very simple—developers can use the exact same build scripts locally and get the same output as the CI pipeline, making it easy to move between different build services.

We’re using Jenkins for our CI process, and Jenkins jobs can be configured with a simple text file that lives in source control along with the application code, Docker files, and Compose files. Listing 11.5 shows part of the pipeline (from the file `ch11/exercises/Jenkinsfile`) along with the batch script that the pipeline step executes.

##### Listing 11.5 Jenkinsfile build step that describes the CI job
    
    
    # the build stage in the Jenkinsfile- it switches directory, then runs two
    # shell commands - the first sets up a script file so it can be executed
    # and the second calls the script:
    
    stage('Build') {
      steps {
        dir('ch11/exercises') {
          sh 'chmod +x ./ci/01-build.bat'
          sh './ci/01-build.bat'
          }
      }
    }
    
    # and this is what's in 01-build.bat script:
    docker compose
      -f docker-compose.yml
      -f docker-compose-build.yml
      build --pull

Well look at that—it’s just the same `docker compose` `build` command you ran locally, except it adds the `pull` flag, which means Docker will pull the latest version of any images it needs during the build. That’s a good habit to get into when you do your builds anyway because it means you’ll always build your image from the latest base image with all the recent security fixes. It’s especially important in the CI process because there could be a change in an image your Dockerfile uses that breaks your app, and you want to find that out as soon as possible.

The build step runs a simple script file. The filename ends with `.bat` so it runs nicely under Jenkins in a Windows container, but it also works just fine in a Linux container. This step runs the build, and because it’s a simple command-line call, all the output from Docker Compose, which is also the output from Docker, gets captured and stored in the build logs.

##### Try it now

You can view the logs in the Jenkins UI by browsing to `http://localhost:8080/job/diamol` to see the jobs. In the pipeline view, click on the Build step for your successful build job, and then click Logs. You can expand the steps of the build to see the usual Docker build output.

My output is shown in figure 11.6.

##### Figure 11.6 Viewing the output from the pipeline build in Jenkins shows the usual Docker logs.

Each step in the build pipeline follows the same pattern; it just calls a batch script that does the actual work by running Docker Compose commands. This approach makes it easy to switch between different build services; instead of writing the logic in a proprietary pipeline syntax, you write it in scripts and use the pipeline to call the scripts. I could add pipeline files to run the build in GitLab or GitHub Actions, and they would call the same batch scripts. The stages of the Jenkins build are all powered by containers:

  * _Verify_ __ —This calls the script `00-verify.bat`, which just prints out version information for Docker and Docker Compose. This is a useful way to start the pipeline because it verifies that the Docker dependencies are available and records the versions of the tools that built the image. 
  * _Build_ __ —This calls `01-build.bat`, which you’ve already seen; it uses Docker Compose to build the images. The `REGISTRY` environment variable is specified in the Jenkinsfile, so images will be tagged for the local registry. 
  * _Test_ __ —This calls `02-test.bat`, which uses Docker Compose to start the whole application, then lists out the containers and brings the application down again. This is just a simple illustration, but it does prove that the containers run without failing. In a real project, you would bring up the app and then run end-to-end tests in another container. 
  * _Push_ __ —This calls `03-push.bat`, which uses Docker Compose to push all the built images. The image tags have the local registry domain, so if the build and test stages are successful, the images get pushed to the registry. 



Stages in the CI pipeline are sequential, so if there’s a failure at any point, the job ends. That means the registry only stores images for potential release candidates—any image that has been pushed to the registry must have successfully passed the build and test stages.

##### Try it now

You have one successful build from Jenkins—build number 1 failed because there was no source code, and then build number 2 succeeded. You can query your local registry container using the REST API, and you should see just a version 2 tag for each of the random number images:
    
    
    # the catalog endpoint shows all the image repositories:
    curl http:/./registry.local:5010/v2/_catalog
    # the tags endpoint shows the individual tags for one repository:
    curl http:/./registry.local:5010/v2/diamol/ch11-numbers-api/tags/list
    curl http:/./registry.local:5010/v2/diamol/ch11-numbers-web/tags/list

You can see my output in figure 11.7. I ran a few more builds, so there are multiple tags for the web and API image repositories, but neither has a `build-1` tag because the first build failed and didn’t push any images.

This is a fairly simple CI pipeline, but it shows you all the key stages of the build and some important best practices. The key thing is to let Docker do the hard work and to build the stages of your pipeline in scripts. Then, you can use any CI tool and just plug your scripts into the tool’s pipeline definition.

##### Figure 11.7 Sending web requests to the registry API to query the images stored in the container

## 11.5 Understanding containers in the CI process

Compiling and running applications in containers is just the start of what you can do with Docker in your CI pipeline. Docker adds a layer of consistency on top of all of your application builds, and you can use that consistency to add many useful features to your pipeline. Figure 11.8 shows a more extensive CI process that includes security-scanning container images for known vulnerabilities and digitally signing images to assert their provenance.

##### Figure 11.8 A production-grade CI pipeline that adds stages with security gates 

Docker calls this approach the _secure software supply chain_ , and it’s important for all sizes of organizations because it gives you confidence that the software you’re about to deploy is safe. You can run tooling in your pipeline to check for known security vulnerabilities and fail the build if there are problems. You can configure your production environment to only run containers from images that have been digitally signed—a process that happens at the end of a successful build. When your containers are deployed to production, you can be certain that they’re running from images that came through your build process and that the software they contain has passed all of your tests and is free from security problems.

The checks and balances you add in your pipeline work on containers and images, so they apply in the same way across all your application platforms. If you work with multiple technologies across your projects, you’ll be using different base images and different build steps in the Dockerfiles, but the CI pipelines will all be the same.

## 11.6 Lab

Lab time! You’re going to build your own CI pipeline—but don’t run scared. We’ll use the ideas and exercises from this chapter, but the pipeline stages will be much simpler.

In the lab folder for this chapter, you’ll find a copy of the source code for the to-do app from chapter 6. The build for that app is almost ready to go as it contains the Jenkinsfile, the CI scripts, and the core Docker Compose file. There are just a couple of things for you to do:

  * Write an override file called `docker-compose-build.yml` with the build settings. 
  * Create a Jenkins job to run the pipeline. 
  * Push your changes to Gogs in the `diamol` repository. 



Although it’s just three tasks, don’t be disheartened if your first few builds fail and you need to check the logs and tweak some things. No one in history has ever written a Jenkins job that passed on the first run, so here are a few hints:

  * Your Compose override will be similar to the one in the exercises—specifying the context and a build argument for the build number label. 
  * In the Jenkins UI, you click New Item to create a job, and you can copy from the existing `diamol` job. 
  * The new job setup will be the same except for the path to the Jenkinsfile—you’ll need to specify the `lab` folder instead of the `exercises` folder. 



If you’re not getting far with this, you’ll find more information in the README file in the lab folder, complete with screenshots for the Jenkins steps and a sample build configuration for the Docker Compose file: <https://mng.bz/JwYK>.


# 12 Running containers on different platforms

The two main types of CPU architecture are Intel and Arm, and the two main types of OS are Linux and Windows. Applications are built for a specific platform and aren’t portable, so if you build your app for Linux on Arm, it won’t work on an Intel machine or on Windows. That’s true for container images too, and it can lead to a whole lot of complexity and confusion. Users might find that an image works fine on their desktop (Intel) but fails on their MacBook (Arm). Luckily, Docker has had this covered for a long time with _multi-platform images:_ these look and behave like normal Docker images, but they’re published with multiple variants, each targeting a different platform, but all using the same image name. When you run a container from a multi-platform image, Docker pulls the matching variant for your CPU and OS. If you use the same image name on a different platform, you’ll get a different image variant, but it will be the same app and will work in the same way. It’s a super-easy workflow for the user, but requires some effort from the image publisher.

In this chapter, you’ll learn various ways to produce multi-platform builds, but if you’re thinking of skipping this one because you don’t use Windows or Arm, you should at least read the first section to learn why this is a game-changing option.

## 12.1 Why you need to support multiple platforms

Docker has supported Arm CPUs for many years, but it was a bit of a niche market—until Apple started building processors based on Arm chipsets and Amazon Web Services (AWS) released cloud virtual machines (VMs) running on Arm that were 40% cheaper than the Intel equivalents. Then developers realized that if they wanted to run their favorite Docker containers on their new Macs, and if administrators wanted an easy way to slash cloud costs, they had to take a deep dive into multi-platform images.

Official images on Docker Hub are all multi-platform, as are all the images for the Try It Now exercises in this book. Users might not be aware of that because their containers just work on whatever platform they use. But you need to understand how it works so that you can support teams who use a mixture of platforms or deploy to cheaper machines in the cloud (Azure and Google Cloud also have Arm options). To do that, you need to build variants of your own images using Arm and Intel machines. That’s the hard part that Docker solves for both production build farms and the developer workflow. Docker Desktop supports emulation to build Docker images and run containers with Arm on Intel machines and vice versa.

##### Try it now 

Docker Desktop is preconfigured with the open source Quick Emulator (QEMU). If you’re using Docker Engine on Linux, you can install QEMU yourself (search for `tonistiigi/binfmt`), and if you’re on Windows, make sure to use Linux container mode. Open a terminal, and build an image using emulation with the `platform` flag:
    
    
    # switch to the exercises folder:
    cd ch12/exercises
    # build for 64-bit Arm:
    docker build -t diamol/ch12-hello:2e-linux-arm64 `
     --platform linux/arm64 ./hello
    # and for 64-bit Intel:
    docker build -t diamol/ch12-hello:2e-linux-amd64 `
     --platform linux/amd64 ./hello
    # check the native architecture of your engine:
    docker info -f '{{.OSType}}/{{.Architecture}}'
    # and the architecture of the images:
    docker image inspect diamol/ch12-hello:2e-linux-arm64 `
     -f '{{.Os}}/{{.Architecture}}'
    docker image inspect diamol/ch12-hello:2e-linux-amd64 `
     -f '{{.Os}}/{{.Architecture}}'

You can build Linux images targeted for the 64-bit Arm platform and the 64-bit Intel platform, even though your own machine is only one of those. This image uses a multi-stage Dockerfile to compile and package a Go application. The Go tools are multi-platform, and the base images in the Dockerfile (for the SDK and the runtime) have Arm and Intel variants. That’s all you need to support cross-platform builds.

You could push these images to a registry and run containers from them on a genuine Arm or Intel machine, and it will work just fine. You can see my output in figure 12.1, where I’ve built an Arm image from an Intel machine.

##### Figure 12.1 Using emulation in Docker Desktop to build images for different platforms

This isn’t a multi-platform image—it’s two images with different tags. Users would need to pick the correct tag to run a container on their platform. Docker knows many things about your machine, including the OS and the CPU architecture, and it will use those as a match when you try to pull images. Pulling an image is more than just downloading the layers—there’s also optimization to expand the compressed layers and get the image ready to run. That optimization only works if the image you want to use is a match for the architecture you’re running on, so if there’s no match, you’ll get an error—you can’t pull the image to even try to run a container.

##### Try it now 

You can use any Docker Engine running Linux containers to verify this. Try downloading a Microsoft Windows image:
    
    
    # pull the Windows Nano Server image:
    docker image pull mcr.microsoft.com/windows/nanoserver:ltsc2022

You can see my output in figure 12.2 where Docker gets the OS and CPU of the current engine and checks to see whether there’s a matching variant in the registry. There’s no match, so the image isn’t pulled, and I get an error.

##### Figure 12.2 You can’t pull an image from a registry if there’s no variant to match your OS and CPU.

The _manifest list_ is the set of variants for the image. The Windows Nano Server image isn’t truly multi-platform—it will run on different versions of Windows, but there are no Linux variants in the manifest list. The basic principle is that the platform for the image has to match the platform of the engine, but there are a few nuances—Linux images can be pulled for nonmatching CPU architectures, but containers will fail with an obscure `user process caused "exec format error"` message. Some older Windows engines have an experimental feature called Linux Containers on Windows (LCOW), so they can run Linux containers (but complex apps will fail with even more obscure errors). It’s best to stick to the matching architecture for the engine, and multi-platform images let you tailor the image to each OS and CPU.

## 12.2 Building multi-platform image variants

You have several options for building image variants for different platforms. In the first, you follow the example of the hello app in this chapter’s exercises: write a multi-stage Dockerfile that compiles the app from source and then packages it to run in a container. You can build that Dockerfile on different platforms, and if the images you use for the SDK and runtime support all the architectures you want to support, you’re good to go.

The huge benefit of this approach is that you have a single Dockerfile that you build on different machines to get the platform variants. I use this approach to build my own golden images for the .NET stack. Figure 12.3 shows the approach for the SDK.

You can’t follow this approach if your source image isn’t multi-platform or if it doesn’t support all the platforms you want to support. The official images on Docker Hub are multi-platform, but they don’t all support every variation. In that case, you’ll need different Dockerfiles with different `FROM` images, maybe one for Linux and one for Windows, or maybe additional ones for Arm 32-bit and 64-bit. This approach requires more management because you have multiple Dockerfiles to maintain, but it gives you a lot more freedom to adapt behavior for each target architecture. I use this approach for my golden image for Maven (a tool to build Java apps). Figure 12.4 shows the stack.

##### Figure 12.3 Use a multi-stage Dockerfile based on multi-platform images to build multiple image variants.

##### Figure 12.4 You can also build image variants using Dockerfiles tailored for each platform.

The exercises for this chapter have a folder-list app that is very simple—it just prints some basic information about the runtime and then lists the contents of a folder. There are four Dockerfiles, one for each of the architectures supported in this book: Windows on Intel, Linux on Intel, Linux on 32-bit Arm, and Linux on 64-bit Arm. You can use Docker Desktop’s CPU emulation with Linux containers to build and test three of these.

##### Try it now 

Build images for different platforms using the Dockerfile for each platform. Each Dockerfile is slightly different, so we can compare the results when we run containers:
    
    
    cd ./folder-list
    # build for the native architecture - Intel/AMD:
    docker image build -t diamol/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-amd64 `
     -f ./Dockerfile.linux-amd64 --platform linux/amd64 .
    # build for Arm 64-bit:
    docker image build -t diamol/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-arm64 `
     -f ./Dockerfile.linux-arm64 --platform linux/arm64 .
    # and for Arm 32-bit
    docker image build -t diamol/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-arm `
     -f ./Dockerfile.linux-arm --platform linux/arm .
    # run all the containers and verify the output:
    docker container run --platform linux/amd64 `
     diamol/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-amd64
    docker container run --platform linux/arm64 `
     diamol/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-arm64
    docker container run --platform linux/arm `
     diamol/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-arm

The containers print some simple text when they run—a hardcoded string stating the OS and architecture they should be using, followed by the actual OS and CPU reported by the OS, and then a folder list containing a single file. You can see my output in figure 12.5. Docker uses emulation where necessary, so it uses an Arm emulator when running the Arm-32 and Arm-64 Linux variants on my Intel machine.

##### Figure 12.5 Images built for a specific platform can be run using emulation in Docker Desktop.

The Dockerfiles for the Linux variants are all very similar, except for the hardcoded string for the expected architecture. The Windows variant has the same behavior, but Windows has different commands to print the output. This is where multiple Dockerfiles for each architecture become useful; I can have completely different Dockerfile instructions but still get the same desired output. Listing 12.1 compares the Dockerfile for the 64-bit Arm Linux version and the 64-bit Intel Windows version.

##### Listing 12.1 Dockerfiles for Linux and Windows image variants
    
    
    # linux
    FROM diamol/base:2e-linux-arm64
    
    WORKDIR /app
    COPY file.txt .
    CMD echo "Built as: linux/arm64" && \ 
        uname -a && \
        ls /app
    
    # windows
    # escape=`
    FROM diamol/base:2e-windows-amd64
    
    WORKDIR /app
    COPY file.txt .
    
    CMD echo Built as: windows/amd64 && ` 
        echo %PROCESSOR_ARCHITECTURE% %PROCESSOR_IDENTIFIER% && `
        dir /B C:\app

Each version starts with a different `FROM` image, which is specific to the target architecture rather than a multi-platform image. The Windows Dockerfile uses the `escape` keyword to change the line-break character, changing it to a backtick instead of the default backslash, so I can use backslashes in directory paths. There’s no Windows equivalent of the Linux `uname` command, so to print the CPU architecture, I echo out some environment variables that Windows sets. The functionality is broadly the same, but I can take a different path to get there because this is a Windows-specific Dockerfile.

This is a simple example, but you typically need multiple Dockerfiles if you want to build a multi-platform version of a third-party app. The Prometheus and Grafana golden images for this book are good examples. The project teams publish multi-platform images for all the Linux variants I want to use, but not for Windows. So, I have Linux Dockerfiles that are based on the project images and Windows Dockerfiles that install the app from a web download. For your own apps, it should be easy to have a single Dockerfile and avoid the extra maintenance, but you need to be careful that you use only a subset of OS commands that you know work in all the target architectures. It’s easy to accidentally include a command (e.g., `chmod`) that doesn’t work on one architecture and results in a broken variant.

##### Try it now 

There’s one other Dockerfile for the folder-list app, which is an attempt at a multi-platform Dockerfile. It uses a multi-platform image as the base, but it mixes Linux and Windows commands, so the image it builds will fail on every architecture:
    
    
    # build the multi-platform app:
    docker image build -t diamol/ch12-folder-list:2e .
    # and try to run it:
    docker container run diamol/ch12-folder-list:2e

You’ll find that the build completes successfully, so it looks like you have a good image, but the container will fail every time you run it. You can see my output in figure 12.6. I ran both Linux and Windows versions of the image, and both containers fail because the `CMD` instruction contains invalid commands.

It’s important to bear this in mind, especially if you use complex startup scripts. `RUN` instructions will fail at build time if you use an unknown OS command, but `CMD` instructions aren’t verified, so you won’t know the image is broken until you try to run a container.

##### Figure 12.6 It’s easy to build a multi-platform image that fails at run time on some (or all) platforms.

One last thing we need to understand here before we go on to push variants and creating multi-platform images is which architectures Docker supports and the various strange code names you’ll encounter when you start using them. Table 12.1 shows the major OS and architecture combinations and aliases for the CPU.

##### Table 12.1 Architectures supported by Docker, with their code names

OS  |  CPU  |  Word Length  |  CPU Name  |  CPU Aliases   
---|---|---|---|---  
Windows   
|  Intel/AMD   
|  64-bit   
|  amd64   
|  x86_64, x64   
  
Linux   
|  Intel/AMD   
|  64-bit   
|  amd64   
|  x86_64, x64   
  
Linux   
|  Arm   
|  64-bit   
|  arm64   
|  aarch64, armv8   
  
Linux   
|  Arm   
|  32-bit   
|  arm   
|  arm32v7, armv7, armhf   
  
  
Docker supports many more architectures, but these are the main ones you’ll find. The amd64 CPU type is the same instruction set in Intel and AMD machines that powers most desktop, server, and laptop computers (Docker also supports 32-bit Intel x86 processors). Apple Silicon is 64-bit Arm, and Arm CPUs are also found in phones, IoT devices, and single-board computers; the most famous is the Raspberry Pi, which was 32-bit until the release of the Raspberry Pi 4, which was the first native 64-bit version. Mainframe users aren’t left out, either—Docker supports IBM CPU architectures for Linux, so if you have an IBM Z, POWER, or PowerPC machine in your basement, you can migrate your mainframe apps to Docker containers too.

## 12.3 Grouping platform-specific images with manifests

You can build images for different platforms using Docker Desktop, but they don’t become multi-platform images until you push all the variants to a registry and group them together with a manifest. The _manifest_ is a piece of metadata that links image variants to the same image tag. You generate manifests using the Docker command line and push them to the registry too. The manifest contains a list of all the image variants, which need to exist on the registry first, so the workflow is to create and push all the images, and then create and push the manifest.

##### Try it now

Push the image variants of the folder-list app you’ve built. First, you’ll need to tag them with your Docker Hub ID so that you can push them to your account—you don’t have permission to push to the `diamol` organization:
    
    
    # store your Docker ID in a variable – with PowerShell:
    $dockerId='<your-docker-hub-id>'
    # or a *nix shell:
    dockerId='<your-docker-hub-id>'
    # tag the images with your own account name:
    docker image tag diamol/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-amd64 `
     "$dockerId/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-amd64"
    docker image tag diamol/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-arm64 `
     "$dockerId/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-arm64"
    docker image tag diamol/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-arm `
     "$dockerId/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-arm"
    # and push to Docker Hub (this pushes all the tags for the image):
    docker image push --all-tags "$dockerId/ch12-folder-list"

You’ll see all of your images get pushed to Docker Hub. Docker registries are platform-agnostic—the image specification is the same for all architectures, and the registry stores them all in the same way. Registries do know which platform an image was built for, and they provide that to the Docker Engine as a check before it pulls them. My output is shown in figure 12.7. The architecture for each image is stored in the image metadata. I like to include the platform name in the image tag too, but that’s not a requirement.

Before we create the manifest, we’ll have a look at an existing multi-platform image. You use `docker` `manifest` commands to create manifests locally, push them to a registry, and also inspect existing manifests on a registry. Inspecting manifests is a helpful way to see which architectures an image supports without having to navigate the Docker Hub UI. You don’t need to pull any of the images locally because the command reads all the metadata from the registry.

##### Figure 12.7 Pushing all the image variants is the first stage in publishing a multi-platform image.

##### Try it now

Use the command-line interface (CLI) to inspect the published manifest of the base image for this book:
    
    
    docker manifest inspect diamol/base:2e

The `manifest` `inspect` command has no filter or format argument, so you can’t limit the output. The command will show you all the manifest details for the image tag, so it works with single images as well as multi-platform images. In the output, you see each image’s unique digest, along with the CPU architecture and OS. My (abridged) output is shown in figure 12.8.

##### Figure 12.8 Multi-platform images have a manifest list, which contains the manifest for each platform variant.

Now, you can create the manifest, which will exist on your local machine first (just like the images), and then you’ll push it up to the registry. What you’re creating is technically a manifest list, which groups together a set of images under a single image tag. Every image already has its own manifest that you can inspect from the registry, but if multiple manifests are returned, you’ll have a multi-architecture image. Figure 12.9 shows the relationship between images, manifests, and manifest lists.

You can think of a manifest list as a list of image tags, and the name of the manifest list as the name of the multi-platform image. The image variants you’ve built in this chapter all have tags to identify the OS and CPU; you can create a manifest using the same image name without a tag, and that will be available as a multi-platform image using the default `latest` tag. You could also push your images with a tag that includes a version number in addition to the OS and CPU and then use just the version number for the multi-platform image tag.

##### Figure 12.9 Manifests and manifest lists exist on a Docker registry and contain metadata about images.

##### Try it now

Create a manifest to link all the Linux variants, and then push it to Docker Hub. The name of the manifest becomes the image tag of the multi-architecture image:
    
    
    # create a manifest list with a name
    # followed by all the tags it contains:
    docker manifest create "$dockerId/ch12-folder-list:2e" `
     "$dockerId/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-amd64" `
     "$dockerId/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-arm64" `
     "$dockerId/ch12-folder-list:2e-linux-arm"
    # push the manifest to Docker Hub:
    docker manifest push "$dockerId/ch12-folder-list:2e"
    # now browse to your page on Docker Hub and check the image

You’ll find when you browse to the image on Docker Hub that there’s a `2e` tag with multiple variants—the UI shows the OS and CPU architecture and the digest that uniquely identifies each image. Anyone with a Linux Docker Engine can run a container from that image, and it will run the amd64 variant on an Intel or AMD machine, the arm64 variant on Apple Silicon, a cloud Arm VM or a recent Raspberry Pi, and the arm variant on older Raspberry Pis. You can see my Docker Hub repository in figure 12.10.

##### Figure 12.10 A multi-platform image has a single tag but many variants. Docker Hub shows all the variants.

These Arm images were built with emulation in Docker Desktop, which is only really viable for occasional builds. Emulation is slow, and not every instruction works in the same way under emulation as it does in a real CPU. If you want to publish multi-platform images and you want builds to be fast and 100% accurate for the target platform, you need a build farm. That’s what I have to build the images in this book—a set of machines with different CPU architectures, set up with all the OSs I want to support. My continuous integration/continuous deployment (CI/CD) pipeline connects to the Docker Engine on each machine to build an image variant and push it to Docker Hub, and then the job creates and pushes the manifest. You can do the same with managed services in the cloud; GitHub Actions and Azure DevOps provide build agents with different OSs and CPU architectures that you can use in a multi-platform pipeline.

## 12.4 Building and pushing multi-platform images with Buildx

There’s another way to run a Docker build farm that is more efficient and far easier to use, with a recent feature of Docker called Buildx. _Buildx_ is an extended version of the Docker build commands, and it uses a new build engine that is heavily optimized to improve build performance. It still uses Dockerfiles as the input and produces images as the output, so you can use Buildx as a straight replacement for `docker` `image` `build`. Buildx truly shines for cross-platform builds because it can send tasks to multiple Docker Engines, which means you can distribute builds across several platforms with a single command.

Buildx has only experimental support for Windows containers right now, and it only supports building from a single Dockerfile, so it won’t cover scenarios where you need different setups in your variants. (I can’t use Buildx for building every image for this book.) But if you only need to support CPU variants for Linux, it works very nicely. You use Buildx to create and manage the build farm as well as to build images.

We’ll walk through an example so that you can try out a real distributed build farm, but we’ll use just one machine and pretend that we have two. The Docker command line can work with different Docker Engines using _contexts,_ which contain the address and connection details for remote machines. We’ll create a copy of the default context Docker is using, so we’ll have two contexts pointing to our single Docker Engine—making Buildx think we have two machines. Figure 12.11 shows how our build farm will work.

##### Figure 12.11 You can use two addresses for a single Docker Engine to host two Buildx builders.

Real build farms use contexts that have the network address of each Docker Engine. With multiple machines, you need to set up Docker to listen on the network, and secure it with SSH or TLS. For our build farm, we don’t need to do that because we’ll be using the local address, and Buildx won’t be sending traffic across the network.

##### Try it now

Create a duplicate of your current Docker context:
    
    
    # print your current context – Docker always creates one by default:
    docker context show
    # now create a copy of the current context:
    docker context create --from $(docker context show) ch12-context
    # list your contexts – the new one will have the same address:
    docker context ls

Figure 12.12 shows my new Docker context, with the endpoint address copied from the default Docker Desktop context (the actual address will be different for you).

##### Figure 12.12 Buildx uses contexts to talk to builders—it doesn’t mind if different contexts use the same address.

Buildx connects to the Docker context when you set it up and identifies the platforms the Docker Engine can build. If the Docker Engine has emulation, then it can build multiple platforms, but typically your build farm will be one platform per builder, so all your image variants are built natively. You can also tell Buildx which platform you want to build on each Docker Engine (Buildx calls them _build nodes_), and that’s what we’ll do with our simulated build farm.

##### Try it now

Create a builder with two build nodes—one for Linux Arm64 and one for Linux Intel. Buildx will use emulation where it needs to, so it doesn’t matter what your actual CPU architecture is:
    
    
    # create the builder – this adds a node with the current context:
    docker buildx create --name ch12-builder --platform linux/amd64
    # append a second node using the duplicate context:
    docker buildx create --append --name ch12-builder `
     --platform linux/arm64 ch12-context
    # list your builders:
    docker buildx ls

This seems like a lot of effort to build images with emulation (which we’ve already done with simple `image build` commands), but it’s the process that’s important here. You would do exactly the same to set up a builder with multiple native nodes: create a context for each Docker Engine and then group them as Buildx builder nodes. Figure 12.13 shows my builder.

##### Figure 12.13 We’ve created a fake build farm with just a few commands—a real build farm is just as easy.

Now the build farm is ready and can build multi-platform images that can run as 64-bit Intel or Arm Linux containers, as long as the Dockerfile it’s building uses images that support those two architectures. Buildx spins up builds concurrently across the builder nodes, sending them the Dockerfile and the folder with the Docker build context (which would normally contain your source code). The build command can also instruct the nodes to push their variant to a registry, and Buildx will compile and push the manifest list. With a single command, you can build and push a multi-platform image with as many variants as your build farm supports.

##### Try it now

Build the same sample app from section 12.1, using the new builder and pushing straight to Docker Hub:
    
    
    # build and push the hello app for all the builder's platforms:
    docker buildx build --builder ch12-builder `
     --platform linux/arm64,linux/amd64 `
     -t "$dockerId/ch12-hello-x:2e" `
     --push ./hello

The output from a Buildx build is impressive; it’s a great thing to have running when other people can see your screen. The client shows log lines from each builder node, and you get lots of fast output, so it looks like you’re doing something immensely technical. Actually, Buildx does all the work, and you’ll see from the output that it pushes the images, creates the manifest list, and pushes the manifest for you. Figure 12.14 shows the start and end of my build.

##### Figure 12.14 When using the builder, each node builds one platform, and Buildx pushes the multi-platform manifest.

Buildx makes these multi-platform builds very simple. You supply the nodes for each architecture you want to support, and Buildx can use them all, so your build commands don’t change whether you’re building for 2 architectures or 10. There’s an interesting difference with Buildx images on Docker Hub: there are no individual image tags for the variants—just the single multi-platform tag. Compare that to the previous section, where we manually pushed the variants and then added the manifest. In that case, all the variants have their own tags on Docker Hub, and as you build and deploy more image versions, that can get difficult for users to navigate. If you don’t need to support Windows containers, Buildx is the best way to build multi-platform images right now.

## 12.5 Considering platform support in your road maps

If you don’t need multi-platform images right now, that’s fine—thanks for reading this chapter anyway. It’s definitely worth knowing how multi-platform images work and how you can build your own, even if you don’t plan to do that yet because they well may come onto your road map. You may join a project where the developers use a mixture of Apple and Intel laptops, you may need to cut running costs in the cloud, or your customers may be clamoring for Windows support. Figure 12.15 shows how projects can evolve with multi-platform support, adding more variants over the years when the need arises.

##### Figure 12.15 Projects typically launch with Linux Intel support and add variants as they become popular.

You can future-proof yourself and make the switch to multi-platform images easy if you stick to two simple rules for all your Dockerfiles: always use existing multi-platform images in your `FROM` instructions, and don’t include any OS-specific commands in `RUN` or `CMD` instructions. If you need some complex deployment or startup logic, you could build that into a simple utility app using the same language as your application, and then compile that in another stage of the build, or install a cross-platform shell (e.g., PowerShell) in your image.

All the official images on Docker Hub are multi-platform, so it’s a good idea to use those as your base images (or create your own golden base images using the official images). All the golden images for this book are multi-platform too, and if you’re looking for inspiration, you can check the `images` folder in the source code for a large suite of examples. As a rough guide, all the modern application platforms support multiple architectures (Go, Node.js, .NET, Java), and if you’re looking for a database, then Postgres is the best multi-platform option I’ve found.

There aren’t any managed build services out there that support the full range of architectures—Docker Build Cloud supports Intel and Arm and integrates with Buildx, but only for Linux. Azure DevOps and GitHub Actions provide Windows agents, and Linux on Intel and Arm64. You could also run a fairly cheap build farm in the cloud using Linux, Windows, and Arm VMs with Docker installed. The important thing is not to assume you’ll never need to support other platforms: follow best practices in your Dockerfiles to make multi-platform support easy, and know what steps you need to take to evolve your build pipeline if you do need to add support for additional platforms.

## 12.6 Lab

This chapter’s lab asks you to fix up a Dockerfile so that it can be used to produce multi-platform images. It’s the sort of thing you may come across if you have a Dockerfile that didn’t follow my best-practice suggestions. This Dockerfile is based on an image for a specific architecture, and it uses OS commands that aren’t portable. I’d like you to fix the Dockerfile in the lab folder for this chapter so that it can build an image targeted for Linux on Intel or Arm, and Windows on Intel. There are lots of ways to solve this; these are just a couple of hints for you:

  * Some Dockerfile instructions are cross-platform, whereas equivalent OS commands in a `RUN` instruction may not be. 
  * Some Windows commands are the same as Linux, and in the golden base image for the book, there are some aliases to make other Linux commands work in Windows. 



You’ll find my approach in the `Dockerfile.solution` file on GitHub for this chapter: <https://mng.bz/qRRJ>.


# 13 Replatforming the legacy: Packaging and running Windows apps in Docker

The cloud is where most companies want to run most of their workloads. Startups can build everything from scratch in Go or Rust, package all their components in Linux Docker images, and run them in containers on any cloud. Established companies don’t have it so easy—larger and older organizations have larger and older apps, and they’re likely to include products that only run on Windows. These legacy apps are often core to the business, and they want to move them to the cloud for the same scale and cost benefits that everyone wants. But rewriting them in the latest tech stack isn’t an option—we’re talking about the sort of apps that were designed a decade ago and now have millions of lines of code. How about just packaging them in Windows Docker images to run in containers like modern apps? It’s an option that some people don’t care for because it feels like a Band-Aid disguising the real problems of a legacy codebase. But I don’t agree—this is _replatforming_ legacy apps, so they can be built, deployed, and managed in the same way as modern apps. It gives you consistency in your tools and practices while also enabling you to run all your apps on the same platform in the cloud.

In this chapter, you’ll learn how to containerize legacy applications using a lot of the same techniques and skills you’ve already learned. The approach is the same for all legacy apps, but the focus here is on Windows containers, which have some quirks you’ll need to understand and which potentially offer the most benefit from a move to Docker.

## 13.1 How Windows containers are different

Microsoft saw the value in Docker very early on and supported the technology with teams dedicated to bringing containers to Windows and Windows containers (called _Windows Server Containers_) to the cloud. Windows Server Containers arrived with Windows Server 2016 and Windows 10 and have been included and improved with every subsequent release. There are two fundamental things to know about Windows Server Containers: they only run on Windows, and the version of Windows has to match between the container image and the machine running the container. This adds another variable to the multi-platform images you learned about in chapter 12: a platform match in Windows includes the CPU architecture, OS, and OS version.

##### Try it now

You can see this by comparing the manifests for a Windows container image stored on the Microsoft Container Registry (MCR) and a multi-platform Docker Hub image. You don’t need to be running Windows for this; you can see the manifest list using any platform:
    
    
    # print the manifest list for a Windows-only image on MCR:
    docker manifest inspect mcr.microsoft.com/dotnet/framework/sdk:4.8
    # and a multi-OS image on Docker Hub:
    docker manifest inspect golang:1.23

You’ll see multiple variants returned for each image. .NET Framework 4.8 is the last supported version of Microsoft’s Windows-only development platform, and all the image variants are for Windows and 64-bit Intel platforms. The Go image is multi-platform, including Linux and different versions of Windows. In my output in figure 13.1, I’ve formatted the results into a table; your output will have the same data, but in JSON.

##### Figure 13.1 Multi-platform image variants include an OS version for Windows images.

This isn’t an exciting way to start the chapter, but it’s important to know: a Docker image built for Windows Server 2025 won’t run on a Windows Server 2022 machine. That goes for the client OSs too: you can run a Windows Server 2022 image on Windows 11, but not on Windows 10. Table 13.1 shows the current Server and OS versions, which you’ll need to refer to if you try to run an incompatible image and get the `no matching manifest` error.

##### Table 13.1 Matching Windows versions between Docker images and OSs

Image OS Version  |  Server Version  |  Client Version   
---|---|---  
10.0.14393   
|  Windows Server 2016   
|  Windows 10 (version 1609)   
  
10.0.17763   
|  Windows Server 2019   
|  Windows 10 (update 1809)   
  
10.0.20348   
|  Windows Server 2022   
|  Windows 11 (version 23H2)   
  
10.0.26100   
|  Windows Server 2025   
|  Windows 11 (update 24H2)   
  
  
With that out of the way, there’s one other difference between Linux and Windows Docker images: the Windows images are big—very, very big. The Windows Server Core image is effectively all of Windows Server without the GUI. The goal is that you can use it to run almost any Windows app, so it has a large feature set to make it backward compatible for older apps—and the 2025 version of that image is 5.1 GB. If your app doesn’t need the full Windows Server feature set, you can use the Nano Sever image instead, which is still Windows but heavily pruned. Nano Server comes in at 480 MB, which is a big saving on the full Server Core image, but doesn’t compare well to Alpine Linux (8 MB).

## 13.2 Packaging applications with Windows Server Containers

The rest of this chapter is all hands-on with Windows Server Containers, so you’ll need access to a Windows machine to follow along (Windows 10 or 11 with Docker Desktop is the easiest option, or you can spin up a Windows Server virtual machine (VM) in the cloud and install Docker). The good news is that the core Docker concepts and workflows you’ve learned so far will operate the same with Windows: you specify your installation steps in a Dockerfile, build it into an image, push the image to a registry, and pull the image to run a container.

##### Try it now

Click on the whale icon for Docker Desktop, and select the option to switch to Windows containers. 

Then, run a web server in a Windows container using Microsoft’s Internet Information Services (IIS) image:
    
    
    # make sure you're using Windows containers :)
    docker info -f '{{.OperatingSystem}}'
    # this image supports several Windows versions:
    docker manifest inspect mcr.microsoft.com/windows/servercore/iis
    # run an IIS container – the Docker options are the same as Linux:
    docker run -d -p 8083:80 mcr.microsoft.com/windows/servercore/iis

That should all seem very familiar. Docker pulls the image and starts a container in the background, publishing a port from the container to your host machine. If you browse to `http://localhost:8083`, you should see the standard IIS welcome page (mine is shown in figure 13.2).

##### Figure 13.2 Running the Windows web server (IIS) in a Windows Server Container

Microsoft provides lots of base images on MCR—all built on top of the core Windows images—including IIS, .NET Framework, and ASP.NET. Your first task in packaging a Windows app to run in Docker is to choose the right base image, and the guidance is the same as for Linux: choose an image that provides you most of the dependencies you need (so you don’t have to maintain that yourself) with no additional dependencies (to keep the image size and attack surface area small). Then, you work out how to automate the deployment of your app, and script those steps in your Dockerfile.

Windows apps have some typical deployment approaches. Simple apps can be distributed in a ZIP file and deployed just by extracting the archive. Third-party apps or more complex enterprise apps may be delivered in a Microsoft Installer (MSI) file. MSIs can do a whole host of things: create files, edit the Windows Registry, and set configuration options. You usually deploy an MSI by double-clicking the file and following the install windows, but there is no GUI in containers, so you need to script that instead. Listing 13.1 shows how you can deploy an MSI-packaged app into a Windows container image.

##### Listing 13.1 Deploying a Windows MSI in a Dockerfile
    
    
    ARG WINDOWS_VERSION=ltsc2025
    FROM mcr.microsoft.com/windows/servercore:${WINDOWS_VERSION} AS installer
    SHELL ["pwsh", "-Command"]
    WORKDIR /install
    COPY hello-world.msi .
    RUN Start-Process msiexec.exe \
      -ArgumentList '/i', 'hello-world.msi', '/quiet', '/norestart' \
      -NoNewWindow -Wait
    
    FROM mcr.microsoft.com/windows/nanoserver:${WINDOWS_VERSION}
    WORKDIR /app
    COPY --from=installer \
     ["C:/Program Files (x86)/diamol-ch13/HelloDiamol.txt", \
     "/app/hello-world.txt"]
    CMD ["cmd", "/C", "type", "hello-world.txt"]

This short Dockerfile has a whole host of interesting features:

  * It’s a multi-stage build. The install stage uses Windows Server Core with a build argument to set the Windows version (which defaults to Windows Server 2025—`ltsc` stands for Long Term Support Channel). 
  * The installer switches to the latest PowerShell release, which is preinstalled in the Server Core image. 
  * An MSI file is copied in from the Docker context and then deployed using the `msiexec` tool (also preinstalled in Server Core). The installation uses PowerShell’s `Start-Process`, which runs the installer in the background and waits for it to complete. 
  * The final application image is based on Nano Server—using the same build argument as the installer stage to set the Windows version. 
  * A text file is copied from the installer into the final image, and the container command is set to use Windows command tools to print the contents of the file. 



You can build this image and run a container in the usual way—but beware that not all Microsoft images are multi-platform. So, if you get errors building the Windows 2025 version, you’ll need to set your version in the build argument.

##### Try it now

Build the container image from the installer, and run the container:
    
    
    cd ch13\exercises
    # build using the default 2025 OS version:
    docker build -t diamol/ch13-hello-world:2e .\hello-world
    # if you get a manifest error, set the version to be 2019 or 2022:
    # docker build --build-arg WINDOWS_VERSION='ltsc2022' `
    # -t diamol/ch13-hello-world:2e .\hello-world
    # run the container:
    docker run diamol/ch13-hello-world:2e

My output is in figure 13.3—you should see this is the standard Docker workflow, and the Dockerfile syntax and CLI commands are the same in Windows and Linux. You could use a `docker tag` command to create another tag with your ID and push this image to Docker Hub. You would see it in the web UI as a Windows AMD64 image.

Deploying an MSI is a valid option if you only have an installer package to work with. You can use a multi-stage build like this to deploy in one stage and then just copy the content you need into the final stage. How do you know the location where the files get installed with the MSI? You have to know, so hopefully there is documentation, or you can work with the development team. If not, then you can try deploying the MSI in a single stage, and you might get your app working, but then your Dockerfile isn’t a clear specification of the deployment; that logic is all still hidden in the MSI.

##### Figure 13.3 Packaging a Windows Docker image by installing an MSI in the Dockerfile

Filesystem deployment is better if that’s an option for your app. Images based on Windows Server Core have PowerShell installed, which gives you a huge and extensible toolset (you can run `Install-Module` commands in the Dockerfile), and the images also include familiar Linux tools such as `curl` and `tar`. That unlocks the typical workflow to download an archive, check the hash, and extract it. The range of images on MCR means you should be able to find a suitable base to keep your Dockerfile simple. Listing 13.2 shows a Dockerfile that deploys an ASP.NET application using the archive pattern.

##### Listing 13.2 Deploying an ASP.NET app from a file archive
    
    
    ARG WINDOWS_VERSION=ltsc2025
    FROM mcr.microsoft.com/windows/servercore:${WINDOWS_VERSION} AS installer
    SHELL ["pwsh", "-Command"]
    
    ARG ZIP_URL=https://is.gd/diamol_2e_ch13_app_zip
    ARG HASH_URL=https://is.gd/diamol_2e_ch13_app_hash
    
    WORKDIR /install
    
    RUN curl.exe -sSL -o app.zip $env:ZIP_URL; \
        curl.exe -sSL -o sha256 $env:HASH_URL; \
        $expectedHash = Get-Content -Path sha256; \
        $actualHash = (Get-FileHash -Algorithm SHA256 -Path app.zip).Hash; \
        if ($actualHash -ne $expectedHash) { exit 1 }; \
        Expand-Archive -Path app.zip -DestinationPath /app -Force
    
    FROM mcr.microsoft.com/dotnet/framework/aspnet:4.8
    
    ENV DIAMOL_KEY_1="first value" \
        DIAMOL_KEY_2="second value"
    
    WORKDIR /inetpub/wwwroot
    COPY --from=installer /app/ ./

I won’t go into detail on the PowerShell here (see _Learn PowerShell in a_ _Month of Lunches_ [Manning, 2022]), but, hopefully, you can see the pattern in action. The installer stage uses the base Windows Server Core image for downloading, and only the verified app contents are copied into the final stage, which is based on the ASP.NET image. You build the image and run the container in the usual way, but it’s worth looking at the processes running in your container when you get there.

##### Try it now

Build the ASP.NET image, run a container, and check the details:
    
    
    # build using the default 2025 OS version:
    docker build -t diamol/ch13-aspnet:2e .\aspnet
    # if you get a manifest error, set the version to be 2019 or 2022:
    # docker build --build-arg WINDOWS_VERSION='ltsc2022' `
    # -t diamol/ch13-aspnet:2e .\aspnet
    # run the container in the background:
    docker run --name aspnet -d -p 8084:80 diamol/ch13-aspnet:2e
    # look at the container processes:
    docker top aspnet

When you run this, you’ll notice a couple of other things about Windows Server Containers: they are much slower to start than Linux containers, and lots of processes are running in the container. You can see my output in figure 13.4.

##### Figure 13.4 Looking at the processes in a Windows container shows a lot more than in Linux.

A Windows Server Container might take 30 seconds to start and have 20 processes running in it, compared to a Linux container, which might start in a second and run a single process. Those two points are related. A Windows machine does a lot of work when it starts up, running core components as Windows Services. Those run in the background like Linux daemons—you’ll see them running as `svchost.exe` processes, and they manage OS-level work such as instrumentation and DNS lookups. The platform you use to run your application might also run as a Windows Service, which adds another challenge for containerizing Windows apps.

The ASP.NET app is hosted in the IIS web server inside the container. IIS runs as a Windows Service, so it’s managed by one of the `svchost.exe` processes you can see listed. When the first client makes a web request to IIS, it spins up a worker process—you’ll see this as `w3wp.exe`. But remember how Docker works: it expects to start a single process when it runs a container, and it will monitor that process, collecting log output and exiting the container if the process ends. The worker process actually runs the app, but Docker knows nothing about that or the IIS Windows Service, so Microsoft provides two utilities to bridge the gap:

  * _Service Monitor_ __ —You can use this console app as the entry point to your Docker containers. You tell the monitor which Windows Service you want to run and it starts and watches that service. If the service fails, then Service Monitor ends and your container exits. This follows the Docker pattern and means your container status correctly reflects your application status. 
  * _Log Monitor_ __ —You can configure this console app to read log entries from different sources (log files, the Event Log, and Event Tracing for Windows). It collects logs and prints them to standard output, so Docker sees them as container logs. Log Monitor wraps your application process, so you set Log Monitor as your entry point and tell it which process to start (which could be Service Monitor). 



These two tools work well together. Web applications on Windows typically use IIS and write logs to a file, and you don’t need to change the way they work to bring them to Docker. Figure 13.5 shows how you use the monitor tools to make apps like that behave like normal containers.

##### Figure 13.5 Monitor tools run a legacy application the way it was designed while making it behave like an ordinary container.

This approach means your old Windows applications work in the same way as all of your other containerized applications. That’s why replatforming legacy applications with Docker is so appealing—you bring them into a modern runtime without needing to rewrite them. You may not even have the source code for the application any more, but you can still package it into a Docker image using your existing deployment artifacts. If you do have the source code and the app is still under active development, that’s even better—you can use Docker for the build and adopt the same CI process you learned in chapter 11.

## 13.3 Building .NET Framework applications in containers

Microsoft released the .NET Framework in 2002 (when my own journey with C# started) and continued expanding the platform until 2022 with version 4.8. Now, it has evolved into the cross-platform .NET, but migrating a large legacy app from .NET Framework to .NET can be an expensive project. Replatforming that old app with Docker is a much faster way of bringing it into a modern cloud environment. Microsoft publishes SDK and runtime images for .NET Framework, so you can use multi-stage Dockerfiles to compile your applications from source.

For the rest of this chapter, we’ll Dockerize an old .NET Framework app called Nerd Dinner. The name will be familiar to C# developers of a certain age—it was an open source application written by some of the team at Microsoft to showcase new features of ASP.NET. The last code change was committed in 2013, so it’s a perfect candidate to see if we can bring it to Docker without having to change any code. Take a look at the Dockerfile in listing 13.3—it should be very familiar from the builder pattern we saw back in chapter 4.

##### Listing 13.3 Building and packaging a .NET Framework app in Docker
    
    
    FROM mcr.microsoft.com/dotnet/framework/sdk:4.8 AS build
    
    WORKDIR /src
    COPY src/NerdDinner.sln .
    COPY src/NerdDinner/NerdDinner.csproj ./NerdDinner/NerdDinner.csproj
    COPY src/NerdDinner/packages.config ./NerdDinner/packages.config
    RUN nuget restore
    
    COPY src/ .
    RUN msbuild NerdDinner/NerdDinner.csproj /p:DeployOnBuild=true
    
    FROM mcr.microsoft.com/dotnet/framework/aspnet:4.8
    
    WORKDIR /inetpub/wwwroot
    COPY --from=build /src/NerdDinner/obj/Debug/Package/PackageTmp/ ./
    COPY docker/web/override/ ./
    COPY docker/web/startup.ps1 /
    
    ENTRYPOINT ["powershell"]
    CMD ["-File", "/startup.ps1"]

The build stage uses the .NET Framework 4.8 SDK from MCR, and it copies in the C# project and NuGet package files, which list all the dependencies. Next, it runs a restore command to load those dependencies, copies in the rest of the source code, and compiles the application (the NuGet and MSBuild tools are already installed in the SDK image). The final image starts from Microsoft’s ASP.NET image, which has the .NET Framework installed, along with IIS. The output from the build stage is copied into the default IIS content directory. There’s a custom startup PowerShell script and a couple of configuration files copied in from the override folder so that we can have container-specific configuration and leave the default configuration files in the source folder for developers.

Now, it’s time to try Nerd Dinner. As you’re waiting for the images to download and the build to run through, remember this is source code that _hasn’t changed since 2013_. We’re building it with a technology that wasn’t even dreamed of when the last change was committed.

##### Try it now

Build the Nerd Dinner image from source—keep an eye on any interesting messages you see in the build process. Then, run a container from the image:
    
    
    # build – the Dockerfile is nested in the project folder:
    docker build -t diamol/ch13-nerd-dinner-web:2e `
     -f .\nerd-dinner\docker\web\Dockerfile .\nerd-dinner
    # run - publish the web port:
    docker run -d -p 8085:80 diamol/ch13-nerd-dinner-web:2e
    # browse to http:/_._ /localhost:8085

I find it amazing that this application builds, let alone runs. It’s proof that the Docker images have all you need, that the .NET Framework 4.8 tools are able to compile and package code written for older versions (4.5 in this case), and that replatforming with Docker can be straightforward. My output in figure 13.6 shows that the website loads without any errors, and the homepage looks pretty much as expected.

We’re not quite done yet, though. All applications have dependencies on other components, and with legacy apps, some of those might throw unexpected problems. You may have noticed some scary-looking warnings in the build output, saying that library packages have “known high severity vulnerabilities.” These are open source libraries over a decade old, so it’s not surprising that exploits have been found. Those have been patched in later versions, and if this was a real migration project, we would want to update the references and try to find a version that has no vulnerabilities but is still compatible with the app.

##### Figure 13.6 Welcome to 2013! Nerd Dinner is running in an ASP.NET Windows container.

External dependencies are also problematic with older apps because they could be using third-party services that are out of support or no longer exist. Nerd Dinner uses Bing Maps in the UI and a geolocation service called IPInfoDB. At the time of writing, both are still available. You can sign up for API keys and plug them into the Nerd Dinner configuration, and the app works as expected (see the README documentation in the Docker folder for details if you want to give it a try). Enterprise apps tend to be tightly coupled, so you may find the migration of one app to the cloud is complicated because it depends on other apps that are still running on-premise.

The final dependency for Nerd Dinner is a SQL database. The story here will probably be a happy one because databases tend to remain backward compatible for many releases, so the old app will work with the latest database version. And as we know from chapter 6, databases run just fine in containers.

## 13.4 Working with SQL Server database containers

SQL Server is Microsoft’s relational database, and it has a history almost as long as Windows itself. The latest versions are cross-platform with builds available for Linux and Windows that are 100% compatible. Microsoft publishes MCR images for SQL Server on Linux, but if you want to run your database in a Windows container, you need to package your own installation of SQL Server Express (free to use).
