I have an image published on Docker Hub with the latest SQL Server version installed on the Windows Server Core base image.

##### Try it now

Run SQL Server in a Windows container—if you have a SQL client installed you can connect on localhost using port 1433:
    
    
    # run SQL Server, exposing the client port:
    docker run --name sql -d -p 1433:1433 diamol/mssql:2e
    # print the password for the `sa` database user:
    docker exec sql powershell 'echo $env:MSSQL_SA_PASSWORD'
    # watch the logs to see when the database is ready to connect:
    docker logs -f sql
    # use Ctrl-C to exit when you've seen the logs

If you’ve ever downloaded and installed a fresh deployment of SQL Server, you’ll know it can take several hours—but with our container, we have a fully usable database in just a couple of minutes. You can use SQL Server Management Studio or a lightweight SQL client to connect using the localhost address and the `sa` user credentials. Figure 13.7 shows my database container running with a client connection using sqlectron.

##### Figure 13.7 Running a fresh instance of SQL Server takes seconds in a container.

An empty database container may be all you need for your application; for example, if the code uses an Object-Relational Mapping (ORM) tool such as Entity Framework, then the app will have its own logic to create the database structure. Nerd Dinner takes the other approach, which is that it expects the database to already exist; the structure is kept in the source code as a set of SQL scripts, but there’s no automated deployment. Wouldn’t it be nice if we could take the same approach to the database code as the application code and build and package it in Docker? SQL Server actually makes it easy to do that.

We’re not going to dig into this too deeply because the technology is specific to SQL Server, but the approach is a good pattern for any database, and the code is all there if you want to look closer. The goal is to have a Docker image that’s built with a versioned setup for your database. You can use this image in two ways: (1) run a container from it to use a local database for development or test environments, or (2) run a container to upgrade the schema from a previous container or on a remote database. Figure 13.8 shows how this looks, and it’s a great way of bringing a modern CI/CD approach to database development.

##### Figure 13.8 Packaging a SQL database in a Docker image lets you run a containerized database or use a container to upgrade an existing database server.

You can do this in SQL Server using a deployment artifact called a DACPAC. You build it from SQL scripts in the same way that you build a .NET project, and the output is a binary file (the DACPAC) that contains the database schema. That DACPAC can be deployed to an empty SQL Server instance to create a new, clean version of your database. Alternatively, you can point the deployment tool to an existing SQL Server database, and it will upgrade the current schema to the definition in the DACPAC. That means you can use fast, disposable containers in your nonproduction environments, and when you have a new release to deploy, you’ll do it on the production server using the same deployment artifact you’ve tested.

It all sounds magical, especially if you’re from an enterprise background where database changes are applied by a separate team and the schema isn’t even held in source control. But the implementation is very simple. Listing 13.4 shows the Dockerfile for the Nerd Dinner database image, which uses the same multi-stage build approach as the application Dockerfile.

##### Listing 13.4 Building and packaging a SQL Server database in Docker
    
    
    FROM diamol/dotnet-sdk:2e 64 AS build
    
    WORKDIR /src
    COPY src/NerdDinner.Database/ ./NerdDinner.Database
    COPY src/NerdDinner.Database.Build/ ./NerdDinner.Database.Build
    
    WORKDIR /src/NerdDinner.Database.Build
    RUN dotnet build NerdDinner.Database.Build.csproj -o /out
    
    FROM diamol/mssql:2e
    SHELL ["powershell", "-Command", "$ErrorActionPreference = 'Stop';"]
    
    WORKDIR /init
    COPY --from=build /out/NerdDinner.Database.Build.dacpac ./NerdDinner.dacpac
    COPY docker/db/initialize.ps1 ./
    
    CMD ./initialize.ps1 -sa_password $env:MSSQL_SA_PASSWORD -Verbose

The builder image here is the standard .NET SDK. The original Nerd Dinner code has a Visual Studio SQL project for the database definition. I haven’t touched that, but I’ve added an extra database build project that uses a community tool to build the old SQL project with the new .NET SDK. The build stage generates the DACPAC file, and the final stage starts from the SQL Server image and copies in the DACPAC and a startup PowerShell script. The deployment logic is in the `intialize.ps1` file, which uses the SQL Server tools to install or update the database from the DACPAC.

##### Try it now

Run a Nerd Dinner database container interactively from the image on Docker Hub, and you’ll see the output of the DACPAC deployment:
    
    
    # run the database, mapping a different client port:
    docker run -it -p 1434:1433 diamol/ch13-nerd-dinner-db:2e
    # use Ctrl-C to exit when you've seen the logs

You’ll see in the container logs that SQL Server gets started, a deployment SQL script is generated from the DACPAC, and then that script is applied, which creates all the database tables, indexes, and keys. Figure 13.9 shows my container running alongside a SQL query showing the list of tables in the database.

##### Figure 13.9 Running the database container deploys the DACPAC, which creates all the objects.

Now we have the application and the database packaged to run in Windows containers, which means we can model the application with Docker Compose. The Compose specification is the same for Windows and Linux containers, and we can use all the features we learned about in chapter 8 to describe the two components of the application, their configuration settings, and the relationship between them. Listing 13.5 shows the Compose file for Nerd Dinner.

##### Listing 13.5 Modeling Nerd Dinner with Docker Compose
    
    
    services:
      db:
        image: diamol/ch13-nerd-dinner-db:2e
        healthcheck:
          test: ["CMD", "powershell", "Test-Path", "/init.done"]
          start_period: 30s
        networks:
          - nd-net
    
      web:
        image: diamol/ch13-nerd-dinner-web:2e
        environment:
          ENV_DB_CONNECTION_STRING: ...
          ENV_IPINFODB_KEY: ...
          ENV_BINGMAPS_KEY: ...
        ports:
         - 8083:80
        depends_on:
          db:
            condition: service_healthy
        networks:
          - nd-net
    
    networks:
      nd-net:

There’s only one feature in this Compose specification that we haven’t already covered, and it’s perfect for legacy apps like this. It can take a minute or two before the database is online, and we don’t want the web container trying to connect before that. The dependency is modeled with a `condition` value, which links the web container to the database container’s health check. The web container won’t start until the database container has started and the container is flagged as healthy. The database health check is very simple—it just looks for the existence of a text file, which the initialize script creates when the DACPAC deployment is done: as soon as the database is deployed, the file is written, and then the next health check will succeed and the web container is created.

##### Try it now

Let’s run Nerd Dinner! You’ll need to register for the third-party services and add your keys to the Compose file if you want the full experience. The rest all happens in Docker:
    
    
    # run the application and database containers:
    docker compose -f .\nerd-dinner\docker\docker-compose.yml up -d

You’ll see the dependency mapping clearly in the output from the Compose command—the web container will be in the status waiting while the database container is starting up, and when the database container status switches to healthy, then the web container will start. Browse to `http://localhost:8086` to use the legacy Windows app with all of its features—you can register an account, create a dinner, and see it listed. Figure 13.10 shows this on my Windows desktop machine.

I’ll say it one last time: this is a Windows app written in an obsolete language version, which no one has touched since 2013. Now it’s running in containers on the latest versions of the .NET Framework, Windows Server, and SQL Server. It was built using Docker without any changes to source code, and the build and deployment processes are the same as any other app you want to run in a modern container platform. The only difference with running this app compared to any others in the book so far is that you need to have a Windows machine to host it.

##### Figure 13.10 The full Nerd Dinner experience, running in the latest Windows Server Containers

## 13.5 Understanding where Windows containers fit in your landscape

Containers and the cloud have pulled lots of Windows applications, developers, and administrators into the world of Linux. You can migrate lots of old .NET Framework apps to the latest .NET without a complex rewrite project—and if you can, you should. Cross-platform .NET apps can be built by developers using Windows laptops and run in Linux containers in the cloud, which are smaller, faster, and cheaper than Windows Server Containers. However, not every app can be easily migrated, and it’s hard to convince stakeholders of the benefits of rewriting an app if it will take 18 months and deliver the exact same functionality. In those cases, the replatforming approach of moving the app to Windows containers is an attractive option.

Large organizations will likely have a catalog of apps they support, and in a migration program, you can start by plotting out the application landscape and classifying everything in terms of the goals and business benefit of the migration. Gartner research came up with a set of categories in the “5 Rs” of cloud migration. It’s a useful framework that people have extended or redefined, and this is my take on the classifications:

  * _Retire_ __ —Apps that are expected to be decommissioned can justifiably be left as is, running on their VMs in the datacenter if they’re likely to be shut down or replaced in the near future. They still need to be secured, though, so if they’re using dependencies that have known vulnerabilities, they might need upgrading to another category. 
  * _Rehost_ __ —This is the classic lift-and-shift, where apps don’t justify much investment and can be run in the cloud using a close approximation of the current infrastructure, provisioning VMs in the cloud and deploying apps in the same way you currently do—whether that’s automated or manual. 
  * _Replatform_ __ —This is the approach we walked through in this chapter with Nerd Dinner. Don’t change the application, but build and model it to run on a different platform. Docker is a great option because every cloud has a container service, and you can run the app locally in containers too. This is good for very complex apps, or apps that don’t have a lot of ongoing development and wouldn’t qualify for a rewrite. It’s a low-investment approach with high returns, moving your app to the cloud in a modern platform with consistent tools and practices. 
  * _Refactor_ __ —Evolve the application to use a more modern architecture. This is a nice extension of replatforming, well suited to apps that are more actively developed and have a longer anticipated lifespan. Container platforms are the ideal way to break down a monolithic application—features can be internally broken off and moved to new components running in separate containers. You can do this gradually and focus on business value, slicing off features to improve speed, quality, or delivery cadence. 
  * _Rewrite_ __ —Throw it all away and start again, using whatever language, platform, and frameworks are the flavor of the day, which is often very tempting for the development team but rarely the best approach. Apps are always more complex than you realize, and there are huge risks in a rewrite: features get missed, logic gets implemented differently, and service contracts are changed. 



These are listed in ascending order of complexity for the migration itself, but that’s not the whole story. Rehosting an app may get it to the cloud faster than replatforming, but the end result could be an app that still has manual deployment steps, no monitoring, and no way to move it to another cloud without extra effort. Replatforming to containers is often a good first step, and it leads on to refactoring based on business value. Rewrites might seem attractive, but be sure you have good justification for them, and reconsider if you don’t have very thorough automated test suites.

## 13.6 Lab

Here’s a nice replatforming lab for you—it’s going to take a bit of investigation, but that’s always the way for legacy app migration. An MSI installer file in the lab directory for this chapter deploys an ASP.NET web application. Your goal is to get this app running correctly in Docker with Windows Server Containers. You’ll need to write a Dockerfile for the web app and a Compose specification to run the app and the database. Here are a few things you’ll need to know:

  * The app connects to a SQL Server database. An empty database is fine, but the app configuration is fixed in the MSI. It expects to find a database at the DNS address `SIGNUP-DB-DEV01`, and it uses the `sa` user account with the password `DockerCon!!!`. 
  * It’s a web application that will run with the IIS Windows Service (called `w3svc`), but it needs .NET Framework 4.8 installed. 
  * The app writes logs to a file at `C:\logs\SignUp.log`. We want those published as container logs, so you’ll need to install the Log Monitor tool as part of your deployment. There’s a `LogMonitorConfig.json` in the lab folder, which is correctly configured, and you’ll need to copy that into the Docker image and set the correct entry point. 
  * Deploying the MSI is the easiest part—it doesn’t need any configuration settings passed in; you can just install it with the `msiexec` tool. 
  * When you have everything running, you should be able to browse to the app at `http://localhost:8088/signup` and see a newsletter sign-up page. If you click the Sign Up button and fill in the details, you should see logs in the web container, and the data will be stored in the SQL Server container. 



As always, there are solution files in the lab folder, and a bit more detail in the README file: <https://mng.bz/Wwwg>.


# 14 Containers in the cloud with Microsoft Azure and Google Cloud

Docker’s earliest slogan was “Build, ship, and run any app, anywhere.” We’ve covered the build, ship, and run workflow with lots of different apps in this book, but so far we’ve been running containers locally on our machines. It’s time to look at the _anywhere_ part of that original promise, and for that, we’ll look at running apps using managed container services in the cloud. These are simplified container platforms that let you run apps in containers without having to provision virtual machines (VMs) or install Docker or manage networks, storage, or anything else. Each cloud has a slightly different approach, and each has its own modeling languages and tools, so you won’t be using the Docker or Compose command lines. But the learning curve is shallow, given everything you already know, and you’ll gain access to a huge amount of functionality for very little effort.

In this chapter, we’ll use some of the most attractive cloud services: Azure Container Instances, Azure Container Apps, and Google Cloud Run. Amazon Web Services (AWS) has a similar service called Fargate, which runs on the Elastic Container Service (ECS), but it’s much more complex to use, so I’ll leave you to explore that if you’re an AWS user. You’ll need to sign up to both Azure and Google Cloud to follow the examples in this chapter, but both clouds offer free credits for new accounts, which will be more than enough to cover the exercises, and you’ll truly benefit from getting hands-on experience with containers in different clouds.

## 14.1 Where managed container services fit in

Although, in the last chapter, I said that every cloud has a container service, the major clouds actually have _multiple_ container services. Microsoft, Google, and Amazon have coalesced around Kubernetes as their flagship container offering, but lots of use cases don’t require the full feature set and overhead of Kubernetes, so they also offer simpler alternatives. The main goal for these managed container services is to provide a production deployment workflow that is very similar to the developer workflow with Docker: you run a command to start a container, passing it the image to use and any configuration settings, and that’s it. This is much better than using plain Docker on a VM in the cloud. You don’t have to provision or maintain any infrastructure, and you get reliability out of the box as the service will keep your container available in the event of outages.

So, what are the use cases that fit these services? The key attribute is simplicity: apps that don’t have a distributed architecture with many dependent services, apps with modest scaling requirements, and apps that don’t need five nines of reliability and detailed monitoring. That doesn’t mean managed container services are only useful for basic to-do applications—lots of apps will fall into the simple category, if the product owners are honest about their nonfunctional requirements. In a cloud migration program, you’ll need to identify which apps need the feature set of a full container platform and which can use the simpler managed services. You’re likely to move to the cloud more quickly and have a lower total cost of ownership for the apps you run in a managed container service.

##### Try it now

Let’s see how easy it is to use Azure Container Instances (ACI). You’ll need an Azure subscription and the Azure command-line interface (CLI) installed. The `az container` commands manage containers; you can add `--help` to any command to print the help text:
    
    
    # log in to your Azure subscription:
    az login
    # create a Resource Group - a logical group for Azure services:
    az group create --location eastus --name diamol-ch14
    # create a container instance to run a simple Hello World app:
    az container create -g diamol-ch14 --name hello `
     --image ghcr.io/diamol2e/ch14-hello-cloud `
     --os-type Linux --cpu 0.1 --memory 0.1
    # print the container logs:
    az container logs -n hello -g diamol-ch14

That’s all you need to do. It takes a few seconds for the `az container create` command to return, and by then, the container is running. Azure has found and reserved some compute capacity (we only requested a tiny amount), pulled the image, and started the container. You can use the Azure CLI to interact with the container and print the logs. You can also browse to your ACI using the Azure Portal, and there you can see the details of the running container, the events in its life cycle, and another view of the logs. My output is shown in figure 14.1.

##### Figure 14.1 Running a simple container with Azure Container Instances

The `container create` command needs a bit more information than `docker run`—the amount of compute to use and the OS of the container image are required—but that was still very simple. This could be a good fit for deploying test environments in a continuous integration/continuous deployment (CI/CD) process. It’s so fast and cheap to run that you could build and deploy from every push to every branch in source code, giving developers their own smoke test setup available within a minute of pushing changes. Of course, it would help if they could access that environment, which in the case of a web application or API server is easily done. ACI supports publishing ports and can provision a domain name system (DNS) route to your container, making it available on the public internet.

##### Try it now

You can use the same `container create` command with some extra arguments to publish an HTTP port and have Azure route traffic into the container from a public URL. You need to choose a globally unique DNS prefix for the container, which will be published to an Azure domain:
    
    
    # choose a good DNS name and put in a variable - with PowerShell:
    $DNS_PREFIX='<unique-name>'
    # or Linux shell:
    DNS_PREFIX='<unique-name>'
    __# now create the container using the DNS prefix you set:
    az container create -g diamol-ch14 --name hello-web `
     --image ghcr.io/diamol2e/ch14-hello-cloud-web `
     --ports 80 --dns-name-label $DNS_PREFIX `
     --os-type Linux --cpu 0.5 --memory 0.5
    # query ACI to print the full domain name which routes to the container:
    az container show -g diamol-ch14 -n hello-web --query 'ipAddress.fqdn'

The output of the `container show` command will be the fully qualified domain name of your instance of this simple web application. You can browse to the URL to see a basic static web app (this uses the Apache web server). There’s no SSL in this setup—that’s not a feature of ACI out of the box. Although there are options to secure access, this is just plain HTTP. My output is shown in figure 14.2.

##### Figure 14.2 Publishing a web container with a public DNS name with ACI

Most of the container options you use with Docker are also available in the managed container services. You can set environment variables for configuration, attach storage, connect to a network, and more. Cloud providers typically support Linux and Windows Server containers, and support for Arm processors is either available or on its way. But the true power of cloud container services is that they integrate easily with the rest of the platform. You might have a web application that uses a SQL database, message queue, and REST API. Developers can be working on different frontend components, but they could all use a shared set of backend services. In that case, your test architecture could look like figure 14.3.

##### Figure 14.3 Hosting multiple test environments with shared services and ACI containers

The services here are specific to Azure, but the concepts are the same for other clouds: your backend is isolated in a virtual network that acts as a security boundary. The database and message queue aren’t accessible on the public internet, but they can be reached by the container instances that are connected to the virtual network. There’s a shared API container, and each developer has their own web container built from their branch of the code. It’s a very effective way to work and is extremely easy to automate—the container’s DNS name can use the branch name from the source code, so you don’t need any manual intervention when new branches are created. You could have another pipeline to remove containers, triggered when branches get deleted.

Let’s look at one more simple example before we move on. Successful cloud migration projects are almost always followed by a cost optimization project, usually about a month afterward when the first cloud bill rolls in. Individual cloud services tend to be attractively priced, but when you’re running tens or hundreds of them, the costs quickly mount up. With everything you deploy to the cloud, you need to know the deployment options that affect the cost. For container services, the main cost is the size of compute reserved for your container.

##### Try it now

We’ve provisioned ACI containers with small amounts of compute. Now we’ll run two instances of the same application—a web app that calculates pi—with different amounts of compute. We’ll compare them to see how they perform:
    
    
    # choose a different DNS name and put in a variable - with PowerShell:
    $DNS_PREFIX='unique-name>'
    # or Linux shell:
    DNS_PREFIX='<unique-name>'
    # create a container with 0.1 CPU cores:
    az container create -g diamol-ch14 --name pi-1 `
     --image ghcr.io/diamol2e/ch14-pi-web `
     --ports 80 --dns-name-label "${DNS_PREFIX}-1" `
     --os-type Linux --cpu 0.1 --memory 0.1 --query 'ipAddress.fqdn'
    # and one from the same image with 3.5 CPU cores:
    az container create -g diamol-ch14 --name pi-2 `
     --image ghcr.io/diamol2e/ch14-pi-web `
     --ports 80 --dns-name-label "${DNS_PREFIX}-2" `
     --os-type Linux --cpu 3.5 --memory 1 --query 'ipAddress.fqdn'
    # browse to each of the two domains and refresh quickly a few times

My output is shown in figure 14.4, where I put both sites under stress with repeated refreshes. The first container with minimal CPU is significantly slower at calculating pi than the second one. This is a compute-intensive workload that shows how you need to find the balance between performance and cost. You only pay for containers while they’re running, but it’s all too common for teams to spin up a few test environments and leave them running 24x7, even though they’re only used a few hours a day. You should make sure you know which parameters affect cost and make sure you right-size your deployments. Then, think about how you could provision environments on demand if they’re not needed all the time.

##### Figure 14.4 Compute size in ACI directly affects application performance—and your Azure bill.

You can deploy straightforward apps to ACI without learning much more than we’ve covered in this section—the service really is that easy to use. But one thing you won’t be doing is publishing images to a public registry so you can run your containers in the cloud. That would be no way to treat your company’s intellectual property.

## 14.2 Private registries vs. Docker Hub

Managed container services let you use the developer workflow for your production deployments, but there’s an extra element to the “ship” and “run” parts of the workflow: you’ll want to use an image registry hosted in the cloud too. You know from chapter 5 that registries are standardized and that you can run your own in a Docker container. Cloud providers also have container registry services, so you can push your application images to a private registry in the cloud that is physically close to your containers. This gives you fast image pulls and the ability to secure the registry so it’s accessible only to your services.

AWS has the Elastic Container Registry (ECR), Google has the Artifact Registry, and Microsoft has the Azure Container Registry (ACR). Fundamentally, they all behave like Docker Hub—you can authenticate, push, and pull images using the Docker command line. But they all have additional features as well, such as automated security scanning and access control integrated with the cloud’s identity provider. Each cloud also has a different take on its registry service—Artifact Registry is designed to store all types of artifacts, not just container images. ECR integrates with the AWS Marketplace for vendor applications. ACR is designed to support the application life cycle, with automated tasks to build your images and trigger events such as a change to the base image to rebuild them. We’ll get a flavor of ACR in this section.

##### Try it now

ACR instances get a DNS entry with an Azure domain—when you create one, you need to supply a globally unique name. Create a registry in your Resource Group, and log in with the Azure command line:
    
    
    # choose a DNS name and put in a variable - with PowerShell:
    $ACR_NAME='<unique-name>'
    # or Linux shell:
    ACR_NAME='<unique-name>'
    # now create the registry using the cheapest tier:
    az acr create -n $ACR_NAME -g diamol-ch14 --sku Basic
    # login to ACR - this configures authentication in the Docker CLI:
    az acr login -n $ACR_NAME

This is your private registry with your custom subdomain. The `login` command sets up a time-limited token behind the scenes, so it expires after a while, and you’ll need to log in again if you get authentication errors later. My output is shown in figure 14.5. This is all you need to do, so now you can tag images with your ACR domain name and push them to Azure.

##### Figure 14.5 Creating and authenticating to ACR using the Azure command line

Typically, you’ll create multiple instances of ACR in your subscription—you might have one registry to store images for nonproduction environments and another one for your production images. Your CI/CD pipeline will build and push images to the nonproduction repository, and then deploy from there to your test environments. When you have a release candidate ready, a second pipeline pushes the tested image to the production registry and deploys it to a pre-production staging environment. Using separate registries means you can secure them independently and have different service tiers—saving the more expensive options for production.

##### Try it now

We have just one ACR, but you can see how images get copied from another registry using an image from Docker Hub:
    
    
    # import an image from Docker Hub using the Azure tools:
    az acr import -n $ACR_NAME --source docker.io/diamol/apache:2e `
     -t diamol/apache:2e
    # check the image manifest:
    docker manifest inspect "${ACR_NAME}.azurecr.io/diamol/apache:2e"
    # and pull the image from ACR:
    docker pull "${ACR_NAME}.azurecr.io/diamol/apache:2e"

The `az` commands in this section may be new to you, but the Docker commands will be very familiar. You now have a repository in your own private image registry where you can securely push and pull any number of images using different tags. When you run this, you’ll see that the source repository on Docker Hub is a multi-platform image. ACR has multi-platform support, and the `import` command copies across all the image variants. My manifest list from ACR is shown in figure 14.6.

##### Figure 14.6 Loading an image from Docker Hub into ACR using the Azure command line

Remembering that cloud services cost money, you need to know that the pricing for ACR is based on the service tier you select—more features cost more—and also on the amount of storage you use and the amount of data you transfer. It’s tempting to build and push images from every commit, and you can definitely do that. But you’ll want an automated clean-up process to remove unused images, so you’ll need to think about the tags you use and how you identify which builds are for keeps. It’s also a good idea not to make yourself dependent on features that aren’t universally available. ACR doesn’t have the same strict two-part repository naming scheme that Docker Hub has (`owner/app`), so you could decide to use a custom naming system. If you do this, you might not be able to move to another registry later without changing all the names. Microsoft does this themselves with their .NET Framework images—you could pull `mcr.microsoft .com/dotnet/framework/sdk:4.8`, tag it to replace the Microsoft Container Registry (MCR) domain with your ACR domain, and successfully push. But you couldn’t push the image to Docker Hub because it has three parts in the repository name.

Now that you have ACR set up, we can go back to ACI and see how to deploy from a private registry. We’ll also look at another option for getting images into ACR. Cloud providers like to make it easy to consume their services because the easier it is, the more likely you are to continue using them. That means there’s often overlap between tools that provide different ways of doing the same thing. For example, you can build images and push them to ACR using the Docker command line, or you can use the automation features of ACR to build your image in Azure. The `acr build` command works a lot like `docker build`—it compresses the local folder containing your Dockerfile and source code and sends it to a Docker Engine to build the image. With ACR, that build happens using Azure compute, and the resulting image is stored in your registry.

##### Try it now

Use ACR to build your own version of the simple website image for the chapter. This is based on Alpine Linux, so the base image is small. See how long the build with ACR takes compared to your local Docker machine:
    
    
    cd ch14/exercises/hello-cloud-web
    # build locally:
    docker build -t diamol/ch14-web --quiet .
    # and build the same image in ACR:
    az acr build --image diamol/ch14-web --registry ${ACR_NAME} .
    # you may get a TasksOperationsNotAllowed error if you're using
    # free credits - if you do just import the image instead:
    az acr import -n $ACR_NAME --source `
    ghcr.io/diamol2e/ch14-hello-cloud-web -t diamol/ch14-web
    # print the tag list for the new image repository:
    az acr repository show-tags -n ${ACR_NAME} --repository diamol/ch14-web

The ACR command runs a task that offloads the work to Azure, and the build doesn’t take much longer than the local Docker build. You get a clean build agent for each ACR task, so the build will take longer if you have a large base image that will need to be pulled every time, as each agent starts with a fresh image cache. In a CI/CD pipeline, that might not be a problem, especially in a project with lots of churn where you would benefit from building images from different branches in parallel. Like other situations where the cloud provider offers alternative tools and processes, you need to decide if the simplicity is worth veering away from the normal toolset. You see my output in figure 14.7 where I now have an image ready to use in my private registry, built in Azure without even needing Docker on my machine.

##### Figure 14.7 Using an ACR task to build and push an image in Azure from a local source folder

You can run containers in ACI using images from any registry. We’ve already used public images on GitHub Container Registry that didn’t require authentication, but to use our private image, we’ll need to supply credentials. There are lots of options for doing this; the most secure integrate with the identity service of the cloud so you don’t need to pass around any sensitive data in your deployment scripts. Those options take a bit more setup and they’re specific to the cloud provider, so we’ll stick with the simplest option, which will also show you how to log in to ACR with a username and password.

##### Try it now

Create a token in ACR to use for authentication, which gives you a username and password, and use them to run an ACI container from your image in ACR:
    
    
    # generate an ACR token and print the password:
    az acr token create -n aci-reader -r $ACR_NAME `
     --repository diamol/ch14-web content/read metadata/read `
     --query 'credentials.passwords[0].value'
    # think of a new unique DNS prefix and store it - in PowerShell:
    $DNS_PREFIX='<new-name>'
    # OR Linux shell:
    DNS_PREFIX='<new-name>'
    # and run an ACI image - the username is your ACR token name
    # and you will need to insert your token password:
    az container create -g diamol-ch14 --name web-2 `
     --image "${ACR_NAME}.azurecr.io/diamol/ch14-web" `
     --ports 80 --dns-name-label ${DNS_PREFIX} `
     --os-type Linux --cpu 0.2 --memory 0.2 `
     --registry-login-server "${ACR_NAME}.azurecr.io" `
     --registry-username aci-reader --registry-password '<token-password>'
    # print the full domain:
    az container show -g diamol-ch14 --name web-2 --query 'ipAddress.fqdn'
    # now you can browse to the website

You have to specify a lot of details in the ACI `create` command because the Azure CLI doesn’t assume many defaults. This is what we’ve set—you only need to include the registry information if the image isn’t publicly accessible:

  * `-g` (or `--group`)—The resource group 
  * `--name`—Name of the ACI 
  * `--image`—Docker image to run the container from 
  * `--ports`—Lists the port(s) to publish 
  * `--dns-name-label`—Prefix to use for the container’s public DNS entry 
  * `--os-type`—The OS (Linux or Windows) 
  * `--cpu` and `--memory`—Specify the compute resources to allocate 
  * `--registry-login-server`—Full domain name of the registry 
  * `--registry-username`—Username for authentication 
  * `--registry-password`—The password 



My output is shown in figure 14.8. It’s the same basic website, but the workflow is the focus here—the container is running from a secure image in ACR, which I built from my local source code. Don’t expect to use my token or my ACR domain—both will be long gone by the time you’re reading this.

##### Figure 14.8 Running a container in ACI from a secure image we built in ACR

We’re done with ACR and ACI now. You can delete the resource group, which deletes all the resources inside it—they will be gone forever.

##### Try it now

You’re being charged (or using up your free credits) for ACR and ACI. Unless you want to keep experimenting, delete the Resource Group:
    
    
    # the --yes flag stops the CLI asking for confirmation:
    az group delete --name diamol-ch14 --yes

A lot of the exercises in this section have had boilerplate setup work, such as creating the Azure resource group, creating the ACR, and generating tokens. Those won’t be part of your everyday workflow if you’re using Azure—you’ll do them once at the start of the project and when you create a new environment, and then every few months to regenerate tokens. The day-to-day workflow might be as simple as `az acr build` and `az container create`, which gets your local cut of the code running in Azure ready to be tested. That could be a helpful way to get simple applications up in the cloud, but when you get to a more complex app, there’s a pretty steep learning curve from a managed container service to Kubernetes. When you need more than one container but you don’t need the full weight of Kubernetes, there are cloud services that sit in between the two and accelerate deployment by supporting the Docker Compose format.

## 14.3 Azure Container Apps

Docker Compose was designed as a generic specification to model an application that runs on containers. You’ve learned how to use Compose with Docker to run containers and build images locally, and the same specification can be used with some of the cloud container services. AWS supports deployment to ECS from Compose files using the `ecs-cli` tool, and Azure has a dedicated service called Azure Container Apps (ACA) that has native Compose support (Google Cloud doesn’t have a container service with Compose support). ACA is a fully fledged platform that supports advanced features such as auto-scale and scale to zero, deployment to staging slots, and close integration with ACR. It’s worth exploring ACA because it adds an important component to your toolkit—if you’re planning on using Azure, you have a suite of container services you can map to your application landscape: ACI for the simplest apps, ACA for typical apps that you can model in Compose, and Azure Kubernetes Service (AKS) for the most complex apps which need full configuration options.

Listing 14.1 shows a Compose file for the random number app we’ve used already in the book. It has two containers—a web app and a REST API—and both are available in public Docker images. There’s nothing unusual in the specifications: the containers are attached to the same network, each publishes a port, and the web app uses an environment variable to set the URL for accessing the API.

##### Listing 14.1 A simple Docker Compose specification
    
    
    services:
      numbers-api:
        image: ghcr.io/diamol2e/ch08-numbers-api:2e
        ports:
          - "8081:8080"
        networks:
          - app-net
    
      numbers-web:
        image: ghcr.io/diamol2e/ch08-numbers-web:2e
        environment:
          - RngApi__Url=http:/./numbers-api:8080/rng
        depends_on:
          - numbers-api
        ports:
          - "8080:8080"
        networks:
          - app-net
    
    networks:
      app-net: {}

You can use this Compose file to run the application with Docker on your machine or on a VM in the cloud. But ACA is a better option than a VM because it will provision all the infrastructure for you and wire up additional features for operations and monitoring—all from the exact same Compose file.

##### Try it now

Run the random numbers app in ACA using the Docker Compose file. We’ll use a new resource group to make it easier to clean up later, but first you need to make sure your Azure subscription and CLI are set up for ACA:
    
    
    # ACA is a relatively new service - this sets it up:
    az extension add --name containerapp --upgrade
    az provider register -n Microsoft.App --wait
    az provider register -n Microsoft.OperationalInsights --wait
    # create a new Resource Group (any location will do):
    az group create --location eastus --name diamol-ch14-aca
    # switch to the exercise directory:
    cd ch14/exercises/numbers
    # deploy to ACA - this will take a while, be sure to watch the logs:
    az containerapp compose create -g diamol-ch14-aca `
     --environment numbers -f docker-compose.yml
    # now list your container apps to show the URLs:
    az containerapp list -g diamol-ch14-aca -o table
    # test the API:
    # curl https://<number-api-domain>/rng
    # browse to https://<numbers-web-domain> to test the website

Lots of interesting things happen when you follow this exercise. You’ll see Azure resources being created that you didn’t ask for, as well as warnings about parts of the Compose specifications that get skipped in the translation to the ACA architecture. You can ignore all that. The extra resources help to make this a production-grade deployment, with monitoring and centralized log collection all set up for you. The parts of the Compose specifications that don’t translate are the parts that don’t make sense in ACA—such as Docker networks (although networking is set up between ACA in the same ACA environment). My output is shown in figure 14.9; you can see the random number API working, but when you try to use the web application, you’ll get an error.

##### Figure 14.9 Running a distributed application modeled in Compose using Azure Container Apps

The broken web app is a typical problem with container communication because services can’t magically discover each other—they need an address to use. The web component reads the address for the API from an environment variable. The value for that variable is set in the Compose file, and it works with Docker, where the API container can be reached from its name inside the Docker network (`numbers-api`). That’s not the address of the API in Azure now, so we need to update the configuration of the web container app.

##### Try it now

Print the current configuration settings for the web container, and then update the API address to see how ACA implements the change:
    
    
    # print the current environment variables:
    az containerapp show -g diamol-ch14-aca -n numbers-web `
     --query 'properties.template.containers[0].env'
    # update the API address - make sure you use your ACA domain:
    az containerapp update -g diamol-ch14-aca -n numbers-web `
     --set-env-vars 'RngApi__Url=https:/./numbers-api.<your-aca-domain>/rng'
    # ACA implements the change by creating a new revision:
    az containerapp revision list -g diamol-ch14-aca -n numbers-web -o table
    # when the new revision is healthy, try your web URL again

That gets things working. This is the approach you take if you want all the containers in your application to have a public address—in this case, clients can use the website or the API directly. ACA generate a DNS subdomain for the environment, and each container gets an address in that domain. You’ll also see that the URLs use HTTPS, which we get set up for free in ACA, mapping the container port specified in the Compose file to the standard port 443. Figure 14.10 shows my random number app running in ACA and giving full value.

There’s a lot more to ACA, but we’ll finish our look at this option with two more features: building from source code as part of the deployment and supporting a distributed architecture where not all the components should be publicly accessible. For this, we’ll go back to the good old to-do list application, which we’ve modeled out in Compose for this exercise. There are two components: the web app, which should be public, and the backend database, which should be private. Listing 14.2 shows the specification of the web component.

##### Listing 14.2 The to-do web app, configured in Compose
    
    
      todo-web:
        image: ch14-todo-web
        ports:
          - "8082:8080"
        environment:
          - Database__Provider=Postgres
        build:
          context: .
        depends_on:
          - todo-db
        networks:
          - app-net
        volumes:
          - ./config/secrets.json:/app/config/secrets.json

##### Figure 14.10 Updating app configuration settings in ACA to enable communication between containers

There’s nothing there that you haven’t seen before. The key things to notice are the `build` section, which tells Compose where to find the build context (the folder containing the Dockerfile and the source code for the app), and the `volumes` mount, which loads a JSON file into the application configuration folder. You can run `docker compose build` and then `docker compose up` from the exercise folder to see the app running locally. We can use the same Compose file to build and run with ACA.

##### Try it now

Remove the numbers app, and deploy the to-do app from the source code. This time, ACA will also provision an ACR for the image it builds:
    
    
    # start by deleting the previous apps and environment:
    az containerapp delete -g diamol-ch14-aca -n numbers-web --yes --no-wait
    az containerapp delete -g diamol-ch14-aca -n numbers-api --yes --no-wait
    az containerapp env delete -g diamol-ch14-aca -n numbers --yes --no-wait
    # switch to the source folder:
    cd ch14/exercises/todo-list
    # deploy the app - the CLI sees the build spec in the Compose file:
    az containerapp compose create -g diamol-ch14-aca --environment todo
    # if your app fails to build on free credits, use my image instead:
    # az containerapp compose create -g diamol-ch14-aca --environment todo `
     -f docker-compose-public.yml
    # list the container app URLs
    az containerapp list -g diamol-ch14-aca -o table
    # browse to the web domain - the app doesn't work

Even more happens behind the scenes when you create this container app. The build section in the Compose file triggers a new workflow—an ACR is provisioned, and an ACR task is used to build the container image. Another Log Analytics Workspace is created for monitoring, along with a new ACA environment where the two container apps are deployed. Both the database and the web app have public DNS entries, but when you browse to the website, you’ll just see an empty page, as shown in figure 14.11.

Both parts of the application are running, but it’s the same communication problem. In this case, there are two problems to fix. The database is publicly accessible, but the routing is configured for HTTPS traffic by default, which doesn’t work for the database protocol because it expects TCP traffic on a specific port. We need to update those network settings and also update the connection details the web container uses to find the database. Both can be configured using ACA features:

  * _Ingress_ —This is the network routing component. A container app can be configured for internal access, which means it’s only available to other containers in the same ACA environment. Internal routing can use TCP over any port. 
  * _Application configuration_ —This can be set as values, or _secrets,_ in the container app and presented to the container as environment variables or file mounts. Secrets are perfect for confidential details, such as the username and password for a database connection. 



##### Figure 14.11 Building and deploying to Azure Container Apps from source code

##### Try it now

All the changes we need to make can be done with the Azure CLI, without needing to redeploy the application. The final step to update the web app creates a new revision, and when that rolls out, the app will be working:
    
    
    # print the current network routing for the database:
    az containerapp ingress show -g diamol-ch14-aca -n todo-db -o table
    # make the database route private:
    az containerapp ingress update -g diamol-ch14-aca -n todo-db `
     --type internal --transport tcp --exposed-port 5432 `
     --target-port 5432 -o table
    __# create a secret in the web app with the database details:
    az containerapp secret set -g diamol-ch14-aca -n todo-web `
     --secrets 'secrets-json="{ \"ConnectionStrings\": { \"ToDoDb\": `
     \"Server=todo-db;Database=todo; `
     User Id=postgres;Password=postgres;\" }}"'
    # and mount the secret as a volume in the web container:
    az containerapp update -g diamol-ch14-aca -n todo-web `
     --secret-volume-mount "/app/config" `
     --query 'properties.configuration.ingress.fqdn'
    # the update creates a new revision:
    az containerapp revision list -g diamol-ch14-aca -n todo-web -o table
    # when the revision has rolled out, browse to the app again

I’m sure this seems fiddly, but all of these steps can be automated in a script that goes into your CI/CD pipeline. You also have the option to take more control, which you would do in a production deployment—using an existing ACR or Log Analytics Workspace, plugging it into the container app, and defining ingress rules and secrets as part of the deployment. But this example has shown that you can get up and running with a distributed application with just a Compose file and the source code, and be running in the cloud with just a few commands. Figure 14.12 shows my to-do list app working again.

Azure is a great choice for running containerized applications in the cloud. The set of services gives you all the features you need for a full landscape of applications, and you can pick the right option for each of your apps. But the core of your deployment is still Docker—you have Dockerfiles to package all your components and ultimately the container services just provide different ways to configure and run containers. The portability of Docker is one of its biggest advantages, especially when you’re running in the cloud. Business requirements and relationships change, and I’ve been involved in many consulting engagements to migrate applications from one cloud to another. When those apps are already packaged to run in containers, the migration becomes a lot simpler. We’ll finish this chapter with a look at the managed container service in Google Cloud, and see how it compares to ACI and ACA. We’re done with Azure now, so you can delete your ACA resources and tidy everything up.

##### Try it now

This removes all the Azure Container Apps and the additional services they created:
    
    
    az group delete --name diamol-ch14-aca --yes

##### Figure 14.12 Fixing app communication with ingress and secret updates

## 14.4 Google Cloud Run

It’s useful to have experience in more than one cloud. Fundamentally, they all provide a similar range of services to solve similar problems, but they take very different approaches. Google Cloud Run is similar to ACI in its ease of use and similar to ACA in the additional features it gives you. We’ll run the same apps we started with in this chapter on Google Cloud so you get an idea of the differences in architecture and tools between the clouds. Google Cloud uses _projects_ to group services together (e.g., Azure resource groups). When you create a new project, you need to link it to your billing account and explicitly enable the services you want to use.

##### Try it now

You’ll need your Google Cloud account set up and the `gcloud` CLI installed. As with the Docker and Azure CLIs, you can add `--help` to any command to print the Help text:
    
    
    # initialize your CLI (if you haven't already):
    gcloud init
    # store variables - you will need a unique project name,
    # and any region will do; with PowerShell:
    $REGION='us-east1'
    $PROJECT='<your-project-name>'
    # OR Linux shell:
    REGION='us-east1'
    PROJECT='<your-project-name>'
    # create the project:
    gcloud projects create $PROJECT
    # list your billing accounts:
    gcloud billing accounts list
    # link your new project to the billing account:
    gcloud billing projects link $PROJECT `
     --billing-account=<your-billing-account-id>
    # and now enable the Artifact Registry service:
    gcloud services enable artifactregistry.googleapis.com `
     --project=$PROJECT

This is all one-time setup work for a new project that could all be automated when you onboard a new application to Google Cloud. Although there’s nothing exciting to see in my output, as shown in figure 14.13, just make sure you follow the steps so your own project is ready to use.

##### Figure 14.13 Creating and configuring a new project in Google Cloud

The major difference with Azure is that Cloud Run won’t deploy containers from a public image registry—no Docker Hub or GitHub Container Registry for Google—so the image has to be sourced from Artifact Registry. Before you can run any containers with Cloud Run, you need to have created your registry, authenticated with Docker, and pushed the image you want to use. And, Artifact Registry has its own naming scheme. The registry domain is fixed and is based on the location where you create the service—unlike in ACR where you get to choose your own subdomain. After the domain name comes your cloud project name, then the repository for your images, and then the image name. I’m using the US-East region, my project name is `diamol2ees`, and I’ll be creating a repository called `ch14` for these images. I can pull the image `ghcr.io/diamol2e/ch14-hello-cloud`; to push it to Artifact Registry, I’ll need to give it a new reference: `us-east1-docker.pkg.dev/diamol2ees/ch14/hello-cloud`.

##### Try it now

Create the Artifact Registry in your project, and push the hello-cloud image from GitHub Container Registry:
    
    
    # create the registry in your project:
    gcloud artifacts repositories create ch14 `
     --repository-format=docker --project=$PROJECT --location=$REGION
    # print the information - this includes the registry domain:
    gcloud artifacts repositories describe ch14 `
     --project=$PROJECT --location=$REGION
    # store the domain in a variable - PowerShell:
    $REGISTRY="${REGION}-docker.pkg.dev"
    # OR Linux shell:
    REGISTRY="${REGION}-docker.pkg.dev"
    # authenticate the registry with the Docker CLI:
    gcloud auth configure-docker --quiet $REGISTRY
    __# pull the source image:
    docker pull ghcr.io/diamol2e/ch14-hello-cloud
    # tag it with your Artifact Registry reference:
    docker tag ghcr.io/diamol2e/ch14-hello-cloud `
     "$REGISTRY/$PROJECT/ch14/hello-cloud"
    # and push:
    docker push "$REGISTRY/$PROJECT/ch14/hello-cloud"

Artifact Registry doesn’t have the same import functionality as ACR, so you can’t directly import an image from another registry. You can use `gcloud builds` to build an image in the cloud from your local source code and push it direct to Artifact Registry. In this exercise, we just pulled and pushed the target image, so now it’s ready to use in Cloud Run. My output is shown in figure 14.14.

##### Figure 14.14 Creating a repository in Artifact Registry and pushing a local image

Now, we can run a container! It seems unnecessary to have to create your own registry and import the images you want to use, but it’s really a security measure. Public registries can and do host all sorts of malware masquerading as useful apps. Even with genuine images, there’s a risk of supply chain injection—where an attacker could compromise a legitimate image and push a malicious version. Lots of larger organizations have a blanket ban on using Docker Hub to avoid that risk. Google Cloud formalizes that rule and forces you to import the images you want to use, which should become part of a workflow that includes validating both the publisher and correctness of the source image.

The first container we’ll run is the Hello World app, which just prints some information and then sits waiting. That will help us understand the next difference in Google Cloud—Cloud Run containers can be either services or jobs. _Services_ are components that receive network requests: websites or APIs. They’re wired up with a public domain name and created with a health check to ensure traffic gets sent only to healthy containers. We don’t want that for this app, which is just a background component. This will need to be a Cloud Run job, which you create by deploying the job specification and then executing the job to run a container.

##### Try it now

Start by enabling the Cloud Run service in your project, and then create and execute a job using the image you uploaded to Artifact Registry:
    
    
    # enable the service:
    gcloud services enable run.googleapis.com --project=$PROJECT
    # deploy the job using the registry image:
    gcloud run jobs deploy hello-cloud `
     --image="$REGISTRY/$PROJECT/ch14/hello-cloud" --project=$PROJECT `
     --region=$REGION
    # now execute the job - this starts the container:
    gcloud run jobs execute hello-cloud --project=$PROJECT `
     --region=$REGION
    # and print the logs:
    gcloud run jobs logs read hello-cloud --project=$PROJECT `
     --region=$REGION

You don’t need to specify as many parameters for Cloud Run as you do with ACI. At the time of writing, only Linux containers on Intel chips are supported, so there are no operating system or architecture options. Compute resources will use default values if you don’t provide them; these will be minimal amounts, such as one CPU core and 512 MB of memory, but beware of the defaults—you’re charged for the amount of compute allocated while the container is running. Figure 14.15 shows the log output from one job execution; note that the container is still running because the script sits and waits indefinitely.

##### Figure 14.15 Running a container as a Cloud Run job

Jobs are perfect for background services, such as message handlers pulling work from a queue, or they can be configured to run on a schedule for recurring processes. Cloud Run jobs are expected to work in the background and complete at some point, so when the container stops, it won’t be restarted—unless it exits with an error code from the startup process. In that case, Cloud Run will retry the execution with a new container to a maximum of three attempts. That’s obviously not suitable for a frontend component that you want to be available all the time, so you’ll use a Cloud Run service instead. The command to deploy a service is similar to jobs but with a wider range of options, including port mapping and authentication.

##### Try it now

Deploy the pi web application as a Cloud Run service—first pushing the image to your Artifact Registry:
    
    
    # pull the source image:
    docker pull ghcr.io/diamol2e/ch14-pi-web
    # tag it with your Artifact Registry reference:
    docker tag ghcr.io/diamol2e/ch14-pi-web "$REGISTRY/$PROJECT/ch14/pi-web"
    # and push:
    docker push "$REGISTRY/$PROJECT/ch14/pi-web"
    # now deploy the service:
    gcloud run deploy pi-web --image="$REGISTRY/$PROJECT/ch14/pi-web" `
     --port=80 --allow-unauthenticated --project=$PROJECT --region=$REGION
    # print the details:
    gcloud run services describe pi-web --project=$PROJECT --region=$REGION
    # browse to the service URL to test the app

Watch the output closely when you deploy the service, and you’ll see terminology that is familiar from ACA—revisions, ingress, and traffic routing. These are the same concepts: updates to the specifications create different revisions; multiple revisions can be active at the same time, receiving specified percentages of the incoming traffic; and all of this happens under one DNS route, which sets up the ingress. The default auto-scaling setup is also the same as ACA, with the service scaling to zero when there are no HTTP requests, and scaling up to a maximum of three containers under load. You can see my output in figure 14.16, where the container is provisioned with one CPU core (although the application code thinks it has more).

That’s as far as we’ll go with Cloud Run, but you can see that it’s a powerful and quick way to get apps running in a production-grade environment. Cloud Run jobs are similar to ACI, and Cloud Run services are similar to ACA, but the big differences are the requirement to use a private registry, the lack of Compose support, and the restriction to Linux containers on Intel—all of which may change as the platform evolves. The key takeaway here is that it takes very little effort to move apps from one cloud to another when you’re using Docker. The pi web application is a simple example, but you know that apps of any size can be packaged in container images, and more complex apps don’t require much more effort than these basic examples. We’ll round off the chapter by looking at how you choose your cloud container platform. You can delete your Google Cloud project unless you want to keep experimenting.

##### Figure 14.16 Deploying a web app container in a Cloud Run service

##### Try it now

Delete the project, and all the services will be deleted too:
    
    
    gcloud projects delete $PROJECT

## 14.5 Choosing between container platforms

Your choice of cloud will probably be dictated for you—organizations tend to have a preferred provider and might mandate that all apps are deployed to the same cloud. That cloud will have more than one container platform, though, so there will always be a choice to make. You’ve learned about simple and more complex services in this chapter, tried the deployment experiences, and seen some typical features. If running or migrating apps to the cloud is on your horizon, moving to Docker first is definitely a smart move. Then, you should expect to spend more time getting to know the options for each of the services you’re considering and map out which service is the best fit for each app. The choice will often be dictated by the requirements of the application, but here are the main factors you should consider:

  * _Platform support_ __ —Do you need Windows and Linux? Can you run on Arm to save costs? 
  * _Builders_ __ —Can you build images in the cloud? Is there integration with GitHub (or wherever you store your code) and your CI/CD platform? 
  * _Docker Compose_ __ —Can you model your app with Compose for local running _and_ the cloud? That will reduce the learning curve and the gap between dev and ops. 
  * _Operations_ __ —Is observability included? Is there a rich configuration model with support for sensitive data? 
  * _Identity-based integration with other cloud services_ __ —Can you connect to storage or queues without having to store passwords? 
  * _Ease of use_ __ —Is the service easy to get started? Is that at the cost of a full feature set? 
  * _Road map_ __ —Can the platform support you as your app evolves? Will you outgrow a simple container service, and if so, is there a big jump to your next hosting option? 



There isn’t really a wrong choice for container services in the cloud. Some are a better fit for certain apps than others, but they all serve the basic purpose of running containers in a scalable and reliable way, without the overhead of managing infrastructure. And if you do hit a ceiling in terms of service features, you know how easy it is to move a containerized app from one service to another or even to a different cloud.

## 14.6 Lab

You probably have some credits remaining in your Google Cloud account, and this lab will help use them up. The goal is to cover one of the features we didn’t get to and build the pi web application from source using a builder in Google Cloud. Here are some tips to get you started:

  * You’ll want to create a new project so you can isolate this piece of work. 
  * The project will need to have billing and services enabled. 
  * In the new project, you’ll need an Artifact Registry. 
  * The command help for `gcloud builds` will be useful. 
  * The source code and Dockerfile is in `ch14/exercises/pi-web`. 
  * Try deploying the app with more CPU, whether or not it’s faster than the previous deployment. 



As always, the solution is in the lab folder, documented in the README file: <https://mng.bz/8XXK>.


# 15 Kubernetes: A primer

In the beginning, there were containers. But for all the benefits that Docker brought, there were still a few gaps when it came to production. You had to provision machines and then install the OS and the Docker Engine yourself. You had to set up secure access to Docker on those machines. Most importantly, there was no high availability or scale—if you wanted multiple containers serving your app, spread across a fleet of machines, there was (and is) no way to do that with Docker or Compose. A new technology emerged to fill those gaps—the _container orchestrator_ —a platform that manages containers for you. With an orchestrator, you model your application like you do with Compose and send the model to the orchestrator, and it takes responsibility. It assigns containers to machines, organizes networking and storage, and keeps your application running. If a container exits or the machine hosting it goes offline, the orchestrator replaces it with a new container.

In this chapter, you’ll get a rapid introduction to Kubernetes, the world’s favorite container orchestrator. You’ll learn how to model, deploy, and manage applications with a local Kubernetes environment using the same sample apps you’re familiar with from other chapters. There’s a huge amount to learn with Kubernetes, but this chapter will give you the basics and the confidence to explore further.

## 15.1 Why Kubernetes?

There used to be a choice of orchestration platforms: Docker Swarm, Apache DC/OS, and HashiCorp Nomad all provided the same core features with slightly different approaches. Those technologies are still around, but there isn’t really a choice any more. Companies running containers in production are probably doing it on the number-one orchestrator—Kubernetes. There are two good reasons for that. The cloud providers offer managed Kubernetes services, which give you a scalable platform with no infrastructure to administer. And, Kubernetes can run pretty much any application—from modern microservice architectures, through legacy n-tier applications, to replicated databases. The power of being able to model all your apps for a single platform and switch from one cloud to another without having to remodel anything makes it difficult to justify choosing something other than Kubernetes.

The only real downside to Kubernetes is its complexity. The modeling language is far richer and more verbose than Docker Compose, and the logical concepts don’t directly map, so the learning curve is steep. Setting up your own Kubernetes cluster in the datacenter is also not trivial, so most companies managing Kubernetes themselves use a commercial platform, which can be expensive, and/or decide to accelerate their move to the cloud, which can be a bigger program than they expect. The huge payback for your investment is the portability:

  * _Vendor portability for businesses_ —All Kubernetes services are running the same core platform, so you can move your apps to a different provider with little or no rework. 
  * _Skills portability for products_ —Once development and operations teams have learned Kubernetes, they can take those skills to any other application and be immediately productive. 



So, let’s start on that journey. The first idea to understand is the cluster. A _cluster_ is a set of machines that each have a container runtime installed and are grouped together with Kubernetes. The runtime could be Docker or the lightweight core of Docker Engine (called _containerd_), and the machines can be running Linux or Windows on Intel or Arm. Figure 15.1 shows the architecture of a sample Kubernetes cluster.

##### Figure 15.1 A multi-platform Kubernetes cluster can run containers of any kind.

Provisioning the cluster is reasonably straightforward to do yourself, but mostly it will be done for you by the platform—in Azure, it could be as simple as running an `az aks create` command. Then, you can pretty much forget about the individual machines because now you work with the cluster as a whole. You use the Kubernetes command line `kubectl` (pronounced “cube-cuttle”—and don’t let anyone tell you different) to talk to the cluster, sending queries or commands. You deploy applications by modeling them in a YAML file and sending that to the cluster using the command-line interface (CLI). Then the management components in the cluster (called the _control plane_) commission containers to run on the machines, but typically, you’re less interested in the infrastructure layer now, as shown in figure 15.2.

##### Figure 15.2 Deploying an application by sending the YAML model to the cluster

Here’s one final bit of theory before we get stuck in. You describe your application model using the Kubernetes API, which is the real power of Kubernetes. It’s a declarative YAML specification where you list all the components of your application and the connections between them. You use it to model the complete implementation of your application:

  * _Compute layer_ —This layer is deployed as containers. 
  * _Communication layer_ —This layer is implemented with virtual networking inside the cluster and traffic routing into and out of the cluster. 
  * _Storage layer_ —In this layer, you can describe abstract requirements, and Kubernetes will allocate or provision real storage. 
  * _Configuration layer_ —In this layer, application settings and confidential data are stored in the cluster and provided to your components. 



The API is standardized, which is what makes your applications portable between Kubernetes services. Even when you need to model a custom setup for integration with a particular cloud service, that can be done in an abstract way to make migration between clouds easier. Figure 15.3 shows the high-level layers of the Kubernetes API.

##### Figure 15.3 Modeling compute, networking, storage, and configuration in the Kubernetes API

If you’re eager to try it out, you can get started very easily, as there are Kubernetes deployments designed to run as a development environment on a single machine. The simplest way to run Kubernetes is to use the built-in functionality in Docker Desktop. Open the configuration settings, select the Kubernetes option, and toggle on the Enable Kubernetes button, as shown in figure 15.4.

The Kubernetes control plane components all run in containers, and Docker Desktop downloads the images and starts everything up for you. If you’re not using Docker Desktop, then the best local option is called k3d (<https://k3d.io>), or if you have access to a cloud environment, then Azure Kubernetes Service (AKS), Amazon’s Elastic Kubernetes Service (EKS), or Google Kubernetes Engine (GKE) are all fine. Any Kubernetes cluster will do for running the samples in this chapter.

##### Try it now

Make sure you have everything set up by running the Kubernetes CLI to query your cluster:
    
    
    # make sure you're connected to the Docker Desktop cluster:
    kubectl config use-context docker-desktop
    # print information about the machines in the cluster -
    # Kubernetes calls them "nodes":
    kubectl get nodes

##### Figure 15.4 Enabling Kubernetes in Docker Desktop

If everything is set up correctly, you’ll see output like mine in figure 15.5. You may have more nodes if you’re connected to a bigger cluster in the cloud, or, if you’re using K3D, then your node will have a different name—but as long as you see a control plane node listed in the Ready state, then you’re good to go.

##### Figure 15.5 Listing the nodes in the cluster is a good way to ensure that everything is running correctly.

Now, we can start exploring the API, modeling some apps, and seeing how to deploy and manage them in Kubernetes.

## 15.2 Modeling applications with Pods and Services

The first thing to wrap your mind around is that you don’t model containers directly in the API: containers are always run in a Kubernetes object called a _Pod_. The Pod is a wrapper for one or more containers, and its job is to manage the containers. In Docker, when a container exits because of an application crash, that’s the end of it. In Kubernetes, the Pod is watching the container—if it exits, the Pod immediately creates a replacement container. It’s the first layer of additional availability that Kubernetes adds.

Pod specs are very simple: at minimum, you give the Pod a name and list out the containers the Pod needs to run. For each container—often there’s only one—you give it a name and specify the Docker image to run. Listing 15.1 shows a simple Pod spec for a console application that prints the value of pi.

##### Listing 15.1 A basic Pod spec
    
    
    apiVersion: v1
    kind: Pod
    metadata:
      name: pi
    spec:
      containers:
        - name: pi
          image: kiamol/ch05-pi

There’s a lot more in there than the equivalent Compose spec or `docker` `run kiamol/ch05-pi`, but much of it is boilerplate. Kubernetes has a versioned API that is broken down into sections. Every Kubernetes YAML file will have these fields:

  * `apiVersion`—The version of the API this model uses. Different releases of Kubernetes support different API versions, and this is how you tell Kubernetes which version you need. 
  * `kind`—The type of the object being modeled. 
  * `metadata`—Contains information about the object—at minimum, its name, but it can include other fields. 
  * `spec`—The actual specification of the resource. The fields available in the spec depend on the API version and the kind. Pods include the container list in the spec; other types of resources have different data. 



When you deploy this application, Kubernetes will allocate the Pod to a node and the node will tell the container runtime to pull the image and start the container.

##### Try it now

You use `kubectl apply` to send YAML models to the cluster. The Kubernetes CLI takes a standard approach to managing resources with common verbs—you use `kubectl get` to list all the resources of a particular type:
    
    
    # deploy the Pod YAML:
    kubectl apply -f ch15/exercises/pi/pod.yaml
    # show the Pod table:
    kubectl get pods
    # print the logs from the pi Pod:
    kubectl logs pi
    # show Pods again with the watch flag which prints any changes:
    kubectl get pods --watch
    # Ctrl-C or Cmd-C to exit the watch

You may be surprised at the result when you run this. Pods are designed to model an application component, which is meant to keep running—like a web app or a queue processor—but our spec is using an image where the container does one thing and then exits. Kubernetes uses a _desired state_ approach where the model describes what should be running in the cluster. Our container is exiting unexpectedly, so the current state doesn’t match the desired state, and the Pod creates a replacement container (Kubernetes calls this a Pod restart). When that container exits, the Pod creates another replacement, and so on. My output is shown in figure 15.6.

##### Figure 15.6 Kubernetes will keep restarting a Pod if the container exits.

Kubernetes is smart enough to know when containers in the same Pod keep crashing—if it carried on, immediately replacing a failing app, then it could overload the node running the Pod. If a Pod keeps restarting in a short period of time, then Kubernetes adds a backoff policy, waiting for longer and longer periods between restarts to try and keep the cluster healthy. This is the `CrashLoopBackOff` status—and when you see it in a real application, it means something is seriously wrong. In this case, our model is at fault—either we’re not configuring the container correctly to make sure it keeps running, or we’re using the wrong part of the API if we just want to run a task that is expected to end.

Let’s switch to a correctly modeled example. The Pod spec in listing 15.2 uses a variation of the random number generator—this is the REST API component. That container should keep running, so if it does crash and exit, then we do want the Pod to restart and keep our application up. 

##### Listing 15.2 A Pod spec for the random number REST API
    
    
    apiVersion: v1
    kind: Pod
    metadata:
      name: numbers-api
      labels:
        app: numbers
        component: api
    spec:
      containers:
        - name: api
          image: kiamol/ch03-numbers-api
          ports:
            - containerPort: 80
              name: http

There are two new fields in this spec:

  * `labels`—Key-value pairs that you can apply to any resource in Kubernetes. They are used for managing objects and for linking together parts of the model in a loosely coupled way. 
  * `ports`—Lists the ports that the container is listening on. This also helps link together parts of the model, but it’s not enforced by Kubernetes—the container might actually be listening on different ports or on none at all. 



As you might guess, we’re still only using a tiny part of the full Pod spec. There are lots more optional fields that you can include to take advantage of more Kubernetes features, but that’s all part of the learning curve. There are extra fields in this example, but deploying this model will create a single Pod, just like in the previous exercise. 

##### Try it now

Deploy the random-number API Pod. It’s the same `kubectl apply` command, but this app is listening for network traffic so we can make HTTP calls to it:
    
    
    # deploy the application:
    kubectl apply -f ch15/exercises/numbers-api/pod.yaml
    # list Pods, showing the labels:
    kubectl get pods --show-labels
    # print human-friendly details about the Pod:
    kubectl describe pod numbers-api
    # set kubectl to listen on port 8015
    # and relay traffic to the Pod on port 80:
    kubectl port-forward pod/numbers-api 8015:80
