    # now browse to http:/_._ /localhost:8015/rng
    # Ctrl-C or Cmd-C to exit the port-forward

Kubernetes provides the networking stack within a cluster. Every Pod gets its own virtual IP address, and Pods can reach each other using that address. You can’t send traffic outside the cluster into Pods directly, but the Kubectl CLI has the handy `port-forward` command for that. It listens for local network traffic and sends it into a Pod. The great thing is that it works for remote clusters too, so you can debug by sending traffic via your local machine to a Pod running on Kubernetes in the cloud. You can see my output in figure 15.7.

##### Figure 15.7 Running an HTTP server in a Pod and routing traffic via the Kubernetes CLI

Port forwarding is a useful test tool, but obviously you can’t use it for apps that should be publicly exposed. For that, you need to model the networking too using another type of Kubernetes object: the _Service_. Don’t confuse this with Services in Docker Compose—in Kubernetes they are specific network routing components. A Service can be configured to listen for traffic coming into the cluster (on any node) and route it into a Pod (whichever node the Pod is running on). The link between Services and Pods uses labels for loose coupling—listing 15.3 shows a Service that listens on port `8015` and directs traffic to Pods with the matching `app` and `component` labels.

##### Listing 15.3 A Service spec with a selector to identify Pods
    
    
    apiVersion: v1
    kind: Service
    metadata:
      name: numbers-api
    spec:
      ports:
        - port: 8015
          targetPort: http
      selector:  
        app: numbers
        component: api
      type: LoadBalancer

Modeling the relationship between objects with labels and selectors is a powerful concept that Kubernetes uses a lot. It allows for one-to-zero, one-to-one, and one-to-many relationships to be modeled in the same way. Services look for Pods with labels matching their selector:

  * If there are none, then Services can’t forward any traffic. 
  * If there is one match, then that Pod gets all the traffic. 
  * If there are multiple matches, then the Service acts as a load balancer, distributing incoming requests evenly among the Pods. 



##### Try it now

Verify that you have a Pod with labels matching the Service’s selector—Kubectl supports the same mechanism. Then, deploy the Service to see how it works:
    
    
    # you can get resources with a label selector:
    kubectl get po --selector app=numbers,component=api
    # deploy the Service that uses the same selector:
    kubectl apply -f ch15/exercises/numbers-api/service.yaml
    # list Services:
    kubectl get svc
    # and print the details of the new Service:
    kubectl describe svc numbers-api
    # try the app using the Service port:
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8015/rng

There are just a few commands in this exercise, but there’s a lot going on. As you work through it, you’ll see that one Pod matches the label selector in the Service—it’s the Pod we deployed in the previous exercise. When the Service is created, it finds that Pod and adds the Pod’s IP address to its _endpoints_ —which is the list of destinations where it will send traffic. When you make an HTTP request to the cluster, it gets picked up by the Service and routed to the Pod. My output is shown in figure 15.8.

##### Figure 15.8 Deploying a Service to route incoming cluster traffic to a Pod

How does the Service know to bind to the `localhost` address? That’s the truly clever part of Kubernetes—clusters might have different capabilities, and you can deploy the same model and get different outcomes. A `LoadBalancer` type of Service integrates with the cluster’s networking stack. Docker Desktop runs a single-node cluster on your machine so it binds to `localhost`. If you deploy the exact same YAML to a cloud Kubernetes service, it will allocate a public IP address for the Service and wire up all the infrastructure to bind that address to the cluster. Your containerized app is then globally available.

You’ll always use Services to model the communication layer of your applications, but Pods are really just a building block in the Kubernetes API. You can’t do much with a single Pod, and in the real world, you’ll use objects that build on Pods and manage them for you.

## 15.3 Scaling and upgrading apps with Deployments

It seems like we’re jumping in at the deep end here, but it’s all deep end in Kubernetes. There’s a minimum set of resources you have to be familiar with to do anything useful and to understand some of the platform’s power. We’ll cover those resources in the rest of the chapter, starting with the main object you’ll use to manage Pods: the _Deployment_. Deployments are Pod controllers—the spec for the Deployment object includes a templated Pod spec, and the Deployment object creates Pods from the template. The Deployment’s job is to make sure the correct number of Pods are deployed and running—just like the Pod’s job is to make sure its containers are running.

Listing 15.4 shows an abbreviated Deployment YAML—you’ll see that here’s a Pod template inside the Deployment spec. Nesting objects is a very powerful pattern that the Kubernetes API follows a lot. The Pod resource inside the Deployment is the same as the Pod resource you could model directly in YAML, so once you’ve learned all the features of a Pod, you can use them inside a Deployment too.

##### Listing 15.4 Deployment spec that includes the templated Pod spec
    
    
    apiVersion: v1 
    kind: Deployment
    metadata:
      name: numbers-api
    spec:                   # this is the Deployment spec
      replicas: 1           # the number of Pods to create
      selector:             # selector to find Pods
        matchLabels:
          app: numbers
          component: api
      template:             # this is the template for creating Pods
        metadata:
          labels:           # labels the Deployment selector uses
            app: numbers
            component: api
        spec:               # and this is the Pod spec
          containers:
            - name: api     # full container spec snipped

Deployments use label selectors to find the Pods they manage, just like Services use them to find the Pods to send traffic to. You can use any labels you like, but the name of the app and the component are common ones. You define them in the Pod template, and the selector in the Deployment has to include some or all of them so that it’s able to find the Pods. When you create a Deployment, it creates the Pod(s), and then each Pod creates the container(s).

##### Try it now

Remove all the existing Pods, and then send the new model to the cluster—the folder path includes YAML files for a Deployment and a Service. List Deployments and Pods, and you’ll see a Pod created with a random(ish) name:
    
    
    # delete all Pods:
    kubectl delete pod --all
    # deploy the new model:
    kubectl apply -f ch15/exercises/numbers-api-v2/
    # list all Deployments:
    kubectl get deploy
    # list Pods with the matching app label:
    kubectl get po -l app=numbers --show-labels
    # delete the Pods:
    kubectl delete pod -l app=numbers
    # list them again:
    kubectl get po -l app=numbers --show-labels

This Deployment creates a single Pod, and the result so far isn’t much different from specifying a Pod in YAML. It’s the management of the Pod that is different. If the node running the Pod goes offline, the Deployment will create a replacement Pod on another node. This is the desired state approach in action again—if the number of Pods doesn’t match the number stated in the model, Kubernetes takes action. You can see my output in figure 15.9.

This process of checking the current state against the desired state is called a reconciliation loop, and Kubernetes does it all the time. If you check on the web application in the preceding exercise, you’ll see it carries on working after you delete the Pod. At that point, the Deployment sees there are no Pods matching its selector and creates a new one. At the same time, the Service sees there’s a change to the Pods matching its selector (the same set of labels used for the Deployment), so it updates the list of endpoints to send traffic to the new Pod instead. Resilience is one of the features of the Deployment.

##### Figure 15.9 Deployments manage Pods; if a Pod goes missing, the Deployment creates another.

Scale is another feature. The Deployment has a template to create Pods, and it can create as many as you need. This actually goes together with resilience because if you have a single Pod and it goes down, there’s some downtime while the replacement Pod gets created and the Services wire it up. If you have multiple Pods running and one goes down, the others will continue serving traffic while the replacement Pod is started.

##### Try it now

You define scale by the number of replicas the Deployment should have. We set it to one in the YAML file but you can manually force a change with the CLI:
    
    
    # set the scale to three - this will create two new Pods:
    kubectl scale deploy/numbers-api --replicas 3
    # all the Pods have the same spec:
    kubectl get po -l app=numbers -o wide
    # and they all get added to the Service endpoints:
    kubectl get endpoints numbers-api
    # repeat a few times - when one Pod fails the others still work:
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8015/rng

This app is deliberately broken to demonstrate how the Service distributes traffic. Kubernetes uses a round-robin approach to get an even spread of requests to the Pods. When one application becomes unhealthy, the next request will work because the other Pods are running healthy applications. This application spec doesn’t let the Service know if a Pod is unhealthy, though, so traffic still gets routed even when all the apps are broken, as you can see in figure 15.10.

##### Figure 15.10 You can scale a Deployment to add Pods; new Pods share the Service’s traffic distribution.

Remember from chapter 8 that you can add health checks to Docker images to let the container runtime know if the application inside the container is healthy. Kubernetes has the same concept, but you need to specify the checks in your model—`HEALTHCHECK` instructions in the Docker image are ignored. You can configure different types of health checks, which result in different actions when they fail. Listing 15.5 shows an update to the Deployment spec that includes two health checks defined as _container probes_.

##### Listing 15.5 Adding health checks to Pod containers in a Deployment
    
    
    apiVersion: v1
    kind: Deployment
    metadata:
      name: numbers-api
    spec:
      replicas: 2
      # ...
        spec:
          containers:
            - name: api
              image: kiamol/ch03-numbers-api
              readinessProbe:
                httpGet:
                  path: /healthz
                  port: http
                periodSeconds: 5
              livenessProbe:
                httpGet:
                  path: /healthz
                  port: http
                periodSeconds: 20
              # the rest of the container spec is the same

Kubernetes supports multiple health check types. You can test to see whether the container has an open TCP port, checking that the app is listening for traffic; you can run a custom command inside the container, which could be a script running lots of tests; and you can make HTTP requests and check the response code. We’ve configured HTTP `GET` requests to the health endpoint in the URL for two probes:

  * `readinessProbe`—Set to run every 5 seconds. If it fails repeatedly, then the Pod leaves the Ready state, and the Service removes its IP address from the endpoint list. 
  * `livenessProbe`—Set to run every 20 seconds. Repeated failures cause the Pod to restart, creating a replacement container. 



Together, these configure high availability for the app. When one application instance fails, that Pod will soon be removed from the Service and won’t get any traffic. That should mean clients won’t see any error responses, and, in some cases, the pause could give the application time to heal. This app doesn’t heal itself, so after a time, the app gets restarted, and a new container comes online in a working state.

##### Try it now

Apply the updated Deployment specification to see if our health checks keep the app online:
    
    
    # update the spec:
    kubectl apply -f ch15/exercises/numbers-api-v3/
    # watch the Pods to see how the update works:
    kubectl get po -l app=numbers -o wide -w 
    # browse to the app at http:/_._ /localhost:8015/rng and refresh a few times
    # Ctrl-C or Cmd-C to exit the watch

The `--watch` (or `-w`) flag in the CLI prints a new line anytime the state of a watched resource changes. That’s very useful for seeing what’s happening in the cluster, but it does produce a lot of text. If you keep a keen eye on the Ready, Status, and Restarts columns when you go through this exercise, you’ll see how Kubernetes is working hard to keep your app reconciled to the desired state. I’ve condensed my output in figure 15.11, but you’ll still need to look carefully.

Changes to the Pod specification in a Deployment are rolled out gradually. A new Pod is created from the updated spec, and Kubernetes waits for the new Pod container to start—if there is a readiness probe in the container, it also waits until the check has passed. When the new Pod is ready, one of the old Pods is terminated, and that process continues until all the old Pods have been replaced. This protects you from bad releases; if there’s a problem with the update and the new Pods don’t start correctly, then the old Pods aren’t replaced and your app keeps running. It’s all about high availability and keeping your app running at scale. Occasional failures in your app can be handled by well-configured container probes, and those midnight support calls will be limited to more drastic problems that require human intervention. There’s much more to Kubernetes, but we’ll finish the chapter with the last big piece of the model that you need to understand to be effective: application configuration.

##### Figure 15.11 Container probes in the model add high availability to the running app.

## 15.4 Injecting configuration settings with ConfigMaps and Secrets

App configuration is a major topic. We have a whole chapter (chapter 18) dedicated to it in part 4 of this book, where we’ll cover how you should design and package your app in Docker to support a flexible configuration approach. Here, we’ll look at what that approach means in Kubernetes. You know that Docker creates the virtual environment where your app runs, which can include setting environment variables and loading files into the container filesystem. That’s the basis of the configuration model in Kubernetes, where you can define application settings as part of your model that get fed into the container runtime.

Listing 15.6 shows a Kubernetes spec for a ConfigMap resource. This is a configuration map used for storing small pieces of data as text files or as key-value pairs.

##### Listing 15.6 A ConfigMap object storing a single key-value pair
    
    
    apiVersion: v1
    kind: ConfigMap
    metadata:
      name: sleep-config-env
    data:
      DIAMOL_EDITION: 2e

This is a standalone object. You can send the YAML to Kubernetes, and it will create a ConfigMap object, which doesn’t do anything by itself. But you can reference a ConfigMap in the container specification for a Pod and have the contents of the ConfigMap loaded as environment variables into the container.

##### Try it now

Run a simple app with a Pod that reads environment variables from a ConfigMap. Check that the variables are set by running a command inside the Pod container:
    
    
    # this creates a Deployment that references a ConfigMap:
    kubectl apply -f ch15/exercises/sleep-configured
    # print the details of the ConfigMap:
    kubectl describe configmap sleep-config-env
    # and print the environment variables in the container:
    kubectl exec deploy/sleep -- printenv

The Kubernetes CLI has an `exec` command that works in the same way as `docker exec`—it runs a command inside a container, which could be an interactive shell to connect you into the container. You can run that for a specific Pod or for a Deployment, and then Kubernetes will pick one of the Pods to run against. You’ll see in figure 15.12 that the details of the ConfigMap show the full content of the data, and the environment variables in the container have that same data.

##### Figure 15.12 Injecting ConfigMap data into a Pod container as environment variables

Modern application stacks usually merge configuration settings from multiple sources—environment variables and text files from different locations. The data section in a ConfigMap can contain multiple key-value pairs, or whole blocks of text in XML, YAML, TOML, or whatever format your application needs. Listing 15.7 shows a ConfigMap object that stores a JSON text block. The spec is the same as for storing environment variables, but in this case, the text is used as the contents of a file and the key is used as the file name.

##### Listing 15.7 Storing a JSON configuration file as ConfigMap data
    
    
    apiVersion: v1
    kind: ConfigMap
    metadata:
      name: sleep-config-file
    data:
      config.json: |-
        {
          "Database" : {
            "Provider" : "Postgres"
          }
        }

That strange pipe-dash combination (`|-`) is YAML for denoting that multiple lines follow but they’re part of the same field, so you can keep your formatting and make the spec easier to read. Pods can mount a ConfigMap as a _volume_ —a similar idea to Docker volumes but more flexible because you can have different types of data sources that get mounted into the container filesystem. A Pod that is set up to mount this ConfigMap into a folder path will see a file called `config.json` at that path with the contents of the data field.

##### Try it now

Update the sleep Deployment with a new spec that references two ConfigMaps—one to load into environment variables and the other to mount into the filesystem:
    
    
    # update the Deployment spec - this will replace the Pod:
    kubectl apply -f ch15/exercises/sleep-configured-v2
    # print the details of the new ConfigMap:
    kubectl describe cm sleep-config-file
    # list the path where the ConfigMap is mounted in the container:
    kubectl exec deploy/sleep -- ls -l /app/config 
    # print the contents of the mounted file:
    kubectl exec deploy/sleep -- cat /app/config/config.json

You know that in Docker, you can’t change environment variables or volume mounts in a running container—they can only be set when the container is created. That’s true in Kubernetes too, so when you change the Pod spec template in a Deployment object that alters the container setup, it gets implemented by rolling out new Pods. This exercise shows you that the new container sees the contents of the ConfigMap as a file that just looks like an ordinary part of the filesystem. My output is shown in figure 15.13; if you also print the environment variables in the new Pod, you’ll see that the ConfigMap from the previous exercise is loaded too.

ConfigMaps are a useful way to keep your application model flexible. You can keep the core parts of the specification the same, but vary configuration for different environments at the ConfigMap level. You’ve seen that anyone with access to the cluster can print the contents of ConfigMaps in plain text, so they’re not suitable for storing sensitive data such as credentials. For that, Kubernetes has _Secrets_ which work in the same way as ConfigMaps but can be linked to an external secret service (e.g., KeyVault in Azure or HashiCorp Vault in the datacenter), so the source of the data is secured outside of the cluster.

##### Figure 15.13 Mounting a ConfigMap to a folder path presents the data content as files.

That’s it for the main parts of the Kubernetes API. There’s plenty more to dig into, but we’ve covered the key parts of the compute, networking, and configuration parts of the model. One final exercise runs our old to-do list app with a Postgres database. The model includes Deployments, Services, ConfigMaps, and Secrets, which is everything you need to start being productive with Kubernetes. Figure 15.14 shows the architecture, and you can explore the YAML if you want to see how it all fits together.

##### Figure 15.14 A full application model in Kubernetes with multiple components using all the core resources

However complex your model is, you deploy it in the same way with the Kubernetes CLI. The cluster creates all the resources and wires everything together for you. This spec creates two replicas for the web application and one for the database, so you’ll see three Pods.

##### Try it now

Deploy the to-do list application and verify that it all works as expected. There are plenty of resources to explore if you have time:
    
    
    # deploy the application:
    kubectl apply -f ch15/exercises/todo-list
    # print the Pods and Services get created:
    kubectl get po,svc -l app=todo -o wide
    # when the Pods are Ready, print the last log line from the database:
    kubectl logs deploy/todo-db --tail 1
    # browse to http:/_._ /localhost:8025 - add some data and refresh

When you run this, you’ll see it’s a pretty good start at modeling a distributed application in Kubernetes. The finer details aren’t there—health checks for the containers and isolating the database data in its own volume—but it’s okay for a first cut. This is how I would approach modeling an app: get each component working first, connect them and get the whole app working, and then iterate on it to improve performance, availability, and maintainability. My output in figure 15.15 shows the app in all its glory.

##### Figure 15.15 Deploying and managing distributed apps in Kubernetes; the tools and processes are the same no matter how complex the app.

That’s all we’re going to cover. When I first started using Kubernetes, I came fresh from the Compose format and thought, “Wow. This is so complicated. Surely, it’s not going to take off.” So don’t come to me for hot tech predictions. The point of Kubernetes is that all the complexity is there to make sure you can model any kind of application. In my consulting work, I’ve helped companies move all sorts of apps to Kubernetes in the cloud—everything from decade-old .NET Framework Windows apps to brand-new microservices apps to big data systems. They all use the same Kubernetes API and tools, and they’re all flexibly designed so that the same model can run minimal test environments and fully scaled production environments, just with a few different configuration settings. That’s really valuable because the deployment is portable and future-proofed, and the skills to do that are worth putting some effort in to learn.

## 15.5 Planning out a Kubernetes learning path

The first step toward using Kubernetes effectively is to master Docker—so you’re already well on the way. The remaining chapters of this book will get you thinking about production readiness with patterns and concepts that all apply to Kubernetes too. When you’re done with _Learn Docker in a Month of Lunches_ and want to continue your journey with containers, here’s what I would recommend:

  * Read _Learn Kubernetes in a Month of Lunches_ (Manning, 2021)_._ Ouch. It’s a bit much to be promoting my own book, but I think the _Month of Lunches_ format is exceptionally well suited to Kubernetes. You need to spend a lot of time working with Kubernetes to become comfortable with it, and you need to experience a lot of the API to truly understand what you can do. _Learn Kubernetes in a Month of Lunches_ guides you through that, with the same goal-oriented real-world approach you’ve had in this book. A small word of warning, though—there’s an awful lot to learn in Kubernetes, and I don’t skip on the important things to keep the chapters short, so you might need clearance for longer lunch breaks. 
  * When you’re comfortable with the Kubernetes API and you can model and run your own applications, you’ll soon realize that there’s a bit of a limitation with the approach. YAML models are static—if you set the replica count to 1 in a Deployment, you can’t change that to 10 for production without altering the YAML. You don’t want multiple copies of the same model with slight differences, so the Kubernetes ecosystem has a couple of templating options. These are languages that take the core Kubernetes API and give you an extensible model so that you can easily configure different variations of the same app. _Learn Kubernetes in a Month of Lunches_ introduces you to _Helm_ , as well as _Kustomize_. You’ll want to get a feeling for both and then choose one (probably Helm) to learn in more detail. 
  * If you want to prove how well you know Kubernetes, there are three certification options from the Cloud Native Computing Foundation (CNCF; which administers the Kubernetes project): Certified Kubernetes Application Developer (CKAD), Certified Kubernetes Administrator (CKA), and Certified Kubernetes Security Specialist (CKS). CKAD is likely to be the one you want because it focuses on modeling, running, and managing apps. I have plenty of training material online to help you there at <https://blog.sixeyed.com>. 
  * That’s still not enough, I’m afraid. Kubernetes integrates deeply with the platform it’s running on, so you’ll need to learn about those integration points and the feature set of your provider(s). I mentioned that LoadBalancer Services will allocate a public IP address and wire up routing when you deploy to Kubernetes in the cloud. Cloud platforms also have their own storage options, secret management, access control, and monitoring. It all builds on top of the core Kubernetes API, but you’ll need to learn what features are available in AKS, EKS, or GKE, as mentioned earlier, so that you can make the best use of them in your app. 



It all sounds like a big ask—but containers are truly a career-changing technology. I started working with Docker in 2014 and Kubernetes in 2016, and—along with the cloud—it’s still the main thing I’m helping companies with today. Kubernetes has become such a dominant technology that even when it does get superseded, whatever replaces it is going to need to support the Kubernetes API, so your learning investment should be safe for years to come.

## 15.6 Lab

You’ll spend a surprising amount of your Kubernetes modeling time working on app configuration. It’s an ops-dev intersection where the ops team need to know how the app loads configuration settings and what can be varied, and the dev team need to know what the options are for running in different environments. This lab gives you some experience with that:

  * We’re starting with the to-do app you deployed in section 15.4. If you don’t have it, deploy it again from the folder `ch15/exercises/todo-list`. 
  * Use the app to check the logs for the web component—you’ll see there aren’t any because the default configuration settings only print logs when there are errors. 
  * There’s a file called `logging.json` in the `ch15/lab` folder that increases the logging level. Your goal is to inject that configuration file into the web container filesystem. 
  * The app looks for that logging configuration file in the same folder as the `config.json` file. 
  * When you make the change, do the existing Pods pick it up and start writing logs, or do you need to force the Deployment to create new Pods to pick up the new configuration file? 



My solution is in the lab folder if you need it, and there’s some documentation in the README file: <https://mng.bz/Ewwq>. And remember the Reset Kubernetes button in Docker Desktop for when you’re done.


# 16 CI/CD in the cloud with Docker and GitHub Actions

The continuous integration/continuous deployment (CI/CD) pipeline is the heart of modern software projects. Developers push changes to source code and trigger a pipeline run that builds, tests, packages, and deploys the entire application. Automatic deployment to production might be a bit much for some organizations, but you can continuously deploy to a pre-production environment and be confident that you always have your latest features ready to go. The pipeline is crucial to the project’s success, so it needs to run quickly and be repeatable and portable. That gives the development team fast feedback and lets you easily debug issues with the pipeline, so you can move between CI/CD services with the minimum amount of rework. Docker is a great fit for powering your pipeline—you can compile code and run unit tests in multi-stage builds; spin up a local deployment of the app using Docker Compose to test it; and publish tested images to a release repository for production deployment.

In this chapter, we’ll walk through a full CI/CD pipeline in the cloud using Docker to do all the hard work. The major CI/CD providers support Docker (Azure DevOps, CircleCI, GitLab, and many more), so you can put your build steps together using familiar commands. We’ll be using GitHub Actions—a mature and popular service with support for Windows and Linux build agents—to also work through a multi-platform build. GitHub Actions are free to run in a public repository. You’ll need to sign up for a GitHub account if you don’t already have one, but following the exercises won’t cost you anything.

## 16.1 DevOps in the cloud with GitHub Actions

GitHub Actions is popular because it’s fast, feature-rich, and easy to use. It follows the same basic pattern as all the cloud CI/CD services:

  * You define your build steps in files that live inside your source code repository. 
  * When you push changes to your code, that triggers a new build. 
  * GitHub Actions provisions some compute—a _runner_ —to perform the build. 
  * The runner pulls your source code repository and executes the build steps. 
  * At the end of the build, the runner is cleaned up and decommissioned. 



You use YAML to define your build process, and each YAML file is a GitHub _workflow_. There’s a large library of steps you can use to build your workflow—checking out a repository, building code, and reviewing code with AI tools. These are the individual _actions_ that model your workflow, and although thousands of them are available, I prefer to use as few as possible and put the bulk of the logic in script files. This is the same approach we followed in chapter 11 with Jenkins, and the justification is the same. GitHub’s workflow YAML is a custom format, and those actions only work in GitHub workflows. If you want to migrate your build provider—which you will eventually—it’s much harder to unpick all the custom steps and re-create them in a new service. Using scripts instead makes the migration much simpler and allows you to run the steps on your own machine to debug issues or add features.

##### Try it now

You’ll need some more tools installed to follow all the exercises in this chapter. You probably already have a (free) GitHub account and Git client. The build logic is all in scripts, so you also need PowerShell 7+ (<https://aka.ms/pwsh>). Optionally, install the GitHub command line (<https://cli.github.com>) if you prefer not to keep refreshing the web UI:
    
    
    # check your dependencies are available:
    git version
    pwsh -version
    # launch PowerShell:
    pwsh
    # and make sure your Docker setup is using Linux containers:
    $info = docker version -f json | ConvertFrom-Json
    echo "Platform: $($info.Server.Os)/$($info.Server.Arch)"

If you don’t like the idea of installing PowerShell, you should use this chapter as an opportunity to learn to love it. The logic around multi-platform builds always ends up being fiddly, and PowerShell gives you a readable and easily maintainable set of scripts (I’d hate to see what some of these steps would look like in Bash). It’s also a cross-platform tool, so team members can run those scripts on whatever laptop OS they have, and GitHub runners come with PowerShell preinstalled, so you get the same experience in the cloud on Linux and Windows. Figure 16.1 shows my output—as long as you have something similar, you’re ready to go.

##### Figure 16.1 Verifying you have all the tools you need for this chapter

The exercises in this chapter are in their own dedicated GitHub repository. You’re going to make your own copy of that repository—called a _fork_ in GitHub—so you can push changes and trigger workflows in your own repository version. Open GitHub in your browser, sign in, and then go to <https://github.com/diamol2e/ch16/fork>. In the screen shown in figure 16.2, leave the defaults as they are, and click Create Fork.

##### Figure 16.2 Forking the repository for the chapter creates a copy in your own GitHub account.

It will take a moment to fork the repository, but then you’ll have your own copy—make a note of the URL (my GitHub username is _sixeyed_ , so my fork is at <https://github.com/sixeyed/>). The repository contains a version of the source code for the Astronomy Picture of the Day app we introduced way back in chapter 4, together with Dockerfiles, Compose files, CI/CD scripts, and GitHub workflows. Those Dockerfiles have been extended to include unit tests that run in a separate stage of the build. Listing 16.1 shows a snip from the Dockerfile for the Go component.

##### Listing 16.1 Running unit tests in a multi-stage Dockerfile
    
    
    FROM diamol/golang:2e AS build
    
    COPY go.mod main.go /src/
    WORKDIR /src
    RUN go mod tidy
    RUN go build -o /server
    
    # unit tests
    FROM build AS test
    COPY sample_test.go /src/
    RUN go test && \
     echo '' > tests.passed
    
    # continues with final stage

There are two things to note here. First, the unit tests run in their own stage, which depends on the build stage. If you want to compile the app and run tests quickly, you’re not interested in packaging the final image, so you can target just the test stage. Second, the test stage generates a text file if the tests pass. That text file is copied into the final image to create a dependency and make sure the test stage isn’t skipped when you do build the full image. There are three components to this app, and they all have the same Dockerfile structure. Listing 16.2 shows part of the build script that runs the compilation and the unit test stages for each of them.

##### Listing 16.2 Building the Docker images targeting the test stage
    
    
    # $components is a variable which stores the component names
    foreach ($component in $components) {
      pushd $component
      docker build -t "$component-test" --target test .
      if ($LASTEXITCODE -ne 0) {
        exit 1
      }
      popd
    }

The focus here is on fast feedback. This will run as the first part of the CI/CD pipeline, and if there’s a breaking change in the code or a failing test, then we want to know that as quickly as possible. The script exits as soon as it hits a failure. It’s also a nice, fast script, which developers can run before they push changes and be confident that if it succeeds on their machine, it will work in GitHub too.

We can do the same thing—and this is the pattern we’ll follow in this chapter as we build up the full pipeline: running the script for each section locally first and then triggering the workflow for that part in GitHub. That way, you can see the build steps in action on your machine and then see how it looks in GitHub.

##### Try it now

Clone your fork of the repository, and run the first build script to compile source code and run the unit tests with Docker:
    
    
    # store your GitHub ID in a variable:
    $ghId = '<your-github-username>'
    
    # and clone your own copy of the repo:
    cd <location-for-the-code>
    git clone "https:/./github.com/$ghId/ch16"
    
    # switch to the build script folder:
    cd ch16/cicd
    
    # run the build script:
    ./build.ps1

It might take a minute or two to download all the base images when you first run that build script, but if you repeat it, the download should complete in seconds. That’s fast feedback and there’s no excuse for developers to skip that step and risk breaking the build. My output in figure 16.3 is abridged, but you know what Docker builds look like by now.

##### Figure 16.3 Scripting Docker image builds includes compiling code and running unit tests.

This is all well and good, but it’s not enough to just test the happy path. What if a code change causes a test to fail? We need to make sure that the failure bubbles up through the Docker build and causes the build script to fail, so we keep that fast feedback and know that there’s an issue.

##### Try it now

There’s a bad test in the Node.js application source code. It’s ignored at the moment, but when you rename the file, it will be included and cause the build script to fail:
    
    
    # rename the bad test file so it gets included in the test run:
    mv ../src/access-log/src/failing.test.js.ignore `
     ../src/access-log/src/failing.test.js
    # now run the build again:
    ./build.ps1

This run should be very quick. The failing component is the first one to build, and the script exits as soon as it fails. This is exactly what we want—developers often work in an Integrated Development Environment (IDE), which is quite different from the build environment. Unit tests may all pass in the IDE but fail in the container, which loads libraries and configuration from different places. You can see my output in figure 16.4, which is telling me not to push my changes because I’ll break the build.

##### Figure 16.4 Proving the build script fails if there’s a failing unit test in the source code

Listing 16.3 shows the first workflow we’ll be running in GitHub. If you’re completely new to the syntax, it should still be fairly easy to see what’s happening. We’ll be running a job on the latest Ubuntu Linux runner, and the job has two steps: check out the code and run the `build.ps1` PowerShell script in the `cicd` directory.

##### Listing 16.3 A simple GitHub workflow to run a PowerShell script
    
    
    jobs:
      build:
        runs-on: ubuntu-latest
        steps:
          - uses: actions/checkout@master
          - name: build
            working-directory: cicd
            run: ./build.ps1
            shell: pwsh

This deliberately simple workflow takes the approach I like—the interesting logic is all in PowerShell, and as we’ve seen, you can run it locally with Docker as the only dependency. If we needed to move this build to Azure DevOps, then only the YAML would need to change. But let’s run it in GitHub—we’ll push our breaking unit test too and confirm that the build fails.

##### Try it now

GitHub looks for workflow YAML files in the `.github/workflows` folder, so to trigger the build, you only need to copy the workflow file to the right place and push the repository:
    
    
    # move the build workflow file:
    mv ../.github/workflows.ignore/build.yaml ../.github/workflows/
    # commit and push the changes:
    git add --all; git commit -m 'Add build workflow'; git push
    # browse to this URL to see your build in the cloud:
    echo "https:/./github.com/$ghId/ch16/actions"

I hope you’re pleasantly surprised at how simple this has been and how seamlessly GitHub Actions fit in with normal development practices. Just a few pages in, and we have a distributed application written in different languages ready to build in the cloud. Yes, the build has failed, as you can see in figure 16.5, but we did that deliberately, and soon we’ll have it working correctly.

##### Figure 16.5 Triggering a build in GitHub that uses logic in the PowerShell script

When we remove the failing test and push again—our equivalent of fixing a coding error or a bad test setup—it will trigger a new workflow run that succeeds.

##### Try it now

Rename the failing test so that it gets ignored, and then push your changes. I’m using the GitHub CLI to print the build status, but you can use the web UI and you’ll see the same:
    
    
    # rename the failing test:
    mv ../src/access-log/src/failing.test.js `
     ../src/access-log/src/failing.test.js.ignore
    # commit and push your changes:
    git add --all; git commit -m 'Fix test'; git push
    # check the new build in GitHub

You may feel like you’re not doing much in this chapter so far—but really that’s the point. The Dockerfiles are doing the heavy lifting, and there’s nothing in there that you don’t already know. The PowerShell scripts and YAML workflows are really just scaffolding to structure the Docker processes, and you don’t have to dig deep into those if you don’t want to. In a typical DevOps delivery, the team members will have their own focus, and you’ll be the star of the show if you can do the complex work in containers and make it easy on everyone else. Figure 16.6 shows the build working now that the bad test has been removed.

##### Figure 16.6 Triggering a GitHub action by pushing changes and fixing the build

This is the continuous integration (CI) part of the build. _Integration_ here means taking the complete code for a product, which may be delivered as many different components by many different teams, and building it all together. You do that so those teams have one view of the build where they can see quickly if a change in their area causes an issue somewhere else. Next is continuous deployment (CD), which means packaging for distribution and being confident that the app you’ve packaged will work when you deploy it.

## 16.2 Pushing images to the GitHub Container Registry

One of the main attractions of GitHub as a CI/CD provider is that it gives you everything in one place: source code management, a build service, and an image registry (not to mention project management, issue tracking, identity, permissions, and everything else). The GitHub Container Registry (GHCR) follows the same billing approach as GitHub Actions—it’s free to push and pull public packages (with a restriction on data storage). You can pay for subscriptions that give you more build runner minutes and more image storage gigabytes for private repositories, but the free quotas are generous enough for a lot of projects.

The next stage in our CI/CD pipeline is to build the Docker images for each component and store them in GHCR in a _staging_ repository. This is a just a normal image repository, but we’re using it as a temporary storage location for images that are unproven because they’ve only passed the unit tests. Once we have images in staging, we can use them to run end-to-end tests, and if those tests pass, we know the images are good and we can push them to a release repository. When we deploy to production, it will be from the release repository. Figure 16.7 shows the full pipeline, where each section will be implemented as a separate GitHub workflow.

##### Figure 16.7 The full multi-platform CI/CD pipeline we’ll build out in this chapter

In our build, the staging repository will be public, but in a real project, it could be restricted to the build process and the dev teams. Every image will be tagged with a build number, and labels in the image will store an audit trail for the build. The script to build and push images is very simple—it uses Compose files and is pretty much just `docker compose build` and `docker compose push`. There are some nice touches in the Compose files that layer on the functionality we need while keeping the files easy to read. Listing 16.4 shows parts of the Compose specification in different files for the Access Log component.

##### Listing 16.4 Environment variables in layered Docker Compose files
    
    
    # docker-compose.yml - the basic spec:
    access-log:
      image: ch16-access-log:2e
      ports:
        - "8012:80"
      networks:
        - app-net
    
    # docker-compose-build.yml - the build section:
    access-log:
      build:
        context: ./access-log
        args:
          BUILD_ID: ${BUILD_ID}
          COMMIT_SHA: ${COMMIT_SHA}
    
    # docker-compose-build-tags.yml - image reference variables:
    access-log:
      image: ${REGISTRY}/${REPOSITORY}/staging/
             ch16-access-log:
             2e-${DOCKER_BUILD_OS}${OS_VERSION_TAG}-
             ${DOCKER_BUILD_CPU}-${BUILD_NUMBER}

Anytime you’re working on a build pipeline, you need to think about two audiences: the developer side and the operations side. Developers may want to run the app locally using Docker Compose, and if they need to tweak the configuration, they don’t want a 100-line YAML file to trawl through. So, you isolate different parts of the Compose model in different files: the basic file is good for running `docker compose up`; the build file adds in the build context and arguments; and the tags file adds the full image reference, which is populated from environment variables. Operations people can work on the bits they care about, and developers have a nice clean Compose file for local running.

The same is true for the build scripts. It’s courteous to structure them so that they can run in a local environment that may not have all the features of the cloud build. The next script we’ll run builds and pushes images to the staging repository—but we’re not running a local registry, so we can pass a flag telling it to do the build part without the push.

##### Try it now

Run the script for the next workflow locally—it uses the same Dockerfiles but completes all stages, not finishing at the unit tests. After the build, check the labels of one of your new images:
    
    
    # run the build script without pushing images:
    ./build-push.ps1 -NoPush
    # list images with the staging repo:
    docker image ls 'registry.local:5010/diamol/staging/*:*'
    # inspect the labels of one of the images:
    $repo = 'registry.local:5010/diamol/staging'
    $image = 'ch16-image-gallery'
    $tag = '2e-linux-amd64-0'
    $reference = "${repo}/${image}:${tag}"
    docker inspect $reference `
     -f '{{json .Config.Labels }}' | ConvertFrom-Json

When you walk through this exercise, you’ll see the images are named with a long, cumbersome reference, and the labels in the image are set to default values. That’s the other thing about designing a build pipeline—a lot of the choices are down to personal preference or team expectations. I like to use the logic of the real CI/CD process as much as possible, without interrupting development—hence the long image names. There is some functionality to work out the references, and it’s good to test that locally, even if it means the names aren’t so easy to work with. Figure 16.8 shows my output; in your own pipelines, you might prefer to use a simple tag for local builds.

##### Figure 16.8 The next build stage is a script that builds and tags images for a staging repository.

Time to run this in GitHub. The app will be published with support for Windows and Linux users. The build scripts and Dockerfiles are all cross-platform, so if you run `build-push.ps1` on a Linux machine, you’ll get Linux images, and on Windows, you’ll get Windows images. GitHub Actions provides runners for both OSs (only on Intel CPUs at the time of writing—Arm64 support is on its way). We have two workflows to build and push the staging images; they use the same PowerShell script and set environment variables from GitHub to set the image references. You can explore the definitions in `package-staging.yaml` and `package-staging-windows.yaml`; the only difference between the Linux and Windows version is the type of the runner.

##### Try it now

There are two staging workflows—if the Linux run succeeds, then it triggers the Windows runs. Move them both to the correct folder, and push your changes to kick off the builds:
    
    
    # move the staging workflow definitions:
    mv ../.github/workflows.ignore/package-staging*.yaml ../.github/workflows/
    # push to your fork:
    git add --all; git commit -m 'Add staging workflows'; git push
    # list all the workflows:
    gh workflow list --repo "$ghId/ch16"
    # print the status of the Linux staging workflow:
    gh workflow view package-staging --repo "$ghId/ch16"
    # open this link to see your images stored in GHCR:
    $repo="https:/./github.com/$ghId/ch16"
    $pkg='pkgs/container/staging%2Fch16-image-gallery'
    echo "$repo/$pkg"

These workflows are triggered on push but also from a successful completion of the build workflow. It takes longer to run the staging workflow, but it won’t execute if the build workflow fails, so we’re keeping that philosophy of failing fast and each stage doing as little as it needs to. Figure 16.9 shows my staging workflow running on Linux.

##### Figure 16.9 Pushing the workflow files triggers the Linux action run.

The Windows workflow takes longer still to run—partly because it takes more time for the Windows runner to be ready and partly because the Windows base images are larger, so they take more time to pull. Again, the Windows workflow is triggered only if the Linux workflow succeeds; they do the same thing on different platforms, so if it fails on Linux, we don’t want to waste time waiting for it to fail on Windows too. Figure 16.10 shows my Windows build in progress and the staging images when they’ve been pushed by both workflows.

Your GitHub repository is public, so your images in GHCR default to being public too. You might not want that for your staging images, although a lot of open source projects do, so the community can test new releases and provide feedback. You authenticate to private registries in GHCR with the usual `docker login` process, but you need to generate a token from GitHub. As these images are public, anyone can pull them without authenticating.

##### Figure 16.10 GitHub Actions gives you a multi-platform build farm in the cloud—for free.

##### Try it now

Pull one of your staging images from GHCR to confirm that it’s publicly accessible, and then check the labels in the image:
    
    
    # no need to authenticate for public images:
    $ref = "ghcr.io/$ghId/staging/ch16-image-gallery:2e-linux-amd64"
    docker pull $ref
    # inspect the labels to see the values set in the workflow:
    docker inspect $ref `
     -f '{{json .Config.Labels }}' | ConvertFrom-Json

This is the Go component that is packaged on a minimal base image, so the pull will be fast. Compare the image reference and labels to your local image, and you’ll see that GitHub Actions provides additional context, which we flow down into the Dockerfiles and Compose files. The labels in the image identify the workflow that built it and the commit ID of the source code—you see mine in figure 16.11.

##### Figure 16.11 Pulling a workflow-built image from the GitHub Container Registry

Now, we have images stored in the staging repository for all components. To get that far, we’ve passed these quality gates: the source code compiles, the unit tests run successfully, and the Docker image builds. That doesn’t mean the application isn’t complete junk, though. There could be a missing configuration file, or the name of the application binary might be wrong, so the container will fail to start. The next quality gate is to smoke-test the whole application and prove that it starts, runs, and responds to requests.

## 16.3 End-to-end tests with Docker Compose

End-to-end testing means starting your whole application and running an automated test suite to validate the key user journeys. It used to be a time-consuming and expensive business. You might need to spin up several application components together with a database and a message queue for communication. You need infrastructure to run it all, and if you’ve got the infrastructure, you may as well leave the testing environment up the whole time, which encourages manual deployment and configuration changes while running the risk of everything getting out-of-date and out of sync. Previously, lots of organizations would skip automated end-to-end testing altogether because it was just too complex to set up and maintain—they’d wait until they had a release candidate and deploy it to an environment for users to test.

No more! With cloud CI/CD services and the ability to model and run your entire application in containers, there’s no excuse not to run an automated end-to-end test suite on every build. That stage takes longer to run than the others, but with a well-structured pipeline like ours, it will only run when we already have a good level of confidence in the build. Our end-to-end tests will be minimal, they’re really just smoke tests to make sure the app holds together. The smoke-test script uses Docker Compose to start the app, makes some web requests to verify the app does at least do something, and then shuts it all down.

##### Try it now

Run the smoke-test script locally—it will use the staging images when it runs in the cloud, but the local flag supports developers using their own image builds:
    
    
    # the flag means we use local images:
    ./smoke-test.ps1 -Local

Running the test script with the local flag uses the images you’ve built already in the chapter. You can run it again without that flag, and it will pull the latest images from the staging repository. As before, this is about supporting the developer workflow. If you’ve made a local change that you want to test, then you’ll use your own images, but if you’re trying to reproduce an issue reported by a tester, then you can use the staging images. Figure 16.12 shows my output, where the single command to run a PowerShell script does an awful lot of work, thanks to Docker.

##### Figure 16.12 Running smoke tests to prove the application starts and runs

These are very basic tests, but remember it’s the pipeline we’re focusing on here. A more complete set of tests could use Behavior-Driven Development (BDD) to define expectations in human language and be executed with a testing framework and a simulated browser. Our build can easily support those tests by just adding a command to the smoke-test script. We’ve structured the build to make it easy to extend. A more complex app might also need a lot of CPU or memory to run everything in containers. GitHub Actions runners have a reasonable specification even in the free tier (4 CPU cores and 16 GB RAM); in a paid plan, you can use larger runners to meet your app’s requirements, but the standard runners are fine for our purposes.

##### Try it now

We have staging images for Linux and Windows containers, and the smoke-test script is cross-platform. Move the workflow YAML files to the correct location, and push the repository to trigger both Linux and Windows workflow runs:
    
    
    # move the two smoke test workflows:
    mv ../.github/workflows.ignore/smoke-test*.yaml ../.github/workflows/
    # commit and push changes:
    git add --all; git commit -m 'Add smoke test workflows'; git push
    # list the workflows if you have the CLI:
    gh workflow list --repo "$ghId/ch16"
    # check progress - or use the Web:
    gh workflow view smoke-test --repo "$ghId/ch16"
    gh workflow view smoke-test-windows --repo "$ghId/ch16"

As always, the Windows run takes longer than on Linux—figure 16.13 shows that my Windows workflow is still running—but after a few minutes, both will be green. That’s the next quality gate, which shows that the application works as expected on both our supported platforms.

Now that we have confidence in the build, we can push those images to the release repository. That’s where the production deployment will get its images, and it could also be where we apply policies such as security scanning or image signing. It’s important we don’t rebuild the images from source, just in case any changes have occurred since the staging images were pushed. That’s possible with updates to base images or library references—the same commit could produce a different Docker image on the next build. We’ve tested these images, so these are the exact images we want to promote to the release repository. As you know, we can do that by pulling the staging images, giving them a new tag, and pushing them again.

##### Figure 16.13 Running smoke tests on Linux and Windows in GitHub

##### Try it now

We have two more workflows to push here. These use a script to pull the latest staging images, tag them with the release repository name, and push them:
    
    
    # move the release workflows:
    mv ../.github/workflows.ignore/package-release*.yaml `
     ../.github/workflows/
    # push changes to trigger the build:
    git add --all; git commit -m 'Add release workflows'; git push
    # list the workflows:
    gh workflow list --repo "$ghId/ch16"
    # monitor the progress and check your packages in GitHub

If you look a bit more closely behind the scenes, you’ll see the release workflows call a script that uses the staging Compose file to pull the images and then uses the release Compose file to push the images. The tagging uses some simple PowerShell to render the Compose specifications as JSON and then match the source and target image references. If you’re still unhappy about installing PowerShell on your Mac or Linux box, just take a look at `tag-push.ps1` and consider how you would do the same thing in a Linux shell script. You don’t need to dig into that, though—just know that the Compose files centralize the image-naming scheme and wait for your GitHub build to finish to see what you get, which will look like figure 16.14.

##### Figure 16.14 Tagging the staging images and pushing them to the release repository

That’s very nearly it. We’ve built our application components from source for different platforms, tested them at different levels, and packaged them for distribution. The final stage is deploying to a production (or production-like) environment.

## 16.4 Deploying to Kubernetes

One of the biggest advantages to using Docker for your CI/CD process is making your test environments as similar as possible to production. All of our components are packaged in Docker images, which means we won’t get any missing dependencies or mismatched runtimes when we deploy. We’ve tested everything in Docker Compose, so we’ve proved that the application components work correctly together. But our production deployment is unlikely to use Docker Compose because that just gives us one machine with zero reliability or scalability. So, we’ve proved the app works using the wrong platform, which is a compromise: Compose apps start and run quickly with minimal compute requirements, and we already have the model that developers use locally. Production is likely to be Kubernetes though, so we really need to model the app with the Kubernetes API and test that deployment too.

This is another decision that often comes down to individual preference and the project’s assets. If you have Kubernetes clusters running all the time with auto-scale configured, you may prefer to deploy to them for the smoke-testing stage of your pipeline so that you get as close to production as early as possible in the workflow. In our case, we’re leaving Kubernetes to the end, and our final stage will create a temporary cluster on the GitHub runner, just to test that the model deploys from the release images without any errors. Listing 16.5 shows how easy that is using a community-supported GitHub Action.

##### Listing 16.5 Running a test Kubernetes cluster with k3d
    
    
    # this creates a single-node Kubernetes cluster in a container:
    - name: create-cluster
      uses: AbsaOSS/k3d-action@v2
      with:
        cluster-name: ch16
        args: >-
          --agents 1
          --no-lb
          --k3s-arg "--no-deploy=traefik,servicelb,metrics-server"
    
    # and this sets the Kubectl CLI to use that cluster:
    - name: set-context
      run: |
        kubectl config use-context k3d-ch16

K3d (<https://k3d.io>) is a helpful tool for running Kubernetes test environments. It uses Docker to run Kubernetes nodes in containers, which access the Docker Engine they’re running on to schedule Pods. You can create multi-node clusters too and use them to test that your Pods are distributed across nodes and that your app communicates correctly. The community GitHub Action wraps up the k3d tool, so you don’t need to install and configure it as part of your workflow. The arguments are definitely esoteric, but they’re just setting up a simple one-node cluster without a load balancer or any of the other defaults that k3d installs.

You can’t run this part of the workflow locally because that k3d setup is all in the custom GitHub Action. I could have scripted the k3d usage, but that would have meant installing another tool, and this stage is only indicative of a real deployment. The Kubernetes model uses Helm, and the `deploy.ps1` script sets the values for the image references to use the release repositories. If you developed a taste for Kubernetes from chapter 15, then the chart in the `helm/iotd` folder is a very simple introduction to Helm. GitHub Actions runners have the Kubectl and Helm command lines installed, so we just need to push the final workflow definition to complete our pipeline.

##### Try it now

Add the deployment workflow to your repository, and watch the build in the GitHub UI to see how it runs:
    
    
    # move the deploy workflow:
    mv ../.github/workflows.ignore/deploy.yaml ../.github/workflows/
    # push changes:
    git add --all; git commit -m 'Add deploy workflow'; git push

What a world we live in—this workflow uses an open source GitHub Action to deploy an open source tool to create a Kubernetes cluster, running in Docker on a managed service in the cloud for which we aren’t paying a single penny. Then, it deploys the Kubernetes model for our application, which spins up in Pods that pull the images we just pushed to the GHCR. My output in figure 16.15 shows the CD stage (albeit to an environment that is then immediately destroyed).

##### Figure 16.15 Simulating a production deployment by deploying to a local Kubernetes cluster

We’ve built up the entire pipeline now and tested each stage individually, so the final step is to make sure the whole thing runs when we push a source code change. GitHub workflows can have multiple trigger events—typically you want to trigger a run if the workflow definition itself changes and then you use other triggers to model out your process. (Remember that process from figure 16.7?) Our first workflow will trigger from a push to the repository, and then each subsequent workflow triggers from the successful completion of its predecessor. The Windows workflows can run in parallel with the Linux ones, and the final deployment is only dependent on the Linux release images being pushed.

##### Try it now

Make a change to the HTML page in the Go application, and push it to your repository. Watch the Actions page in the web UI to see all the workflows triggering in the correct order, finishing with the dummy deployment to Kubernetes:
    
    
    # overwrite one source file:
    mv -fo ../src/image-gallery/index.html.ignore `
     ../src/image-gallery/index.html
    # push the changes:
    git add --all; git commit -m 'Acknowledge APOD'; git push
    # watch the pipeline unfold:
    echo "https:/./github.com/$ghId/ch16/actions"

It takes a few minutes for the full pipeline to run, and most of that time is spent on the Windows workflows. Quite likely, your Linux builds and deployment will have completed while the Windows parts are still running—as mine did in figure 16.16. Many applications won’t need multi-platform support, so you can do without those stages in your build. But if you write your Dockerfiles and build scripts to be cross-platform, then it will be very easy to add Windows, Arm64, or RISC-V image builds to your pipeline.

And that is all it takes to create a fully fledged CI/CD process with Docker and Kubernetes in the cloud. I deliberately kept the focus on the Docker parts and the overall approach rather than dig into GitHub workflow syntax and PowerShell, but you have all those assets in the repository that you can use as the basis for your own pipelines.

##### Figure 16.16 The full CI/CD pipeline, triggered from a change to the source code

## 16.5 Structuring a portable build process

The pipeline we built out in this chapter isn’t so far removed from a real production CI/CD setup. The biggest difference for commercial projects is that the GitHub repository and packages will be private. That takes some more configuration in the workflows to authenticate, but it’s all pretty standard stuff, and you’ll find that GitHub Actions are well documented. Then, there are some design choices—you might prefer to have a single workflow with all the stages defined in one place. That makes it easier to model out dependencies and to see them in the UI because you get a graph of different jobs in a workflow, but that method also makes it harder to run separate stages if you need to. Other than that, this is a pretty good starter project for the next time you need to get a build going in GitHub Actions (for any other CI/CD service, you’ll just need to write the YAML). Here’s my advice for building pipelines that take advantage of the platforms without sacrificing portability:

  * _Do as much as you can in Docker._ You would expect that advice in this book, but it’s a universally good approach. Compile code and run unit tests in your Dockerfile, and you have no dependencies to install to build the app and gain some confidence that all is well. Model your app in Compose, and you can easily spin up a smoke-test environment and centralize your image naming scheme. 
  * _Do everything else in scripts._ Isolate each build stage, and you’ll have a set of simple, manageable scripts that are mostly running familiar Docker commands. The role of the scripts is to coordinate work and act as an intermediatory between Docker and the build service—transforming pipeline context such as the build number into environment variables used by Docker and Compose. 
  * _Support both the developer workflow and the operations workflow._ The pipeline will mainly run in the CI/CD service, but if you keep your scripts flexible, then they’re good for developers to use too. Being able to build code and run tests using the exact same infrastructure as the build service should eliminate situations where developers check in failing tests and break the build—or at least give you a good justification to shout at them (or encourage them, depending on your team culture). 
  * _Don’t fall for all the shiny things the build service offers._ Any proprietary features you use will make it harder to move to another provider. I can’t think of a single long-term project I’ve worked on that hasn’t moved between services eventually, and those that had scripted builds were the easiest to migrate. You need to be pragmatic and not spend project time building things the service does for free, but you should try to keep provider-specific features to a minimum. 



And remember—this is just my opinion. You may not choose to follow my recommendations, or you may not have the option to do so, but do try to bear in mind the guiding principles. They will help you, whichever path you take.

## 16.6 Lab

One thing missing from this super-duper CI/CD pipeline is that we build images for different platforms, but we don’t publish them as a set of multi-platform images. You can push manifest lists to GHCR, so this lab gets you to do that final step:

  * Write a script that creates and pushes a manifest list for each of the three components in this chapter’s app. 
  * Each manifest list should contain Linux and Windows variants for the Intel (AMD64) CPU architecture. 
  * Your script should follow the same approach as the others, using Compose files as the source of image references. 
  * When you’ve tested your script locally, write a GitHub Actions workflow to run the script. 



My solution is in the lab folder of the ch16 repository. The documentation is in the README file, which even has pictures to prove that the solution works: <https://mng.bz/lZZj>.


# 17 Optimizing your Docker images for size, speed, and security

Once your apps are containerized with a continuous integration (CI)/continuous deployment (CD) process to deploy to a container platform, you may think you’re good to go to production, but there are some best practices you still need to invest time in. Optimizing your Docker images is one of the most important because you need your builds and deployments to be fast, your application content to be secure, and your evenings free to call your own—you don’t want to be paged at 2 a.m. when your build service runners are out of disk space. The Dockerfile syntax is small and intuitive, but it hides some complexity that you need to understand to make the most of your image builds. 

This chapter will walk you through the finer details of the image format so that you know how and why to optimize it. We’ll be building on chapter 3, where you learned that Docker images are actually merged from multiple image layers.

## 17.1 How you optimize Docker images 

The Docker image format is already heavily optimized. Layers are shared between images wherever possible, which reduces build times, network traffic, and disk usage. But Docker has a conservative approach toward data, and it doesn’t automatically remove images that you’ve pulled—that’s something you need to do explicitly. So, when you replace containers to update your application, Docker will download the new image layers, but it won’t remove any of the old image layers. It’s easy for your disk to get swallowed up with lots of old image layers, especially on development or test machines that are regularly updating.

##### Try it now

You can see how much disk space your images are physically using with the `system` `df` command, which also shows container, volume, and build cache disk usage:
    
    
    docker system df

If you’ve never cleared out old images from your Docker Engine, you’ll probably be surprised at the results. My output is in figure 17.1 from a Windows machine running two Docker Engines. In total, I have more than 100 images using 90 GB of storage, even though I’m not running any containers.

##### Figure 17.1 It’s easy to see your disk swallowed up by Docker images you’re not even using.

This example is a mild one—I’ve seen unloved servers that have been running Docker for years, wasting hundreds of gigabytes on unused images. It’s a good habit to run `docker` `system` `prune` regularly—it clears out image layers and the build cache without removing full images. You can run it with a scheduled job to remove unused layers, but if your images are optimized, it will be less of a problem. Optimizing parts of your technology stack is often a cyclical process with many small improvements, but with Docker, it’s very easy to make big improvements by following some simple best practices.

The first is not to include files in your image unless you need them. It sounds obvious, but you’ll often write a Dockerfile that copies in a whole folder structure without realizing that the folder includes documentation, images, or other binaries that aren’t needed at runtime. Being explicit about the files you copy can be the first big saving you make. Compare the Dockerfiles in listing 17.1—the first example copies in a whole folder, whereas the second example realizes the copy added some extra files and includes a new step to delete them.

##### Listing 17.1 Trying to optimize a Dockerfile by removing files
    
    
    # Dockerfile v1 – copies in the whole directory structure:
    FROM diamol/base:2e
    CMD echo app- && ls app && echo docs- && ls docs
    COPY . .
    
    # Dockerfile v2 – adds a new step to delete unused files
    FROM diamol/base:2e
    CMD echo app- && ls app && echo docs- && ls docs
    COPY . . 
    RUN rm -rf docs

In the v2 Dockerfile, you’d think the image size would be smaller because it deletes the extra `docs` folder, but that’s not how image layers work. The image is a merge of all the layers, so the files still exist from the `COPY` layer; they just get hidden in the deleted layer, so the total image size doesn’t shrink.

##### Try it now

Build both examples, and compare the sizes:
    
    
    # switch to the source folder:
    cd ch17/exercises/build-context
    # build the first image:
    docker image build -t diamol/ch17-build-context:2e-v1 .
    # build the supposedly optimized image:
    docker image build -t diamol/ch17-build-context:2e-v2 `
     -f ./Dockerfile.v2 .
    # compare the sizes:
    docker image ls "diamol/ch17-build-context*"

You’ll find that the v2 image is exactly the same size as the v1 image, as if the `rm` command to delete the folder hadn’t been run at all. You can see my output in figure 17.2—I’m using Linux containers, so the sizes are tiny, but almost half of the size is from unnecessary files in the `docs` folder.

##### Figure 17.2 Surprise! Deleting files doesn’t reduce the image size if the delete is in its own layer.

Each instruction in a Dockerfile produces an image layer, and layers are merged together to form the whole image. If you write files in a layer, those files are permanently there; if you delete them in a subsequent layer, all Docker does is hide them in the filesystem. This is a fundamental thing to understand when you come to image optimization—it’s no good trying to remove bloat in later layers—you need to optimize every layer. You can see the layers and their sizes by printing the history of the image.

##### Try it now

The merged filesystem in Docker obscures inefficient image builds. Print the image history to show the physical structure of the layers:
    
    
    # run a container from the v1 image:
    docker run -it diamol/ch17-build-context:2e-v1
    # the docs folder is not in the v2 image:
    docker run -it diamol/ch17-build-context:2e-v2
    # check the history for the v2 image:
    docker history diamol/ch17-build-context:2e-v2

The layer history is a useful way to see the breakdown of the size of your image. When you run containers from these two images, you’ll see that the files aren’t accessible in the v2 build—but the history shows the `COPY` layer adding the large files. Once data is in a layer, there’s no way to remove it from the image. Figure 17.3 shows my output—you can see the same layer information if you look at the image details for the `2e-v2` tag on Docker Hub (<https://mng.bz/Bzzv>).

##### Figure 17.3 The merged filesystem hides deleted files, but the layers are still part of the image.

This is the first point about optimizing—don’t copy anything into the image that you don’t need to run the app. Even if you try to delete it in later instructions, it will still be there somewhere in the image stack, taking up disk space. It’s much better to be precise in your `COPY` instructions to bring only the files you want into the image. That makes for smaller image sizes and also a more clearly documented installation in your Dockerfile. Listing 17.2 shows the optimized v3 Dockerfile for this simple app—the only change from v1 is that it copies the `app` subfolder rather than the whole of the directory.

##### Listing 17.2 Optimized Dockerfile to only copy necessary files
    
    
    FROM diamol/base
    CMD echo app- && ls app && echo docs- && ls docs
    COPY ./app ./app

If you build this, you’ll see that both the image size and the build context are smaller. Docker compresses the build context (the folder where you run the build) and sends it to the engine along with the Dockerfile when you run a build. That’s how you can build images on a remote engine from files on your local machine. The build context often has files you don’t need—Docker used to send the whole folder, but optimizations in BuildKit (the image builder component) inspect the Dockerfile to see what content needs to be included. 

In a simple Dockerfile, you can use precise `COPY` commands and get two optimizations in one—smaller contexts speed up the build, and smaller images speed up pushing and pulling. It’s harder to do that in more complex apps where the Dockerfile is part of a nested folder structure. You can end up with lots of convoluted `COPY` instructions with peculiar file-matching patterns polluting your Dockerfile. There’s an alternative optimization that achieves the same result—you can list files and folders to exclude from the build context in a file called `.dockerignore`. Ignored content doesn’t get sent in the build context, so you don’t need to be specific in your `COPY` commands because the source folder already excludes everything you don’t want.

##### Try it now

Build from the original Dockerfile, which copies in the whole context folder, and then build it again with a `.dockerignore` file to reduce the size of the context and the image:
    
    
    # build the v3 image - don't use the cache:
    docker build -t diamol/ch17-build-context:2e-v3 --no-cache .
    # now rename the already prepared ignore file and check the contents:
    mv dockerignore.txt .dockerignore
    cat .dockerignore
    # run the same build again with a new tag:
    docker build -t diamol/ch17-build-context:2e-v4 --no-cache .

You’ll see that in the first build command, Docker sends 2 MB of build context to the Engine. That’s the uncompressed size of the files in that folder—most of which is a 2 MB picture of a whale. In the second build, there’s a `.dockerignore` file in the current directory that tells Docker to exclude the `docs` folder, so the build context is only 190 bytes. If you then list the images, you’ll see the `v4` tag is 2 MB smaller than the others, so we’ve finally achieved the correct optimization. You can see my output in figure 17.4.

##### Figure 17.4 Using a `.dockerignore` file reduces the size of the build context and the time to send it.

A `.dockerignore` file can save you a lot of time when you count the cost of sending unused data in the build context, and it can save space, even when you’re using explicit paths in your Dockerfile.
