For example, if you’re building the code locally and also using a multi-stage build to compile in Docker, you can specify the build binaries in your `.dockerignore` file and be sure the local build output doesn’t get copied into the image. The file format is broadly the same as Git’s `.gitignore` file, and you can use the template for your app platform from GitHub as a good starting point (you should include the Git history folder `.git` if your Dockerfile is at the root of the repository too). Now that you’ve seen the importance of managing which files make it into your Docker image, we’re going to take a step back and look at the image you’re using as a base.

## 17.2 Choosing the right base images

Base image choice is as much about security as it is about disk space and network transfer time. If your base OS image is large, it probably has all sorts of tools that might be useful on a real machine but can be a security hole in a container. If your OS base image has curl installed, an attacker could use that to download malware or upload your data to their servers, if they manage to break out of your app into the container. 

That’s also true of application platform base images. If you’re running Java apps, the OpenJDK official image is a good base (published as `eclipse-temurin` on Docker Hub), but there are many tags with different configurations of the Java runtime (the Java Runtime Environment [JRE]) and the developer SDK (the Java Development Kit [JDK]). Table 17.1 shows the size differences between the multi-platform images for the SDK versus the runtime and the most minimal versions.

##### Table 17.1 Size differences between compatible Java 22 images on Docker Hub

eclipse-temurin  |  :22-jdk  |  :22-jre  |  :22-jre-alpine  |  :22-jre-nanoserver   
---|---|---|---|---  
|  440 MB   
|  281 MB   
|  189 MB   
|   
|  3.78 GB   
|  3.45 GB   
|  |  444 MB   
  
  
Linux users can use a 189 MB base image instead of 440 MB, and Windows users can use 444 MB instead of 3.8 GB, just by checking the variants on Docker Hub and picking the ones with the smallest OS image and the smallest Java installation. The OpenJDK team members are cautious with their multi-platform images. They select images with the widest compatibility, but it’s simple to test your app with a smaller variant. Try Alpine or Debian Slim images as the base OS for Linux containers, and try Nano Server for Windows containers (the alternative is Windows Server Core, which is pretty much the full Windows Server OS—that’s why the images take up gigabytes of disk). Not every app will work with the smaller variants, but it’s easy to switch images in your `FROM` lines and test it out.

Size isn’t just about disk space—it’s also about what’s using the space. The largest OpenJDK images include the whole Java SDK, so there’s a nice attack vector there if someone manages to compromise your container. They could write some Java source code files into the container’s disk, compile them with the SDK, and then run an app that does anything they want in the security context of your application container. 

##### Try it now

A Java app that uses the default JDK image is provided in the exercises folder for this chapter. It runs a very simple REST API that always returns the value `true`:
    
    
    # from the repository root folder:
    cd ch17/exercises/truth-app
    # build the image - the base image uses the :22-jdk tag:
    docker image build -t diamol/ch17-truth-app:2e .
    # run the app:
    docker run -d -p 8010:80 --name truth diamol/ch17-truth-app:2e
    # and try it out:
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8010/truth

The container you’re running serves a Java REST API that is compiled in the image, but the container also has all the tools to compile other Java apps. If an attacker manages to break out of the app and run arbitrary commands on the container, they could run their own code to do whatever they liked. In this image, I’ve “accidentally” included a test code file, and a malicious user could find and run that to change the app’s behavior.

##### Try it now

Simulate a container breakout by connecting to a shell in your API container. Then, use the JDK to compile and run the test code, followed by checking the app again afterward:
    
    
    # connect to the API container - for Linux containers:
    docker container exec -it truth sh
    # OR for Windows containers:
    docker container exec -it truth cmd
    # inside the container compile and run the test Java file:
    javac FileUpdateTest.java
    java FileUpdateTest
    exit
    # back on your machine, try the API again:
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8010/truth

You’ll see that the behavior of the app has changed. In the packaged image, the API call always returns `true`—but the test fixture sets the response to be `false` instead. My output in figure 17.5 shows the original response and the changed response after the “hack.”

##### Figure 17.5 Having the SDK in your app image leaves you open to arbitrary code execution attacks.

This is a slightly contrived example with the handy test file lying around in the image to make things easy, but container breakouts are possible, and this illustrates an interesting attack option. The container could be locked down by the platform to prevent network access, and this attack would still work. The lesson is that your base image should have all you need to run your app, but no extra tooling for building apps (interpreted languages such as Node.js and Python are an exception because the build tool is the runtime, so you need it for the app to run).

Golden images are one way around this problem. You have a team that chooses the right base images and builds their own versions for your organization. I use that approach for this book—my Java apps are built from `diamol/openjdk:2e`, which is a multi-platform image that uses the smallest variant for each OS. I can control how often my golden image gets updated, and I can trigger application image builds after the golden image builds. Another advantage of building your own golden image is that you can integrate additional security checks on the base layer in the build process by using a third-party tool such as Trivy.

##### Try it now

Trivy is an open source project for security scanning Docker images and other content. You can install the Trivy command line locally or run it in a container:
    
    
    # create a volume to use as a data cache:
    docker volume create trivy-cache
    # run a vulnerability scan on the book's OpenJDK image:
    docker run -v trivy-cache:/root/.cache `
     -it diamol/trivy:2e image --scanners vuln diamol/openjdk:2e
    # run the same scan but ignore low severity problems:
    docker run -v trivy-cache:/root/.cache `
     -it diamol/trivy:2e image --scanners vuln `
     --severity HIGH,CRITICAL diamol/openjdk:2e
    # and finally ignore specific problems we have accepted:
    docker run -v trivy-cache:/root/.cache `
     -it diamol/trivy:2e image --scanners vuln `
     --severity HIGH,CRITICAL --ignorefile /sample.trivyignore `
     diamol/openjdk:2e

It takes a while for Trivy to start the first scan because it downloads databases of known security problems. That’s why we create the volume—so that the data is cached and subsequent runs don’t need to download it again. Typically, you’d integrate Trivy into your CI/CD process—you can run the container as a centralized server for other builds to use—so this hit would only happen when you first deploy it, and when the databases need updating. Figure 17.6 shows my output; when I ran my scan, the ultimate base image was Alpine version 3.20.3, which had no critical vulnerabilities but had three high-severity problems.

Vulnerability databases are constantly updated, so your output will likely be different from mine. I found one high-severity problem—CVE-2024-12797—which was present in three libraries. The output from Trivy tells you if the problem has been fixed in a newer version of the component, and it includes a link describing the vulnerability. 

##### Figure 17.6 Using Trivy to analyze Docker images for known security vulnerabilities

When you find problems like this, you need to make your own decision on how to proceed. It could be critical for you, in which case, you update the vulnerable components if there is a fix or block deployments until a fix is released. Or you may decide this isn’t a problem—in this case, it was an obscure use of HTTPS that could be subject to a man-in-the-middle attack. I’m confident none of my apps are using Raw Public Keys (RPKs), which is how the exploit works, so I added the Common Vulnerabilities and Exposures (CVE) number to a `.trivyignore` file. You keep that in your source folder to suppress warnings in Trivy, which has the advantage of tracking what you suppressed over time.

Two more features of Trivy are worth using and incorporating into your build process to make sure your builds and deployments are secure. It can scan source code folders and Git repositories as well as Docker images, and it can scan for different types of problems.

##### Try it now

Use Trivy to scan the GitHub repository for chapter 16 of this book, looking for vulnerabilities in application libraries:
    
    
    # run the vulnerability scan:
    docker run -v trivy-cache:/root/.cache `
     -it diamol/trivy:2e repository --scanners vuln `
     https:/./github.com/diamol2e/ch16.git
    # run the same scan but ignore low severity problems:
    docker run -v trivy-cache:/root/.cache `
     -it diamol/trivy:2e repository --scanners vuln `
     --severity HIGH,CRITICAL https:/./github.com/diamol2e/ch16.git

Your results for this will probably be even worse than mine, as new exploits will have been found since I ran the scan. Trivy doesn’t look for security problems in your own code (you would need a static analysis tool for that, e.g., SonarQube), but it understands the format of dependency lists such as `package.json` in Node.js or the package list in a C# `.csproj` file. Those libraries will be added as binary files in your application build, and it’s the binaries that Trivy checks for vulnerabilities. The output for this app shows we have some hardening work to do before we go live; you can see in figure 17.7 that I have one problem to look at for the Node.js component and 84 high or critical vulnerabilities in my Java component.

This is good stuff to add to your CI/CD pipeline. You can supply an exit code to Trivy so that if it finds problems, the pipeline fails. In our sample pipeline in chapter 16, we could add security scanning as part of the build stage, so we don’t even push images to the staging repository if they have critical vulnerabilities. Or, if you have security specialists, you may run a separate stage for scanning, so the pipeline doesn’t fail as long as the code works, but security vulnerabilities raise a notification to another team.

The goal is to run image and repository tests regularly, build your severity and ignore filters so that Trivy reports zero failures. That means all the binaries in your base image and all the libraries in your application references are clean—or they have known problems that can’t be exploited with the way your app runs—and your build only fails when a new exploit is discovered. The last thing to get from Trivy is a scan of the deployment model for your app. You can look for bad configuration in Dockerfiles, Kubernetes manifests, Helm charts, and cloud infrastructure as code (IaC) files.

##### Try it now

Use Trivy to scan the chapter 16 GitHub repository again, this time looking for misconfigured deployment artifacts:
    
    
    # run the misconfiguration scan ignoring low severity problems:
    docker run -v trivy-cache:/root/.cache `
     -it diamol/trivy:2e repository --scanners misconfig `
     --severity HIGH,CRITICAL https:/./github.com/diamol2e/ch16.git

##### Figure 17.7 Scanning remote Git repositories with Trivy

The results of this scan should be more acceptable, and they highlight an important point about security hardening for your apps. Docker images can be easily inspected to create a Software Bill of Materials (SBOM) that lists all the binaries in the filesystem, and then it’s easy to search for exploits of those binaries. Dockerfiles and Kubernetes manifests are open specifications, so, again, it’s easy to have a set of rules to evaluate them. But when the tools find security problems, you need to be pragmatic in how you approach them. The misconfiguration scan will find some problems you need to understand—mine are in figure 17.8—but you can’t necessarily fix them.

##### Figure 17.8 Scanning for misconfigured Dockerfiles and Kubernetes manifests with Trivy

If you dig into the detail of these problems, there will be a couple of points you’ll see with your apps too. The Dockerfiles are using the _root_ administrator account, which means that you don’t get any permissions problems in your apps, but if an attacker breaks out of your container, they could be running as an admin account on your server. It’s a well-documented problem and easy to fix with a `USER` instruction in your Dockerfile, but some apps expect to run as root and will fail if they’re not. It’s the same with Kubernetes—a common recommendation is to make the container filesystem read-only, which makes it a lot harder for attackers to download tools or write scripts. But some apps need a writable filesystem and won’t even start if there isn’t one. For all of these problems, you need to apply the fix and then run a full set of end-to-end tests—your goal is to secure your deployment as much as possible, while making sure the app still actually works.

## 17.3 Minimizing image layer count and layer size

A minimal and secure base image, secure application libraries and deployment models are the prerequisites for getting your app images optimized and ready for production. The next step is really about setting up your image with everything your app needs and nothing else—which is a much deeper sentence than it sounds. Many processes for installing software leave residues behind because they cache package lists or deploy extra recommended packages. You can keep those under control; the details are different for different operating systems, but the general approach is the same.

##### Try it now

Debian Linux uses Advanced Package Tool (APT) to install software. This exercise uses a simple example to show how removing unnecessary packages and clearing out the package list provides big savings (this exercise won’t work with Windows containers):
    
    
    # switch to the exercise folder:
    cd ch17/exercises/socat
    # the v1 image installs packages using standard commands:
    docker image build -t diamol/ch17-socat:2e-v1 .
    # v2 installs the same packages but using optimization tweaks:
    docker image build -t diamol/ch17-socat:2e-v2 -f Dockerfile.v2 .
    # check the image sizes:
    docker image ls "diamol/ch17-socat*"

Both versions of the Dockerfile install the same two tools—curl and socat—on top of the same Debian Slim image, and they both have exactly the same functionality. But you’ll see that the v2 image is around 25 MB smaller as in my output in figure 17.9.

##### Figure 17.9 Optimizing software installs reduces the image size in this exercise by 20%.

I used just a couple of tweaks to the install commands to get that savings. The first makes use of an APT feature to install only the listed packages and not any recommendations. The second is to combine the install steps into a single `RUN` instruction, which ends with a command to delete the package list cache and free up that disk space. Listing 17.3 shows the difference between the Dockerfiles.

##### Listing 17.3 Optimizing software package installations 
    
    
    # Dockerfile - the naive install with APT:
    FROM debian:12-slim
    RUN apt-get update
    RUN apt-get install -y curl=7.88.1-10+deb12u8
    RUN apt-get install -y socat=1.7.4.4-2
    
    # Dockerfile.v2 - optimizing the install steps:
    FROM debian:12-slim
    RUN apt-get update \
     && apt-get install -y --no-install-recommends \
        curl=7.88.1-10+deb12u8 \
        socat=1.7.4.4-2 \
     && rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*

Another advantage of combining multiple steps in a single `RUN` instruction is that it produces a single image layer. Reducing the number of image layers isn’t really an optimization. There is a maximum layer count, but it should be plenty big enough—typically 127 depending on the OS. But having fewer layers does make it much easier to keep track of your filesystem. It would be easy to put the final `rm` command to delete the package lists into its own `RUN` instruction, which arguably makes the Dockerfile easier to read. But you know from this chapter that deleting files from a previous layer simply hides them from the filesystem, resulting in no savings on disk space.

Let’s look at one more example of this pattern, which applies across all platforms. Often, you need to download a compressed package from the internet and then expand it. It’s tempting to put the download step in a separate instruction while you’re working on the Dockerfile, so you can work with the cached download layer and speed up your development iterations. That’s fine, but once your Dockerfile is working, you need to go through and tidy up afterward to combine the download-expand-delete steps into a single instruction.

##### Try it now

Machine learning (ML) datasets are a good example here because they are large downloads that expand to even larger folder structures. In the exercises for this chapter, there’s an example that downloads a dataset from the University of California at Irvine (UCI) archives and extracts just one file from the dataset:
    
    
    # switch to the exercise folder:
    cd ch17/exercises/ml-dataset
    # v1 downloads and expands the archive then deletes unnecessary files:
    docker image build -t diamol/ch17-ml-dataset:2e-v1 .
    # v2 downloads the archive but only expands the necessary file:
    docker image build -t diamol/ch17-ml-dataset:2e-v2 -f Dockerfile.v2 .
    # compare the sizes:
    docker image ls "diamol/ch17-ml-dataset*"

You’ll see a massive size difference, which is purely because of the same optimization technique—making sure the layers don’t have any more files than they need. My results are in figure 17.10: both images have the same single file from the data download, but one is nearly 2.5 GB and the other is only 32 MB.

##### Figure 17.10 Paying close attention to how you work with files can save huge amounts of disk space.

This isn’t an overly contrived example as it’s common when you’re iterating on a Dockerfile to keep instructions separate to make it easier to debug—you can work on subsequent instructions but keep the cached download. You can’t do that when you’ve compressed multiple commands into one `RUN` instruction, but it’s important to make that optimization once you’re happy with your build. Listing 17.4 shows the optimized Dockerfile, which produces a single layer for the data file (the download URL is abbreviated here, but you’ll see it in the source code for the chapter).

##### Listing 17.4 Optimized method of downloading and extracting files
    
    
    FROM diamol/base:2e
    
    ARG DATASET_URL=https:/./archive.ics.uci.edu/.../url_svmlight.tar.gz
    
    WORKDIR /dataset
    
    RUN wget -O dataset.tar.gz ${DATASET_URL} && \
        tar -xf dataset.tar.gz url_svmlight/Day1.svm && \
        rm -f dataset.tar.gz

The biggest savings here isn’t actually from deleting the archive; it’s from extracting just the single file. The v1 approach expands the whole archive (where the 2 GB of disk space goes) and then deletes all the files except the desired one. Knowing how your tools behave and which features minimize disk usage helps you keep your layer size under control, as you’ve seen with the tar tool in this example and the APT tool in the previous one.

There’s an alternative approach to this scenario that gives you the best developer workflow and an optimized final image. That method is using multi-stage Dockerfiles with separate stages for all the disk-hungry steps.

## 17.4 Taking your multi-stage builds to the next level

You first saw multi-stage builds in chapter 4 where we used one stage to compile an app from source code and then used a later stage to package the compiled binaries for runtime. Multi-stage Dockerfiles should be a best practice for all but the simplest images because they make it far easier to optimize the final image. 

We can revisit the dataset downloader and use separate stages for each of the steps. Listing 17.5 shows that we get a much more readable Dockerfile that way (the download URL is abbreviated again).

##### Listing 17.5 Multi-stage Dockerfiles simplify optimization
    
    
    FROM diamol/base:2e AS base
    
    FROM base AS download
    ARG DATASET_URL=https:/./archive.ics.uci.edu/.../url_svmlight.tar.gz
    RUN wget -O dataset.tar.gz ${DATASET_URL} 
    
    FROM base AS expand
    COPY --from=download dataset.tar.gz .
    RUN tar xvzf dataset.tar.gz
    
    FROM base
    WORKDIR /dataset/url_svmlight
    COPY --from=expand url_svmlight/Day1.svm .

It’s clear in each stage what you’re doing, and you don’t need to dive into unusual command optimizations to save disk space because the final image will only have the files explicitly copied in from earlier stages. When you build v3, you’ll find it’s the same size as the optimized v2, but it has the advantage of being easy to debug. Multi-stage Dockerfiles can be built up to a specific stage, so if you need to check the filesystem partway through the build, you can easily do so.

##### Try it now

The `target` parameters let you stop a multi-stage build at a specific stage. Try building that v3 image with different targets:
    
    
    # switch to the exercise folder:
    cd ch17/exercises/ml-dataset
    # build the full v3 image:
    docker image build -t diamol/ch17-ml-dataset:2e-v3 -f Dockerfile.v3 .
    # build to the 'download' target - same Dockerfile, different tag:
    docker image build -t diamol/ch17-ml-dataset:2e-v3-download `
     -f Dockerfile.v3 --target download .
    # and build to the 'expand' target:
    docker image build -t diamol/ch17-ml-dataset:2e-v3-expand `
     -f Dockerfile.v3 --target expand .
    # check the image sizes:
    docker image ls "diamol/ch17-ml-dataset:2e-v3*"

Now, you’ll have three variations of the v3 image. The full build is the same 32 MB as the optimized build, so we haven’t lost any optimization by moving to a multi-stage Dockerfile. The other variants stop the build at specific stages, and you can run a container from one of those images to navigate the filesystem if you need to debug. The stage builds also show where the disk space is going—you can see in figure 17.11 that the download is around 260 MB and expands to nearly 2.5 GB.

##### Figure 17.11 Building multi-stage Dockerfiles to specific stages lets you debug contents and check sizes.

This really is the best approach—you get an optimized image, but you can keep your Dockerfile instructions simple because you don’t need to clean up the disk in intermediate stages. 

One final advantage of multi-stage builds really brings it home: every stage has its own build cache. If you need to tweak the build and edit the Dockerfile for the `expand` stage, then when you run the build, the `download` stage will still come from the cache. Maximizing the build cache is the final part of optimization, which is all about the speed of building the image. 

The basic way to make the most of the build cache is to order the instructions in your Dockerfile so that the things that change least frequently are at the start and the things that change most frequently are toward the end. This can take a few iterations to get right because you need to understand how often the steps change, but you can typically put static setup items such as exposed ports, environment variables, and the application entry point at the beginning of the file. Things that change most are your application binaries and configuration files, and they can go toward the end. Get this right, and you can drastically reduce build times.

##### Try it now

This exercise builds a minimal Jenkins install. Although the install is incomplete, so you can’t run it, it makes a good demonstration. The Dockerfile downloads the Jenkins Java file and sets up the initial configuration. The v2 Dockerfile makes good use of the cache, which you’ll see when you make a content change:
    
    
    # switch to the jenkins folder:
    cd ch17/exercises/jenkins
    # build the v1 image and the optimized v2 image:
    docker build -t diamol/ch17-jenkins:2e-v1 .
    docker build -t diamol/ch17-jenkins:2e-v2 -f Dockerfile.v2 .
    # now change the config file both Dockerfiles use:
    echo 2.0 > jenkins.install.UpgradeWizard.state
    # repeat the builds and see how long they run:
    docker build -t diamol/ch17-jenkins:2e-v1 .
    docker build -t diamol/ch17-jenkins:2e-v2 -f Dockerfile.v2 .

The second round of builds is where the cache comes in. The v1 Dockerfile copies the configuration file into the image before downloading the Jenkins file (a 90 MB download), so when the configuration file changes, the cache busts and the download happens all over again. The v2 Dockerfile uses a multi-stage build and orders the instructions to put the configuration file copy last. I ran my exercise using the `Measure-Command` function in PowerShell to check the duration of each build (there’s an equivalent called `time` in Linux). You can see in figure 17.12 that correctly ordering instructions and using a multi-stage Dockerfile cuts the build time from nearly 10 seconds to just 1 second.

##### Figure 17.12 Ordering your Dockerfile instructions correctly can mean huge savings in build time.

Making good use of the cache lets you build and push Docker images from every change to source control without soaking up time in the CI/CD pipeline. You do need to make sure you don’t over-cache things, though, because if you install or download software using `RUN` instructions, they will be cached until the instruction changes in the Dockerfile (assuming the cache isn’t busted before that instruction). You should always use explicit versions when you add packages to your image so that you know exactly what you’re running and can choose when to update. The socat example in listing 17.3 used explicit version numbers in the APT commands, and the Jenkins example used an `ARG` instruction for the version to download—both approaches let you use the cache until you change the versions to install.

There’s one final aspect to layer optimization, which is the exact opposite of what we’ve covered so far. Usually, you want to optimize your image into a small number of layers, but sometimes that over-optimization hurts performance. There are scenarios where you build an image that stores a lot of data—I have clients who build database images they use in end-to-end tests containing gigabytes of data files. If you have an image with one very big layer, then you don’t benefit from parallelism when Docker pushes and pulls that image. The Docker Engine downloads multiple layers concurrently when you pull an image (the default is three, but you can change this in the Engine configuration). When you have all your data in one 6 GB layer, then you get no parallelism, and it would perform better if you split the data files across many layers.

Listing 17.6 shows a variation of the ML dataset download. In this case, we want all the data files for days 70–79 in the final image. For comparison, we have a stage called `one-layer` where we copy the files into a single layer and an alternative stage called `multi-layer` where we copy each file into its own layer.

##### Listing 17.6 Copying large files in one layer or multiple layers
    
    
    # other stages - base, download & expand - are as before
    
    FROM base AS one-layer
    WORKDIR /dataset/url_svmlight
    COPY --from=expand url_svmlight/Day7?.svm .
    
    FROM base AS multi-layer
    WORKDIR /dataset/url_svmlight
    COPY --from=expand url_svmlight/Day70.svm .
    COPY --from=expand url_svmlight/Day71.svm .
    ...
    COPY --from=expand url_svmlight/Day78.svm .
    COPY --from=expand url_svmlight/Day79.svm .

The multi-layer approach is more brittle because you have to explicitly list every file you want to copy, instead of using a more flexible file pattern. But this is a less common scenario, and when you’re trying to speed up transfer times for a large image, this is definitely worth experimenting with.

##### Try it now

Build the two targets of the ML dataset image, and compare image layers:
    
    
    # switch to the ML folder:
    cd ch17/exercises/ml-dataset
    # build the one-layer and multi-layer images:
    docker build -t diamol/ch17-ml-dataset:v4-one-layer `
     -f Dockerfile.v4 --target one-layer .
    docker build -t diamol/ch17-ml-dataset:v4-multi-layer `
     -f Dockerfile.v4 --target multi-layer .
    # compare image size:
    docker image ls "diamol/ch17-ml-dataset:v4*"
    # and check the layers:
    docker history diamol/ch17-ml-dataset:v4-one-layer
    docker history diamol/ch17-ml-dataset:v4-multi-layer

You’ll see how the multi-layer image is better optimized for transfer when you run this. Figure 17.13 shows that it has many small layers, which is especially beneficial when the image is pulled on a new machine (as it would be in an auto-scaling container platform). In my client’s use case, this approach reduced download times from minutes to seconds.

##### Figure 17.13 Splitting data files across many layers can make big performance gains.

This last example proves that optimization is critical but not governed by a fixed set of rules. You need to know what parameters you affect when you change how your image builds, and how that changes build time, transfer time, and security so that you can tailor your approach to your own requirements.

## 17.5 Why optimization counts

You’ve seen in this chapter that you can follow some simple best practices and make your Dockerfiles and container images a joy to work with. Those practices boil down to the following:

  * Choose the right base image; ideally, curate your own set of golden images. 
  * Use multi-stage Dockerfiles for all but the simplest apps. 
  * Don’t add any unnecessary packages or files; focus on layer size. 
  * Sort your Dockerfile instructions by change frequency to maximize the cache. 
  * Monitor your build and your push/pull times, and iterate to keep optimizing. 



Building, pushing, and pulling images becomes a core part of your organization’s workflow as you move more apps to containers. Optimizing those images can remove a lot of pain points, speed up workflows, and prevent more serious problems. Figure 17.14 shows the typical life cycle of an image and the areas where optimization counts.

##### Figure 17.14 Optimizing your Docker images has a beneficial effect across the life cycle of your projects.

## 17.6 Lab 

Now, it’s time to put your optimization skills to the test. Your goal is to optimize an image that installs the Docker command line. There are Linux and Windows examples in the lab folder for this chapter; the Dockerfiles work right now, but they produce unnecessarily large images. Your goals are as follows:

  * Optimize the filesystem so that the image is under 50 MB for Linux containers or under 330 MB for Windows containers. 
  * Make use of the image layer cache so that repeat builds of your image take less than a second. 
  * Produce an image that writes the Docker command-line interface (CLI) version correctly from `docker run -it` `<image>` `docker` `version` (the command will give you an error for the server because it’s not connected to a Docker Engine, but the CLI version should print correctly). 



You shouldn’t need any hints, but you’ll need to think creatively when you look at the original Dockerfiles. You might not get there by optimizing the existing instructions; it might be better to work backward from the goals for the image.

My optimized files are in the same lab folder—you can also check them on GitHub: <https://mng.bz/dWWD>. You have the knowledge—now go optimize!


# 18 Application configuration management in containers

Applications need to load their configuration from the environment they’re running in, which is usually a combination of environment variables and files read from disk. Docker creates that environment for apps running in containers, and it can set environment variables and construct a filesystem from many different sources. The pieces are all there to help you build a flexible configuration approach for your apps, so when you deploy to production, you’re using the same image that passed all the test phases but you inject the settings you need for the live environment. You just need to do some work to bring the pieces together, setting up your app to merge configuration values from multiple places. 

This chapter will take you through the recommended approach (and some alternatives) using examples in .NET, Java, Go, and Node.js. Some of the work here lives in the developer space, bringing in libraries to provide configuration management, and the rest lives in that gray area between dev and ops that relies on communication so that both sides know how the configuration model works. 

## 18.1 A multitiered approach to app configuration

Your configuration model should reflect the structure of the data you’re storing, which is typically one of three types: 

  * Release-level settings, which are the same for every environment for a given release 
  * Environment-level settings, which are different for every environment (test, production, etc.) 
  * Feature-level settings, which can be used to change behavior between releases 



Some of those are fairly static, some are dynamic with a known set of variables, and others are dynamic with an unknown set of variables. Figure 18.1 shows some sample configuration settings and where they can be read from the environment.

##### Figure 18.1 A configuration hierarchy with settings from the image, filesystem, and environment variables

The first example we’ll use is Node.js with a popular configuration management library called node-config. The library lets you read configuration settings from multiple file locations in a hierarchy and override them all with environment variables. The access-log sample app in the exercises for this chapter uses the node-config library and sets up two directories to read configuration files from:

  * `config`—This will be packaged with default settings in the Docker image. 
  * `config-override`—This doesn’t exist in the image but can be provisioned in the container filesystem from a volume, configuration object, or secret. 



##### Try it now

Run the sample app with the default configuration from the image, and then the same image with an override file for the development environment:
    
    
    # switch to the exercise folder:
    cd ch18/exercises/access-log
    # run a container with the default config in the image:
    docker run -d -p 8080:80 diamol/ch18-access-log:2e
    # run a container loading a local config file override:
    docker run -d -p 8081:80 `
     -v "$(pwd)/config/dev:/app/config-override" `
     diamol/ch18-access-log:2e
    # check the config APIs in each container:
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8080/config
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8081/config

The first container uses only the default configuration file that is packaged in the image, which specifies the name of the release cycle (19.12) and sets the Prometheus metrics to be enabled. There’s an `UNKNOWN` setting for the environment name—if you ever see that, you know the environment-level configuration settings haven’t been correctly applied. The second container loads a local configuration directory as a volume in the expected location for the app to find overrides—it sets the environment name and flips the metrics feature to off. You’ll see when you call the configuration API that containers from the same image have applied different settings—mine is shown in figure 18.2.

##### Figure 18.2 Merging configuration files using volumes, configuration objects, or secrets is straightforward.

Loading configuration overrides from a known path in your app code lets you provide them from any source into the container. I’m using a local bind mount, but the source could be a configuration object or a secret stored in a container platform (Kubernetes has ConfigMap and Secret resources as we saw in chapter 15), and the behavior would be the same. There’s one nuance to this pattern—your configuration target can either be a specific file path or a directory. A directory target is more flexible (Windows containers don’t support loading volumes from a single file), but the source file names need to match the configuration file names the app expects. In this example, the bind source is the directory `config/dev`, which has a single file—the container sees `/app/config-override/local.json`, which is where it looks for overrides.

The node-config package can also load settings from environment variables, and they override any settings loaded from the file hierarchy. This is the configuration approach recommended in the 12-factor app (<https://12factor.net>)—a venerable approach to application architecture where environment variables always take precedence over other configuration sources. It’s a useful principle that helps you get into the mindset that containers are ephemeral because changing environment variables to set application configuration means replacing containers. Node-config has a slightly unusual implementation: if you want to specify individual settings as environment variables, you need to have a JSON file that maps environment variable names to settings (the access-log app has this built into the image).

##### Try it now

Run a third version of the access log container in development mode but with metrics enabled. Use the volume mount to load the dev configuration and an environment variable to override the metrics setting:
    
    
    # run a container with an override file and an environment variable:
    docker container run -d -p 8082:80 `
     -v "$(pwd)/config/dev:/app/config-override" `
     -e APP_METRICS_ENABLED=true `
     diamol/ch18-access-log:2e
    # check the config:
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8082/config

The third container merges configuration from the default file in the image, the local configuration override file in the volume, and the specific environment variable setting. This is a good example of building configuration to keep the developer workflow running smoothly. Developers can run the default settings without metrics enabled (saves CPU cycles and memory), but when they need to turn metrics on for some debugging, they can do it with the same image and an environment variable switch. Figure 18.3 shows my output.

##### Figure 18.3 Merging configuration from environment variables makes it easy to change specific features.

This is the core pattern for configuration that you should look to apply in all your apps. From this example, you can see that the pattern is quite clear, but the details are significant, and that’s the gray area where knowledge can break down between delivery and deployment. The access-log app lets you override the default configuration file with a new one, but that target file has to be in a specific location. You can also override all file settings with environment variables, but only environment variables with the correct names get picked up. Ultimately, that will be documented in the application model you use for deployment, but you need to be aware that the pattern has the potential for mistakes. An alternative approach removes that risk at the cost of making configuration management less flexible.

## 18.2 Packaging configuration for every environment 

Many application frameworks support a configuration management system where you bundle all the configuration files for every environment in your deployment, and you set a single value at runtime to specify the name of the environment you’re running in. The app platform loads the configuration file with the matching environment name, and your app is fully configured. .NET does this with its default configuration provider setup, where configuration settings are merged from these sources:

  * `appsettings.json`—The default values for all environments 
  * `appsettings.{Environment}.json`—The overrides for the named environment 
  * _Environment variables_ —Used to specify the environment name, and for setting overrides 



There’s a new version of the to-do list app for this chapter that uses this approach of packaging all the configuration files in the Docker image. You use a specific environment variable to provide the current environment name, and that gets loaded in before the rest of the configuration files.

##### Try it now

Run the to-do list app with the default configuration, which is set with the environment name Development, and then with the test environment settings:
    
    
    # run the to-do list app with default config:
    docker run -d -p 8083:8080 diamol/ch18-todo-list:2e
    # run the app with the config for the test environment:
    docker run -d -p 8084:8080 `
     -e DOTNET_ENVIRONMENT=Test diamol/ch18-todo-list:2e

The two containers are running from the same image but loading different configuration files. Inside the image are environment files for development, test, and production environments. The first container merges the core `appsettings.json` with `appsettings.Development.json`—it runs in development mode because Development is set as the default environment in the Dockerfile. The second container merges `appsettings.json` with `appsettings.Test.json`. Both environment configuration files are already present in the Docker image, so there’s no need to mount an external source for the new configuration. Browse to `http://localhost:8083/diagnostics` to see the dev configuration and `http://localhost:8084/diagnostics` to see the test version. My output is shown in figure 18.4.

This approach can work nicely if you have separate systems to manage your configuration files and your source code. The continuous integration (CI)/continuous deployment (CD) pipeline can bring the configuration files into the Docker image as part of the build, so you keep configuration management separate from development. The downside is that you still can’t package every setting because you need to keep confidential information out of the Docker image. You need to have a security-in-depth approach and assume that your registry could be compromised—you don’t want someone to find all your passwords and API keys in nice plain-text files in your images.

If you like this approach, you still need to allow for override files and for final overrides with environment variables. The to-do list app does that by loading files from a folder called `config-overrides` if it exists and using the standard .NET approach of loading environment variables last. That lets you do useful things such as run the production environment locally if you’re trying to replicate a problem, but override the environment settings to use a database file instead of a remote database server.

##### Figure 18.4 Packaging every environment’s configuration file in the image makes it easy to switch environments.

##### Try it now

The to-do list app supports configuration overrides even though all the environment configuration is bundled in the app. If you run in production mode, the app fails because it’s expecting to find a database server, but you can run in production with an override file to use a database file instead.
    
    
    cd ch18/exercises/todo-list
    docker run -d -p 8085:80 `
     -e DOTNET_ENVIRONMENT=Production `
     -v "$(pwd)/config/prod-local:/app/config-override" `
     diamol/ch18-todo-list:2e

You can browse to `http://localhost:8085/diagnostics` and see that the app is running in production mode, but the configuration file override changes the database setting so that the app still works without running a Postgres container. My output is shown in figure 18.5.

##### Figure 18.5 Selecting an environment to run in should still support configuration overrides from additional files.

This container merges the default `appsettings.json` file with the `appsettings .Production.json` environment file in the image, and the `local.json` override file in the `prod-local` folder. The setup is similar to the Node.js example, so there’s some consistency around folder and file names, but .NET takes a different approach to setting overrides with environment variables. In node-config, you use a JSON file to map environment variables to configuration settings, so you can use any environment variable names you like. You specify configuration as environment variables in .NET by using the property names from the JSON file as the environment variable names.

##### Try it now

Run the same local version of production but with a custom release name by overriding that setting with an environment variable:
    
    
    # run a container with a bind mount and a custom environment variable:
    docker run -d -p 8086:8080 `
     -e DOTNET_ENVIRONMENT=Production -e release=CUSTOM `
     -v "$(pwd)/config/prod-local:/app/config-override" `
     diamol/ch18-todo-list:2e

Browse to `http://localhost:8086/diagnostics`, and you’ll see the custom release name from the environment variable. My output is shown in figure 18.6.

##### Figure 18.6 The configuration hierarchy overrides values from any of the configuration files with environment variables.

Although this way of packaging multiple configuration files is a common approach across lots of app platforms, I must say I don’t like it. There’s a danger that you’ll include some configuration setting in your image that you don’t think is sensitive, but your security team might disagree. Server names, URLs, file paths, logging levels, and even cache sizes could all be useful information to anyone trying to hack your system. By the time you move all the confidential settings to override files that you apply from the container platform, there’s probably very little left in those packaged environment files anyway. I also don’t like the split, where some settings are managed in source control and others are in a configuration management system. 

The beauty of containers is that you can follow whichever pattern you like, so don’t let me decide for you. Some approaches work better, depending on your organization and technology stack. Things get more complicated too if you have multiple stacks to deal with—you’ll see that in the next example using a Go application.

## 18.3 Loading configuration from the runtime 

Go has a popular configuration module called Viper that offers much of the same functionality as the .NET libraries or node-config. You add the module to your package list, and in your application code, you specify the paths to the configuration directories and whether you want environment variables brought in to override the configuration files. I’ve added it to the image gallery app for this chapter, using a similar hierarchy to the other examples:

  * Files are loaded from the `config` directory first, which is populated in the Docker image. 
  * Environment-specific files are loaded from the `config-override` directory, which is empty in the image and can be the target for a container filesystem mount. 
  * Environment variables override the file settings. 



Viper supports a wider set of languages for configuration files than the other examples. You can use JSON or YAML, but a well-supported format in the Go world is TOML (named after its creator, Tom Preston-Werner). TOML is helpful for configuration files because it maps easily to dictionaries in code, and it’s easier to read than JSON or YAML. Listing 18.1 shows the TOML configuration for the image gallery app.

##### Listing 18.1 TOML allows for easily managed configuration files
    
    
    release = "19.12"
    environment = "UNKNOWN"
    
    [metrics]
    enabled = true
    
    [apis]
    
     [apis.image]
     url = "http://iotd/image"
    
     [apis.access]
     url = "http://accesslog/access-log"

You see TOML being used in lots of cloud-native projects because it’s so much easier than the alternatives. If you have a choice of formats, TOML is worth considering because “easy to read” also means easy to debug and easy to see the differences between versions in a merge tool. Other than the file format, this example works in the same way as the Node.js app, with a default `config.toml` file packaged into the Docker image.

##### Try it now

Run the Go app without any additional configuration setup to check the defaults:
    
    
    # run the container:
    docker run -d -p 8087:80 diamol/ch18-image-gallery:2e
    # check the config API:
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8087/config

When you run this exercise, you’ll see the current app configuration, which all comes from the default TOML file. My output in figure 18.7 (the response is piped through the jq tool to make it easier to read) shows the release cycle and the default URLs for the APIs that this app consumes.

##### Figure 18.7 You can package your app with default settings that work but don’t comprise a complete environment.

The output is from a configuration API that returns JSON for the current configuration settings. A configuration API is a very useful feature in your app when you have multiple layers of configuration sources; it makes debugging configuration problems much easier, but you need to secure that data. There’s no point in using secrets for confidential settings if they can be publicly read by anyone who tries browsing to `/config`. So, if you’re going to add a configuration API, you need to do three things: 

  * Don’t just publish the whole configuration; be selective, and never include secrets. 
  * Secure the endpoint so that only authorized users can access it. 
  * Make the configuration API a feature that can be enabled through configuration. 



The image gallery Go component takes a slightly different approach from the hierarchical configuration model—default settings are saved in the image, but not for any specific environment. The expectation is that every environment will specify its own additional configuration file, which extends or overrides settings in the default file to set up the full environment.

##### Try it now

Run the same app again with an override file to build a complete environment:
    
    
    # switch to the app directory:
    cd ch18/exercises/image-gallery
    # run the container with a bind mount to the config directory:
    docker container run -d -p 8088:80 `
     -v "$(pwd)/config/dev:/app/config-override" `
     diamol/ch18-image-gallery:2e
    # check config again:
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8088/config

My output in figure 18.8 shows the app now fully configured for the development environment, merging the release-level configuration file in the image with the environment override file from the container platform (my local Docker machine).

##### Figure 18.8 The Go Viper module merges configuration files in the same way as the node-config package.

Showing you all of these slight variations on the configuration theme isn’t just a cheap way to fill out the chapter. When organizations adopt Docker, they tend to find that usage accelerates quickly, and they soon have a lot of apps running in containers that each have their own opinions on configuration. Lots of small variations like this are bound to happen because the app platforms differ in the features they provide and the conventions they expect. You can apply standards at a high level—images must come packaged with default configuration and must support file and environment variable overrides—but the details of the configuration file and environment variable formats will be hard to standardize.

We’ll see that in this last example with the Go application. The Viper module supports environment variables to override settings in configuration files, but with a convention that is different again from node-config and from .NET.

##### Try it now

Run the container with an environment variable override. The configuration model in this app only uses environment variables prefixed with `IG`:
    
    
    # make sure you're in the app directory:
    cd ch18/exercises/image-gallery
    # run the container with config override and an environment variable:
    docker run -d -p 8089:80 `
     -v "$(pwd)/config/dev:/app/config-override" `
     -e IG_METRICS.ENABLED=TRUE `
     diamol/ch18-image-gallery:2e
    # check the config:
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8089/config

The Viper convention is to prefix environment variable names so that they don’t clash with other environment variables. In this app, the prefix is `IG`, followed by an underscore, followed by the configuration setting name in dot notation (so `IG_ METRICS.ENABLED` matches the `enabled` value in the `metrics` group in the TOML file). You can see from my output in figure 18.9 that this setup adds the development environment on top of the default settings but then overrides the metrics settings to enable Prometheus metrics.

##### Figure 18.9 All the sample apps support environment variables for configuration, but with small variations.

We’ve walked through configuration modeling with three different apps, and we have three slightly different approaches. The differences are manageable and easy to document in the application manifest files, and they don’t actually affect how you build the image or run the container. We’ll look at one last example in this chapter that takes the same configuration model and applies it to an application that doesn’t have a nice new configuration library, so it needs some extra work to make it behave like a modern app.

## 18.4 Configuring legacy apps in the same way as new apps

Legacy apps have their own ideas about configuration, which don’t usually involve environment variables or file merges. .NET Framework apps on Windows are a good example—they expect XML configuration files in specific locations. They don’t like looking for files outside the application root folder, and they don’t look at environment variables at all. You can still take the same configuration approach when you replatform those apps, but you need to do some extra work in your Dockerfile.

The approach here is to package a utility app or set of scripts that transform the configuration settings in the container environment into the configuration model the application expects. The exact implementation will depend on your app framework and how it uses configuration files, but the logic will be something like this:

  1. Read in the configuration override settings from a specified source file in the container. 
  2. Read in the overrides from environment variables. 
  3. Merge the two sets of overrides so that environment variables take precedence. 
  4. Write the merged overrides to the specified target file in the container. 



In the exercises for this chapter, there’s an updated version of the image-of-the-day Java API that uses this approach. It’s not actually a legacy app, but I’ve built the image with the legacy pattern as though the app can’t use the normal container configuration options. There’s a utility app that runs at startup and sets up the configuration, so although the internal configuration mechanism is different, users can configure containers in the same way as the other examples.

##### Try it now

Run the legacy-style app with default configuration settings and with a file override:
    
    
    # switch to the app directory:
    cd ch18/exercises/image-of-the-day
    # run a container with default configuration:
    docker container run -d -p 8090:80 diamol/ch18-image-of-the-day:2e
    # run with a config override file in a bind mount:
    docker container run -d -p 8091:80 `
     -v "$(pwd)/config/dev:/config-override" `
     -e CONFIG_SOURCE_PATH="/config-override/application.properties" `
     diamol/ch18-image-of-the-day:2e
    # check the config settings:
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8090/config
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8091/config

The user experience is very similar to the other apps—mounting a volume with the environment override file (and the source could be a configuration object or secret)—but you have to additionally specify the override file location in an environment variable so that the startup utility knows where to look. You’ll see when you run the exercise that the default configuration in the image specifies the release cycle but not the environment—that gets merged in with the override file in the second container. My output is shown in figure 18.10.

##### Figure 18.10 This app has a utility to bootstrap the configuration model, but the user experience is the same.

The magic happens here in a simple Java utility program, which gets compiled and packaged in the same multi-stage build as the rest of the app. Listing 18.2 shows the key parts of the Dockerfile that build the utility and set it to run at startup.

##### Listing 18.2 Building a configuration load utility in the Dockerfile
    
    
    FROM diamol/maven:2e AS builder
    # ...
    RUN mvn package
    
    # config util
    FROM diamol/maven:2e as utility-builder
    WORKDIR /usr/src/utilities
    COPY ./src/utilities/ConfigLoader.java .
    RUN javac ConfigLoader.java
    
    # app
    FROM diamol/openjdk:2e
    ENV CONFIG_SOURCE_PATH="" \
        CONFIG_TARGET_PATH="/app/config/application.properties"
    
    CMD java ConfigLoader && \
        java -jar /app/iotd-service-0.1.0.jar
    
    WORKDIR /app
    COPY --from=utility-builder /usr/src/utilities/ConfigLoader.class .
    COPY --from=builder /usr/src/iotd/target/iotd-service-0.1.0.jar .

The important takeaway here is that you can extend your Docker image to make old apps behave in the same way as new apps. You control the startup logic, so you can execute any setup steps you need before running the actual application. When you do this, you’re increasing the amount of time between the container starting and the app being ready, and you’re also increasing the risk the container might fail (if the startup logic has an error). You should always have health checks in your image or your application model to mitigate that.

My configuration loader utility app supports the 12-factor approach that has environment variables override other settings. It merges environment variables with the override configuration file and writes the output as a configuration file in a location the app expects. The utility takes the same approach as Viper, looking for environment variables with a specific prefix that helps keep app settings separate from other settings in the container.

##### Try it now

The legacy app doesn’t use environment variables, but the configuration utility sets them up so that the user experience is the same as a modern app:
    
    
    # run a container with an override file and an environment variable:
    docker run -d -p 8092:80 `
     -v "$(pwd)/config/dev:/config-override" `
     -e CONFIG_SOURCE_PATH="/config-override/application.properties" `
     -e IOTD_ENVIRONMENT="custom" `
     diamol/ch18-image-of-the-day:2e
    # check the config settings:
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8092/config

The utility lets me work with my old app in the same way as my other apps. It’s mostly transparent to the user—they just set environment variables and load override files into volumes. It’s transparent to the app, which just reads the configuration files it expects to see—there are no changes to the original app code here. Figure 18.11 shows that this legacy-style app uses the modern multitiered configuration approach.

##### Figure 18.11 Environment variables make the configuration model for this old app behave like new apps.

Now, every component in the image gallery app uses the same configuration pattern. Although a level of standardization exists across all components, there are also small implementation differences. Every component can be configured with a file override to run in development mode, and every component can be configured with an environment variable to enable Prometheus metrics. How you actually do that differs for each app, which is that gray area I mentioned right at the beginning—it’s difficult to enforce a standard to say every component will run a Prometheus endpoint if the environment variable `ENABLE_METRICS=true` because app platforms work in different ways.

Documentation is how you remove that confusion, and in the Docker world, deployment documentation is best done in the application model. There’s a Docker Compose file in the exercises for this chapter that does exactly what I’ve laid out in the previous paragraph—setting every component to development mode and enabling Prometheus metrics. Listing 18.3 shows the configuration parts of the Compose file.

##### Listing 18.3 Documenting configuration settings in Docker Compose
    
    
    services:
      accesslog:
        image: diamol/ch18-access-log:2e
        environment:
          APP_METRICS_ENABLED: "true"
        secrets:
          - source: access-log-config
            target: /app/config-override/local.json
    
      iotd:
        image: diamol/ch18-image-of-the-day:2e
        environment:
          CONFIG_SOURCE_PATH: "/config-override/application.properties"
          IOTD_MANAGEMENT_ENDPOINTS_WEB_EXPOSURE_INCLUDE: "health,prometheus"
        secrets:
          - source: iotd-config
            target: /config-override/application.properties
    
      image-gallery:
        image: diamol/ch18-image-gallery:2e
        environment:
          IG_METRICS.ENABLED: "TRUE"
        secrets:
          - source: image-gallery-config
            target: /app/config-override/config.toml
    
    secrets:
      access-log-config:
        file: access-log/config/dev/local.json
      iotd-config:
        file: image-of-the-day/config/dev/application.properties
      image-gallery-config:
        file: image-gallery/config/dev/config.toml

That’s a lengthy code listing, but I wanted to keep it all in one place so you can see how the patterns are the same, although the details are different. The Node.js app sets an environment variable to enable metrics and loads a JSON file as a configuration override. The Java application uses an environment variable that lists the management endpoints to include; adding Prometheus in there enables metrics collection. Then, the app loads a configuration override from a properties file, which is a series of key/value pairs. The Go application uses an environment variable to enable metrics and loads the configuration override as a TOML file. I’m using the secret support in Docker Compose for the file sources, but the pattern is the same for ConfigMaps or Secrets in Kubernetes.

The user experience here is both good and bad. It’s good because you can easily load different environments by changing the source paths for the configuration overrides, and you can change individual settings with environment variables. It’s bad because you need to know the quirks of the application. The project team will likely evolve various Docker Compose overrides to cover different configurations, so editing configuration settings won’t be a common activity. Running the app will be far more common, and that’s as easy as starting any app with Compose.

##### Try it now

Let’s run the app as a whole with a fixed set of configurations for all the components. Start by clearing down your containers, and then run the app with Docker Compose:
    
    
    # remove all containers:
    docker rm -f $(docker container ls -aq)
    # switch to the exercise folder:
    cd ch18/exercises
    # run the app with the config settings:
    docker compose up -d
    # check all the config APIs:
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8030/config
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8020/config
    curl http:/_._ /localhost:8010/config

You can browse to `http://localhost:8010` and use the app in the normal way, and then browse to the Prometheus endpoints to see the component metrics (on `http://localhost:8010/metrics`, `http://localhost:8030/metrics`, and `http:// localhost:8020/actuator/prometheus`). But actually, all the confirmation that the app is configured correctly comes from those configuration APIs. 

You can see my output in figure 18.12. Every component loads the release cycle name from the default configuration file in the image, the environment name from the configuration override file, and the metrics setting from the environment variable.

That’s all we really need to cover regarding the patterns for building your applications to fetch configuration from the container environment. We’ll wrap up this chapter with some thoughts on where that multitiered configuration model can take you.

##### Figure 18.12 Docker Compose can document app configuration settings and start the app with that configuration.

## 18.5 Why a flexible configuration model pays off

As discussed in chapters 11 and 16, the core design of the CI/CD pipeline with Docker is that you build one image, and your deployment process is about promoting that image through your environments up to production. Your apps will need to work slightly differently in each environment, and the way to support that while keeping the single-image approach is to use a multitiered configuration model.

In practice, you’ll use the release-level settings built into the container image, with the environment-level override file provided by the container platform in almost all cases, but the ability to set feature-level configuration with environment variables is a useful addition. It means you can react quickly to production problems—tuning down the level of logging if that’s a performance problem, or turning off a feature that has a security hole. It also means you can create a production-like environment on a development machine to replicate a bug by using the production configuration override with secrets removed and using environment variables instead.

That ability to run the exact same image in any environment is the payback for investing time in your configuration model. Figure 18.13 shows the life cycle of an image from the CI/CD pipeline onward.

##### Figure 18.13 The CI/CD pipeline produces one image, and you use the configuration model to change the behavior.

The work you do in producing this flexible configuration model will go a long way toward future-proofing your app. All container platforms support setting environment variables and loading files into the container from configuration objects or secrets. The Docker images for this chapter’s image gallery app will work in the same way with Docker Compose, Azure Container Instances, or Kubernetes. And it’s not just container platforms—standard configuration files and environment variables are the models used in platform as a service (PAAS) products and serverless functions too.

## 18.6 Lab

It can be tricky to dig into the configuration model for a new app and work out how to set override files and configure feature overrides, so you’re going to get some practice in this lab. You’ll be using the same image gallery app—in the lab folder for this chapter, there’s a Docker Compose file with the app components specified but with no configuration. Your job is to set up every component to do the following:

  * Use volumes to load configuration override files. 
  * Load the configuration overrides for the test environment. 
  * Override the release cycle to be 25.03 instead of 19.12. 



This should be fairly straightforward, but it will be useful to spend some time tweaking app configuration without making any changes to the apps. When you run the app with `docker` `compose` `up`, you should be able to browse to `http://localhost:8010` and the app should work. You should also be able to browse to all three configuration APIs and see that the release name is 25.01 and the environment is TEST.

My solution is in the same folder in the `docker-compose-solution.yml` file. You can also check it on GitHub here: <https://mng.bz/rZZg>.


# 19 Writing and managing application logs with Docker 

Logging is usually the most boring part of learning a new technology, but not so with Docker. The basic principle is simple: you need to make sure your application logs are being written to the standard output streams because that’s where Docker looks for them. There are a couple of ways to achieve that, which we’ll cover in this chapter, and then the fun begins. Docker has a pluggable logging framework—you need to make sure your application logs are coming out from the container, and then Docker can send them to different places. That lets you build a powerful logging model, where the application logs from all of your containers are sent to a central log store with a searchable UI on top of it—all using open source components, all running in containers. 

## 19.1 Welcome to stderr and stdout! 

A Docker image is the snapshot of a filesystem with all of your application binaries and dependencies, as well as some metadata telling Docker which process to start when you run a container from the image. That process runs in the foreground, so it’s like starting a shell session and then running a command. As long as the command is active, it has control of the terminal input and output. Commands write log entries to the standard output and standard error streams (called _stdout_ and _stderr_), and in a terminal session, that means you see the output in your window. In a container, the Docker Engine watches stdout and stderr and collects the output from the streams—that’s the source of the container logs.

##### Try it now

You can see this easily if you run a simple app that prints the time in a loop. The application itself runs in the foreground and writes log entries to stdout:
    
    
    # run the container in the foreground: 
    docker run -it diamol/ch19-timecheck:2e
    # exit the container with Ctrl-C/Cmd-C when you're done

You’ll see some log lines in your terminal, but you can’t enter any more commands. This is because the container is running in the foreground, so it’s just like running the app itself in your terminal. Every few seconds, the app writes another timestamp to stdout, so you’ll see another line in your session window. My output is shown in figure 19.1.

##### Figure 19.1 A container in the foreground takes over the terminal session until it exits.

This is the standard operating model for containers—Docker starts a process inside the container and collects the output streams from that process as logs. Almost all the apps we’ve used in this book follow this same pattern: the application process runs in the foreground—that could be a Go binary or the Java runtime—and the application itself is configured to write logs to stdout (or stderr; Docker treats both streams in much the same way). Those logs are written to the output stream by the application runtime, and Docker collects them. Figure 19.2 shows the interaction between the application, the output streams, and Docker.

##### Figure 19.2 Docker watches the application process in the container and collects its output streams.

Container logs are stored as JSON files by the Docker Engine, so the log entries remain available for both the detached containers that don’t have a terminal session and for the containers that have exited, so there’s no application process. Docker manages the JSON files for you, and they have the same life cycle as the container—when the container is removed, the log files are removed too.

##### Try it now

Run a container from the same image in the background as a detached container, and check the logs and then the path to the log file:
    
    
    # run a detached container:
    docker run -d --name timecheck diamol/ch19-timecheck:2e
    # check the most recent log entry:
    docker logs --tail 1 timecheck
    # stop the container and check the logs again:
    docker stop timecheck
    docker logs --tail 1 timecheck
    # check where Docker stores the container log file:
    docker container inspect --format='{{.LogPath}}' timecheck

If you’re using Docker Desktop with Linux containers, remember that the Docker Engine is running inside a virtual machine (VM) that Docker manages for you. You can see the path to the log file for the container, but you don’t have access to the VM, so you can’t read the file directly. If you’re running Docker Engine on Linux or you’re using Windows Server containers, the path to the log file will be on your local machine, and you can open the file to see the raw contents. You can see my output (using a Docker Engine running in Windows Subsystem for Linux) in figure 19.3.

##### Figure 19.3 Docker stores container logs in a JSON file and manages the lifetime of that file.

Docker keeps the log file as long as the container exists—even if it’s stopped—but it deletes the file when the container is removed. The actual file is really an implementation detail that you don’t usually need to worry about. The format is very simple; it contains a JSON object for each log entry with the string containing the log, the name of the stream where the log came from (stdout or stderr), and a timestamp. Listing 19.1 shows a sample of the logs for my `timecheck` container.

##### Listing 19.1 The raw format for logs: A simple JSON object
    
    
    {
     "log":"Environment: DEV; version: 1.0; time check: 15:25.40",
     "stream":"stdout",
     "time":"2025-03-07T15:25:40.371840485Z"
    }
    
    {
     "log":"Environment: DEV; version: 1.0; time check: 15:25.45",
     "stream":"stdout",
     "time":"2025-03-07T15:25:45.372227195Z"
    }

The only time you’ll need to think about the JSON file is if you have a container that produces lots of logs, and you want to keep all the log entries for a period but have them in a manageable file structure. Docker creates a single JSON log file for each container by default and will let it grow to any size (until it fills up your disk). You can configure Docker to use rolling files instead with a maximum size limit, so that when the log file fills up, Docker starts writing to a new file. You also configure how many log files to use, and when they’re all full, Docker starts overwriting the first file. You can set those options at the Docker Engine level so that the changes apply to every container, or you can set them for individual containers. Configuring logging options for a specific container is a good way to get small, rotated log files for one application but keep all the logs for other containers.

##### Try it now

Run the same app again, but this time specifying log options to use three rolling log files with a maximum of 5 KB each:
    
    
    # run with log options and an app setting to write lots of logs:
    docker run -d --name timecheck2 `
     --log-opt max-size=5k --log-opt max-file=3 `
     -e Timer__IntervalSeconds=1 `
     diamol/ch19-timecheck:2e
    # verify the log setup in the container:
    docker container inspect `
     --format='{{json .HostConfig.LogConfig}}' `
     timecheck2
    # wait for a few minutes
    # check the logs:
    docker container inspect --format='{{.LogPath}}' timecheck2

You’ll see that the log path for the container is still just a single JSON file, but Docker is actually rotating log files using that name as the base but with a suffix for the archived log file numbers. If you’re running Windows Server Containers or Docker Engine on Linux, you can list the contents of the directory where the logs are kept, and you’ll see those file suffixes. Mine are shown in figure 19.4.

##### Figure 19.4 Rolling log files let you keep a known amount of log data per container.

There’s a collection and processing stage for the application logs coming from stdout, which is where you can configure what Docker does with the logs. In the previous exercise, we configured the log processing to control the JSON file structure, and there’s much more you can do with container logs. To take full advantage of that, you need to make sure every app is pushing logs out of the container, and in some cases, that takes a bit more work.

## 19.2 Relaying logs from other sinks to stdout

Not every app fits nicely with the standard logging model; when you containerize some apps, Docker won’t see any logs in the output streams. Some applications run in the background as Windows Services or Linux daemons, so the container startup process isn’t actually the application process. Other apps might use an existing logging framework that writes to log files or other locations (called _sinks_ in the logging world), such as syslog in Linux or the Windows Event Log. Either way, there are no application logs coming from the container start process, so Docker doesn’t see any logs.

##### Try it now

There’s a new version of the timecheck app that writes logs to a file instead of stdout. When you run this version, there are no container logs, although the app logs are being stored in the container filesystem:
    
    
    # run a container from the new image:
    docker run -d --name timecheck3 diamol/ch19-timecheck:2e-v2
    # check - there are no logs coming from stdout:
    docker container logs timecheck3
    # now run a command in the container to print the log file:
    docker container exec -it timecheck3 cat /logs/timecheck.log

You’ll see that there are no container logs, even though the application itself is writing lots of log entries. My output is shown in figure 19.5—I need to connect to the container and read the log file from the container filesystem to see the log entries.

##### Figure 19.5 If the app doesn’t write anything to the output streams, you won’t see any container logs.

This happens because the app is using its own log sink—a file in this exercise—and Docker doesn’t know anything about that sink. Docker will only read logs from stdout; there’s no way to configure it to read from a different log sink inside the container.
